- Define roles and responsibilities in the runbook:
  - **SOC/Insider risk analyst**: creates case and tags evidence
  - **HR**: provides/validates HR-related context and employment status where applicable
  - **Legal**: reviews/approves the risk basis and any constraints
  - **System administrator / IAM engineer**: ensures technical disablement is executed
  - **Authorizing official**: final approval (or emergency override procedure, if your organization uses one)
- Require that the case includes references to HR/legal coordination artifacts.

### 6) Monitoring and exception handling
- Add detection that flags any case where disablement completion is missing or late.
- For exceptions (e.g., accounts not found, system outage), require documented justification and authorizing official approval, and still track the SLA breach.

## WHO
- **IAM Engineer / Security Automation Engineer**: implements workflow, integrations, and automation.
- **SOC/Insider Risk Analyst**: initiates cases with discovery timestamp and evidence tagging.
- **HR Manager** and **Legal Counsel**: provide/validate supporting context and coordinate decision basis.
- **Authorizing Official**: approves disablement decision.
- **System Administrator**: ensures downstream account disablement completes.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. For test cases with a recorded **discovery timestamp**, the workflow completes disablement of the identified accounts in the connected systems **within 24 hours** (measured as completion timestamp minus discovery timestamp).
2. Each completed case contains:
   - Evidence fields demonstrating the defined “significant risks” criteria were met.
   - Named authorizing official approval and timestamp.
   - Attached/referenced audit evidence from IdP/AD/PAM (or equivalent systems) showing the account state changed to disabled/blocked.
3. If disablement is not completed by **T0 + 18 hours**, the workflow triggers escalation to the authorizing official/system admin.
4. The workflow prevents execution without required evidence fields and approval (no “manual bypass” without documented override).
5. Quarterly review report can be generated showing all cases, timeliness compliance, and exceptions with justification.

### ac-3 — Access Enforcement (Control)

#### Enforce role-based authorization at app/service endpoints

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement technical access enforcement so that logical access to application functions, APIs, UI actions, and data objects is granted only when an authenticated subject’s authorization is approved by the organization’s access control policy.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-3 Access Enforcement** requires the organization to **enforce approved authorizations for logical access** to information and system resources **in accordance with applicable access control policies**. The guidance emphasizes that enforcement can be applied at the **application and service level** to increase security and privacy.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define enforcement points and map them to policy roles/permissions**
   - Inventory all application entry points: web routes, mobile endpoints, API operations, background jobs, message consumers, admin functions, and data access paths.
   - Create a mapping from each endpoint/action to the required **role(s)/permission(s)** per the approved access control policy.
   - Ensure separation between standard user and administrative functions.

2. **Implement centralized authorization checks in the application/service layer**
   - Use a single authorization mechanism (e.g., middleware/guard layer) so every request is evaluated consistently.
   - For each request, enforce:
     - **Authentication** (identity established via enterprise IdP)
     - **Authorization** (subject roles/claims → permission checks)
   - Deny by default: if no explicit authorization mapping exists, return **403 Forbidden** (or equivalent) and do not execute the underlying business logic.

3. **Integrate authorization decisions with enterprise identity/entitlements**
   - Source roles/permissions from approved identity/entitlement systems (e.g., IdP groups/roles, IAM roles, or an entitlement service).
   - Ensure service accounts and privileged operators use distinct identities and are authorized via the same policy-driven mechanism.

4. **Enforce data-level access controls (not only UI-level)**
   - Apply authorization to data queries and object access (e.g., row-level filtering, object ownership checks, tenant scoping, or database role constraints).
   - Prevent “IDOR”/direct object access by validating that the subject is authorized for the specific resource identifier.

5. **Add audit logging for authorization outcomes**
   - Log authorization decisions (at minimum: subject identifier, resource/action, decision allow/deny, and correlation/request ID).
   - Ensure denied attempts are recorded for monitoring and incident investigation.

6. **Test enforcement with negative test cases and CI gates**
   - Create automated tests that verify:
     - Authorized roles can access each endpoint/action.
     - Unauthorized roles are denied.
     - Data-level access is blocked for unauthorized resource IDs.
   - Run tests in CI/CD and require them to pass before deployment.

7. **Document evidence artifacts for auditability**
   - Maintain evidence that authorization enforcement is implemented:
     - Endpoint-to-permission mapping (policy-aligned)
     - Code/config references to the authorization middleware/guard
     - Sample logs showing denied and allowed decisions
     - Test results demonstrating denied access

## WHO
- **Application Security Engineer / Backend Engineer**: implement authorization middleware/guards, data-level checks, and logging.
- **IAM/Identity Engineer**: ensure roles/claims/entitlements are correctly provisioned from the enterprise identity system.
- **Application Owner**: approve the endpoint-to-permission mapping aligned to access control policy.
- **QA/Test Engineer**: implement and maintain negative/positive authorization test suites.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
- For every externally reachable endpoint/API operation and privileged function, the application enforces authorization via a centralized mechanism (no “unguarded” routes).
- Unauthorized subjects receive **403 Forbidden** (or equivalent) and the business logic/data access does not occur.
- Data access is authorized at the object/data level (e.g., tenant/resource scoping or row/object checks), not only via UI visibility.
- Authorization allow/deny events are logged and can be correlated to requests.
- Automated tests exist and pass in CI/CD demonstrating both allowed and denied access for representative roles, including at least one negative test per major resource type.
- Evidence package includes: endpoint-to-permission mapping, implementation references, sample authorization logs, and test execution results.

### ac-3.2 — Dual Authorization (Enhancement)

#### Dual-approval workflow gate for privileged access/security changes

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement a dual-authorization (two-person) mechanism that **requires two distinct authorized individuals to approve and then execute** any in-scope privileged command/action that changes **access control settings**, **security configurations**, or performs **administrative changes to Secret/Confidential data**.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-3(2) Dual Authorization** enhancement requires enforcing dual authorization for **{{ insert: param, ac-03.02_odp }}**. Dual authorization reduces risk from insider threats by preventing a single person from executing high-risk privileged actions.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define the in-scope privileged actions (authoritative list)
- Create/maintain an **“In-scope Dual Authorization Catalogue”** that maps to **ac-03.02_odp**: *privileged commands and/or other actions that change access control settings, security configurations, or execute administrative changes to Secret/Confidential data*.
- For each catalogue item, record:
  - Action name (e.g., “change IAM role mapping”, “modify firewall rule”, “rotate database encryption key”, “grant admin on Secret store”)
  - Target system/component (app, IAM, secrets manager, DB, network control)
  - Execution method (API/script/console/IaC pipeline)
  - Required approval groups (Approver Group A and Approver Group B)

### 2) Configure identity/ITSM roles for two distinct approvers
- In the enterprise IdP/ITSM, define **two authorized approver roles/groups** (e.g., `DUAL_APPROVER_ACCESS` and `DUAL_APPROVER_SECURITY`) aligned to the catalogue.
- Enforce **distinct individuals**: the two approvals must come from **two different user identities** (no same person twice).
- Ensure initiators (requesters) are not eligible to be one of the two approvers for the same request.

### 3) Implement a workflow that blocks execution until two approvals are recorded
- Use the organization’s controlled change mechanism (recommended: ITSM change workflow + automation runner, or CI/CD pipeline with policy gates).
- Workflow states must include at minimum:
  - `Requested` → `Approved-1` → `Approved-2` → `Ready-to-Execute` → `Executed`
- Configure the automation/execution integration so that **execution is refused** unless the workflow is in `Ready-to-Execute` and contains evidence of **two distinct approvals**.

### 4) Enforce technical gating at the execution layer
- Integrate the workflow with the privileged execution mechanism (e.g., pipeline runner, automation service, admin console API) so that:
  - The execution endpoint validates the workflow approval status before running.
  - If validation fails, the action is not executed and an audit event is generated.

### 5) Record audit evidence (who/when/what) before execution
- Ensure the workflow and execution system record:
  - Request ID / change ticket ID
  - Action catalogue item ID
  - Approver identities (two distinct users)
  - Approval timestamps
  - Execution timestamp and execution result
- Store audit records in an **immutable/tamper-evident** log store and forward to SIEM for auditability.

### 6) Collusion-risk consideration (operational pairing/rotation)
- Add pairing rules to reduce collusion risk, such as:
  - Rotating which approver group is expected to approve first
  - Time-based rotation of approver assignments
  - Periodic review of approver pairings
- Document the rationale and how exceptions are handled.

### 7) Emergency/safety exception process (documented)
- Where immediate response is necessary for public/environmental safety, document an emergency exception process that still produces auditable evidence and includes compensating controls.
- Ensure exceptions are rare, time-bounded, and reviewed.

## WHO
- **Security Governance / GRC Lead**: owns the dual-authorization policy, catalogue scope, and exception criteria.
- **IAM/Privileged Access Administrator**: configures approver roles/groups and ensures distinct-identity enforcement.
- **ITSM/Automation Engineer**: implements the workflow states, execution gating, and audit evidence.
- **System Owners (App/Infra/Secrets/Network)**: nominate in-scope actions and validate catalogue accuracy.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. For every in-scope catalogue item mapped to **ac-03.02_odp**, execution cannot occur unless **two distinct authorized approvers** have approved the request **before** execution.
2. The system rejects requests where:
   - Only one approval exists
   - The same identity provides both approvals
   - The approvers are not members of the authorized approver groups
3. Audit evidence exists for each executed action showing **who approved (two identities), when, what action, and that approvals occurred before execution**.
4. Execution attempts that fail gating generate an auditable event (and are visible in SIEM/logs).
5. The in-scope catalogue is reviewed and kept current (e.g., via a defined periodic review process) and is traceable to the control scope.


### ac-3.3 — Mandatory Access Control (Enhancement)

#### Enforce Bell-LaPadula MAC with label-locked reference monitor

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Months
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement a Mandatory Access Control (MAC) enforcement mechanism that applies the organization-defined Bell-LaPadula policy (**no write up** and **no read down**) uniformly across all **covered subjects** and **covered objects**. The enforcement must prevent subjects from:
- Passing information to unauthorized subjects/objects (including via indirect channels within the system boundary)
- Propagating access/privileges to other subjects
- Changing security attributes/labels (including labels on newly created/modified objects)
- Changing access-control rules

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-3(3) Mandatory Access Control (enhancement)** requires the system to **enforce the organization-defined mandatory access control policy** over the set of covered subjects and objects, and to ensure the policy is **uniformly enforced** and implemented with a **reference monitor concept** (AC-25). The guidance emphasizes that without reference-monitor style enforcement, the access control policy can be circumvented, and that the policy is **bounded by the system**.

This solution directly implements the organization-defined parameter **ac-3.3_prm_1**: *Bell-LaPadula (confidentiality) with no write up and no read down*.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define the MAC coverage model (policy-as-code inputs)
- Identify and enumerate **covered subjects**: authorized users, service accounts, and trusted system processes (ac-03.03_odp.03).
- Identify and enumerate **covered objects**: all data stores, files, database rows/records, message payloads, and other information objects that the application can read/write/transfer (ac-03.03_odp.02).
- Ensure each covered object has a system-controlled **security level label** (Confidential, Secret) (ac-03.03_odp.02).

### 2) Implement reference-monitor style enforcement at the system boundary
- Deploy an OS/kernel-level MAC framework (or equivalent platform MAC capability) for all compute components that handle covered data (e.g., application hosts, API gateways, worker nodes, ETL/data-mover nodes).
- Configure the MAC policy so that:
  - **No read down**: deny read/open/access requests where the subject’s label is higher than the target object’s label.
  - **No write up**: deny write/modify/create operations where the subject’s label is lower than the target object’s label.
- Ensure the MAC decision point mediates access for all relevant operations (open/read/write/execute and any platform-specific equivalents) so enforcement cannot be bypassed by application logic.

### 3) Lock label management and prevent label/attribute changes
- Configure the platform so that **only the trusted policy-management component** can change labels/rules.
- Ensure normal subjects (including the application runtime identities) cannot:
  - Set or override labels on newly created/modified objects
  - Change security attributes/labels of existing objects
- Implement default label assignment for new objects strictly according to the subject’s current label and the policy (so the subject cannot choose a more permissive label).

### 4) Implement trusted exceptions explicitly (limited and auditable)
- For any required exceptions (e.g., controlled data-movers, backup, indexing, or migration), run those processes under **explicitly authorized trusted identities** (ac-03.03_odp.03) with dedicated MAC contexts.
- Grant only the minimum required privileges (ac-03.03_odp.04) and only for the explicitly defined object classes/actions.
- Ensure exceptions still cannot be used to propagate privileges or to violate the Bell-LaPadula constraints outside the defined trusted workflows.

### 5) Enforce “bounded by the system” for exports/egress
- Add system-boundary controls so that when information leaves the MAC-controlled environment (e.g., export to external systems, downloads, message handoff to non-covered components), the policy constraints remain effective via:
  - Label-aware export mechanisms (deny or re-label only through trusted components)
  - Egress allowlisting to approved destinations
  - Blocking of untrusted direct file transfer paths

### 6) Evidence generation and continuous verification
- Version and store the MAC policy (policy-as-code) with change control.
- Enable audit logging for:
  - Denied MAC decisions (read down / write up)
  - Label/rule modification attempts
  - Trusted exception usage
- Periodically run automated tests that attempt prohibited flows and verify they are denied.

## WHO
- **Security Architect / IAM & Access Control Engineer**: defines coverage model, label taxonomy, and trusted exception design.
- **Platform/Infrastructure Engineer**: implements MAC framework configuration and label locking on hosts/containers.
- **Application Owner / DevSecOps**: ensures application/service accounts map to correct subject labels/contexts and that any required trusted workflows are implemented as trusted processes.
- **Compliance/Assurance**: reviews policy changes, approves trusted exceptions, and validates audit evidence.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. **Policy enforcement**: For all covered subjects/objects, attempts that violate **no read down** and **no write up** are denied by the MAC enforcement point (not by application logic).
2. **Uniform coverage**: There are no uncovered paths where covered data can be accessed or modified without passing through the MAC mediation (documented and tested).
3. **Label immutability**: Non-trusted subjects cannot change security labels/attributes of existing objects or assign labels to newly created/modified objects.
4. **Rule immutability**: Only the trusted policy-management component can modify MAC rules; all other attempts are denied and logged.
5. **Privilege propagation prevention**: Trusted/untrusted subjects cannot use access to obtain or delegate privileges beyond what is explicitly permitted by the MAC policy.
6. **Trusted exceptions**: Any exception workflows operate only under explicitly authorized trusted identities and only for the minimum required actions; all exception usage is logged.
7. **System boundary**: Approved egress/export paths preserve the MAC constraints; unapproved export paths are blocked or denied.


### ac-3.4 — Discretionary Access Control (Enhancement)

#### Enforce same impact/classification for discretionary sharing & grants

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement the organization-defined discretionary access control policy (AC-3(4) enhancement) so that discretionary actions—specifically **passing data to other subjects/objects**, **granting privileges**, and **changing security attributes / access-control rules**—are permitted **only when the acting subject and the target (subject/object/component) are authorized at the same impact and classification level** per the access policy.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-3(4) requires enforcing the organization-defined discretionary access control policy over covered subjects/objects, including the policy-specified discretionary capabilities. The guidance emphasizes that discretionary access control allows passing, but AC-3(4) constrains that discretion so it cannot cross **impact/classification levels**.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define the enforcement model and covered surfaces
- Identify the **covered subjects** (e.g., authenticated users, service accounts, privileged operators) and **covered objects** (e.g., documents/records, database rows/collections, API resources, message topics/queues, OT-mediated data endpoints) within the application/system boundary.
- Define the **same-level rule** exactly as in the organization parameter:
  - `ac-3.4_prm_1`: “Discretionary access control policy allowing subjects to pass data, grant privileges, and change attributes only within the same impact and classification level as defined in the access policy.”

### 2) Create an authoritative level attribute model
- Ensure both identities and resources have authoritative attributes for:
  - `impactLevel`
  - `classificationLevel`
- Source these from the enterprise access policy/label service (or IdP claims + resource metadata), ensuring the application can retrieve:
  - acting subject level(s)
  - target object/subject/component level(s)

### 3) Implement same-level authorization gates at every discretionary action entry point
Add enforcement in the application and its integrated services at the moment the discretionary action is requested (before the action executes):
- **Passing data to other subjects** (e.g., share-to-user, delegation, secure message recipient selection)
  - On request: evaluate `actingSubject.level == targetSubject.level`.
  - If not equal: deny and return a policy error.
- **Passing data to other objects** (e.g., move/copy/share to another folder, dataset, topic, or external object)
  - On request: evaluate `actingSubject.level == targetObject.level`.
  - If not equal: deny.
- **Granting privileges** (e.g., “grant access”, “add collaborator”, “assign role/permission”, “delegate permissions”)
  - On request: evaluate `actingSubject.level == targetPrincipal.level`.
  - Additionally, require that the granted privileges do not exceed what the acting subject is authorized to grant within the same level (enforce via the same-level policy engine).
- **Changing security attributes** (e.g., changing labels/ACL attributes, security settings, or metadata that affects access)
  - On request: evaluate `actingSubject.level == targetObject/component.level`.
  - Deny any attribute change that would result in cross-level authorization effects.
- **Changing access-control rules** (e.g., editing ACLs/permission matrices)
  - On request: evaluate `actingSubject.level == targetObject/component.level`.
  - Deny rule changes that would create cross-level access paths.

### 4) Use a centralized policy decision point (PDP) and consistent enforcement
- Implement a policy decision integration (policy-as-code or vendor policy integration) so all discretionary actions call the same decision logic.
- Cache carefully only for short TTLs and always include the acting subject identity and target identifiers in the decision input.

### 5) Audit allow/deny decisions with level context
For each discretionary action attempt (allow or deny), emit audit events to the centralized logging/SIEM with at minimum:
- acting subject identifier
- target subject/object/component identifier
- action type (share, pass-to-object, grant, change-attributes, change-access-rules)
- acting subject `impactLevel` and `classificationLevel`
- target `impactLevel` and `classificationLevel`
- decision (allow/deny)
- reason code explicitly tied to same-level enforcement (e.g., “level mismatch”)

## WHO
- **Application Security Engineer / Backend Engineer**: implement authorization gates and PDP integration in the application services and APIs.
- **IAM/Identity Architect**: ensure authoritative level attributes are available and correctly mapped from the enterprise access policy.
- **Security Operations / SIEM Engineer**: configure audit event schemas, retention, and SIEM correlation rules.
- **System Owner**: confirm covered subjects/objects and approve the enforcement scope.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. For each discretionary action type (pass-to-subject, pass-to-object, grant privileges, change security attributes, change access-control rules), the application **denies** requests where acting and target are at different impact/classification levels.
2. For requests where acting and target are at the **same** impact and classification level, the application **allows** the action according to the discretionary policy.
3. Audit logs are generated for **every** discretionary action attempt (allow and deny) and include level attributes and a reason code indicating same-level enforcement.
4. Enforcement is applied consistently across all identified discretionary entry points (UI, API endpoints, background workflows, and any integrated sharing/delegation mechanisms within system boundary).


### ac-3.5 — Security-relevant Information (Enhancement)

#### State-gated access to security-relevant configs and key material

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement technical enforcement so access to **security-relevant information**—defined as **ACLs, firewall/router filtering rules, security service configuration parameters, and cryptographic key management information**—is **prevented during normal operation** and **only permitted during secure, non-operable system states** (e.g., offline maintenance, boot-up, troubleshooting, shut down).

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-3(5)** (Enhancement) requires: **“Prevent access to {{ param, ac-03.05_odp }} except during secure, non-operable system states.”** The guidance clarifies that security-relevant information includes **ACLs, firewall/router filtering rules, security service configuration parameters, and cryptographic key management information**, and that secure, non-operable states include times when systems are **offline for maintenance, boot-up, troubleshooting, or shut down**.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define the exact security-relevant set and map storage locations
- Create an inventory that lists each security-relevant item and its storage/access path, including:
  - ACLs (e.g., router/switch ACL exports, firewall rule sets)
  - Firewall/router filtering rules (e.g., config files, policy objects)
  - Security service configuration parameters (e.g., TLS/VPN/IdP/WAF/EDR policy configs)
  - Cryptographic key management information (e.g., key identifiers, key-enrollment metadata, key-store references, secrets/vault entries that govern key usage)
- For each item, record the **system component**, **repository/location**, and **the access mechanism** (filesystem path, management API, secrets/vault UI/API, CI/CD artifact location, etc.).

### 2) Implement default-deny access to those items in normal operation
- Remove/deny direct read access to the above items from normal operational admin roles.
- Ensure only a mediated path can access them (e.g., privileged access workflow, restricted service account, or controlled management plane endpoint).
- Enforce least privilege so that even privileged users cannot directly query/view these items outside the approved state.

### 3) Add state-gated authorization (only allow during secure, non-operable states)
- Define the allowed **secure, non-operable states** in operational terms and map them to a machine-checkable signal, such as:
  - “Host/service marked offline/maintenance”
  - “Maintenance ticket approved and active”
  - “System in boot/troubleshooting/shutdown mode” (as applicable to your platform)
- Implement a gating check in the access mediation layer so that requests to security-relevant items are allowed **only if**:
  - The request is made by an authorized privileged role, AND
  - The target system is currently in an approved secure, non-operable state (per the machine-checkable signal), AND
  - The request is time-bounded (JIT) and tied to the approved maintenance/troubleshooting ticket.

### 4) Enforce access via a single controlled mechanism and audit everything
- Route all access attempts to security-relevant information through the mediation layer (e.g., PAM/privileged workflow, restricted management endpoint, or secrets/vault access broker).
- Configure audit logging to capture at minimum:
  - user identity
  - target item/resource
  - timestamp
  - requested action (read/view/export)
  - decision (allowed/denied)
  - associated maintenance/troubleshooting ticket ID
- Forward logs to the enterprise SIEM for retention and review.

### 5) Operational runbook and revocation
- Provide a runbook for how privileged operators request access during non-operable states (ticket creation, approval, expected duration, and how to verify the system state signal).
- Ensure automatic revocation/expiration when the maintenance window ends or the system returns to normal operation.

## WHO
- **Security Architect / IAM/PAM Engineer**: designs the state-gated access model, defines the security-relevant inventory mapping, and implements the mediation policy.
- **Privileged Access Management (PAM) Administrator** (or equivalent): configures the privileged workflow, JIT, and conditional access rules.
- **Platform/Infrastructure Engineers**: integrate the system-state signal (offline/maintenance/boot/troubleshooting indicators) with the gating mechanism.
- **SOC/Monitoring Team**: validates audit log ingestion into SIEM and supports alerting on denied attempts.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. **Default deny**: In normal operation, attempts to access any inventoried security-relevant item (ACLs, firewall/router filtering rules, security service config parameters, cryptographic key management information) are **denied** (no direct access path works).
2. **State gating**: When the system is in an approved secure, non-operable state (offline/maintenance/boot/troubleshooting/shutdown), authorized users can access the items **only** during that state; access is **denied** when the state signal is absent or the maintenance window is not approved.
3. **Audit evidence**: For every access attempt (allowed or denied), logs exist showing **who**, **what resource**, **when**, **action**, **decision**, and **ticket/state reference**.
4. **Time-bounded access**: Privileged access sessions expire automatically when the approved state ends.
5. **Coverage**: The implemented controls cover all items listed in the security-relevant inventory for the application’s administrative and security components (as defined by ac-03.05_odp).

### ac-3.7 — Role-based Access Control (Enhancement)

#### Implement centralized RBAC with HR-verified role assumption

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement role-based access control (RBAC) for the application so that access to defined subjects (users) and objects/functions (application resources and administrative actions) is granted strictly based on organization-defined roles, and role assumption is permitted only for users whose authorization to assume those roles is verified against approved HR/contractor records.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 AC-3(7) (Enhancement) requires: “Enforce a role-based access control policy over defined subjects and objects and control access based upon {{ insert: param, ac-3.7_prm_1 }}.” The organization-defined parameter **ac-3.7_prm_1** specifies “RBAC roles for DoD system users (e.g., ISSO, IAM admin, data steward) with authorised role assumption via approved HR/contractor records.”

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define RBAC subjects and objects/functions (in the application authorization model)
- Create an RBAC catalog that maps:
  - **Subjects**: covered user population (employees/contractors/partners/service accounts as applicable) and specifically the roles listed in **ac-03.07_odp.01** (ISSO, ISO, IAM Administrator, Data Steward, System Administrator, Helpdesk Analyst).
  - **Objects/functions**: application endpoints, UI actions, API operations, data domains/records, and administrative functions (e.g., user management, configuration changes, report exports, data approval workflows).
- Ensure the RBAC model is applied consistently across presentation, API, and background/admin services.

### 2) Implement role-to-privilege mapping using the organization-defined roles
- For each role in **ac-03.07_odp.01**, define allowed privileges (role-to-privilege mapping) aligned to the application’s object/function catalog.
- Remove/disable ad-hoc per-user authorization grants in the application where feasible; authorization decisions must be derived from role membership.

### 3) Enforce authorization based on role membership (not direct permissions)
- Integrate with the enterprise identity provider (IdP) and enforce authorization at the application layer:
  - Use role claims/groups from the IdP to determine the user’s effective role(s).
  - Apply authorization checks on every protected action (server-side enforcement for web/API; deny-by-default).
- Ensure administrative functions are isolated from standard user flows where feasible (e.g., separate admin routes/services with the same RBAC enforcement).

### 4) Enforce HR/contractor-verified role assumption for covered roles
- Configure the IdP/provisioning workflow so that role assignment for the covered roles is sourced from approved HR/contractor records:
  - Only users with an approved HR/contractor record indicating authorization to assume the role(s) in **ac-3.7_prm_1** may receive the corresponding IdP role/group.
  - For exceptions, require an approval ticket/reference that is stored/linked to the provisioning event and is auditable.
- Block role assumption if the HR/contractor authorization reference is missing, expired, or does not match the role.

### 5) Produce audit evidence for role changes and access decisions
- Enable and retain audit logs for:
  - Role assignment/provisioning events (who assigned/approved, when, and which role).
  - Role assumption/role change events (including exception approvals).
  - Authorization decisions (allow/deny) for protected objects/functions.
- Ensure logs are centralized to the enterprise logging/SIEM platform and are retained per organizational policy.

### 6) Validate and document the RBAC implementation
- Document:
  - The RBAC catalog (roles, subjects, objects/functions, role-to-privilege mapping).
  - The enforcement points (where authorization is checked).
  - The HR/contractor verification mechanism (source of truth and exception handling).
- Perform a test plan that verifies each role can only perform its mapped actions.

## WHO
- **IAM Administrator**: owns IdP role/group configuration, provisioning integration, and HR/contractor verification workflow.
- **Application Owner / System Administrator**: owns application authorization enforcement implementation and RBAC catalog for application objects/functions.
- **ISSO / Data Steward (as applicable)**: reviews and approves role-to-privilege mappings and exception handling criteria.
- **Security/Compliance (supporting)**: validates audit evidence and test results.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. **RBAC enforcement**: For every protected object/function, the application denies access by default and allows access only when the user has the mapped role membership.
2. **Role assumption restriction**: Users cannot obtain covered roles unless their role assignment is backed by approved HR/contractor authorization (missing/expired authorization results in blocked provisioning/role assignment).
3. **No ad-hoc grants**: Direct per-user authorization overrides for covered roles are either eliminated or demonstrably controlled and auditable; effective access is derived from role membership.
4. **Auditability**: Audit logs exist and show (a) role assignment/assumption events and (b) authorization allow/deny decisions for protected actions.
5. **Test coverage**: For each role (ISSO, ISO, IAM Administrator, Data Steward, System Administrator, Helpdesk Analyst), test cases confirm only the intended actions are permitted and all others are denied.

### ac-3.8 — Revocation of Access Authorizations (Enhancement)

#### Session/token invalidation and re-eval on attribute change

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement revocation timing enforcement so that when a subject’s or object’s security attributes change, any affected access authorizations are revoked according to the organization-defined rules:
- **Security-label changes:** revocation takes effect **immediately**.
- **Other security-attribute changes:** revocation takes effect at the **earliest of**:
  1) the **next time the object is opened**, or
  2) the **next time the subject attempts to access** the object.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST **AC-3(8)** enhancement requires enforcing revocation of access authorizations resulting from changes to security attributes of **subjects and objects** based on the organization-defined timing rules (**ac-03.08_odp**). Guidance notes that revocation may be delayed for some attribute changes (e.g., group membership) until the next object open or next access attempt, while **security-label changes may take effect immediately**. 

## HOW (specific approach)
### 1) Define “label-change” vs “non-label attribute change” in the app
- In the application’s authorization layer, classify attribute-change events into:
  - **Label-change** (e.g., data classification/security label changes on objects; security label changes on subjects)
  - **Non-label attribute change** (e.g., group/role membership changes, entitlement changes that are not security-label changes)
- Ensure the classification is driven by the same attribute taxonomy used by the enterprise IAM/data classification services.

### 2) Immediate revocation for security-label changes (session-aware)
Implement one of the following (choose what matches your stack capabilities):
- **Preferred (IdP-supported):** when a label-change event is detected, call the IdP/session service to **revoke/terminate active sessions or invalidate tokens** for the affected subject(s) (and/or force re-auth on next request).
- **Application-enforced immediate revocation:** maintain a **server-side authorization version/epoch** (e.g., `authz_epoch`) per subject and/or per object label scope.
  - On label-change event, increment/update the relevant epoch(s).
  - On every request that could use cached authorization (especially for sensitive operations), compare the token/session’s embedded epoch (or a cached epoch value) to the current epoch.
  - If mismatch, deny access and require re-authentication.

### 3) Delayed revocation for non-label attribute changes (enforce at first access boundary)
For non-label attribute changes, ensure the system revokes at the earliest boundary:
- **Object-open boundary:** for UI/API operations that “open” or load an object (document view, record retrieval, file open, metadata fetch that enables access), perform an authorization check that re-evaluates current attributes.
- **Subject-access attempt boundary:** for operations that attempt access (read/write/execute/delete), perform authorization checks at the start of each sensitive operation.
- Do **not** rely on long-lived cached entitlements for these checks; if caching exists, set it to short TTL and/or invalidate on attribute-change events.

### 4) Triggering mechanism (event-driven)
- Subscribe to attribute-change events from enterprise sources (e.g., IAM/IdP events for subject attribute changes; data classification/label management events for object label changes).
- Route events to an internal “Authorization Revocation Service” that:
  - Determines label-change vs non-label attribute change.
  - Applies immediate revocation actions (session/token invalidation or epoch bump).
  - Records the event for auditability.

### 5) Auditability (evidence for timing)
Log at minimum:
- Attribute-change event timestamp
- Subject ID(s) and/or object ID(s)
- Attribute type (label vs non-label)
- Enforcement action taken (session invalidated/epoch bumped; or “revocation deferred until next open/attempt”)
- For each subsequent access attempt that is denied due to revocation: decision timestamp and reason (e.g., epoch mismatch; authorization re-evaluation failed)

## WHO
- **Application Security Engineer / Backend Engineer:** implement authorization boundary checks, epoch/versioning, and event handling.
- **IAM/Platform Engineer:** integrate with IdP/session revocation APIs (if used) and ensure event feeds are available.
- **Security Operations / Compliance Analyst:** validate audit evidence and timing behavior during testing and audits.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. **Label-change immediate revocation:**
   - When a subject or object security label changes, an active session/token that previously had access is denied **immediately** (within the organization’s defined “immediate” operational expectation, e.g., next request) without requiring manual logout.
2. **Non-label attribute change delayed revocation:**
   - When a non-label security attribute changes, access is allowed until the **first** subsequent object-open or subject access attempt, and then is denied.
3. **Correct boundary selection:**
   - If the object is opened before the subject attempts access (or vice versa), the system denies at the earlier of the two boundaries.
4. **Audit evidence:**
   - Audit logs show the attribute-change event time and the enforcement/denial time, and clearly indicate whether the change was treated as label-change (immediate) or non-label (deferred).
5. **Regression coverage:**
   - Automated tests simulate both label-change and non-label attribute-change events and verify denial timing behavior for representative object types and access operations.


### ac-3.9 — Controlled Release (Enhancement)

#### Attribute-mapped controlled release gate for external recipients

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement a controlled-release decision gate that only allows the application to release information outside the system to **defined external receiving systems/contractor-controlled endpoints** when the recipient’s **security and privacy controls** are **equivalent and appropriate** for the recipient’s accreditation and the **specific attributes** of the exported information.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-3(9) Controlled Release** requires that release information outside the system only if the organization can validate the information’s appropriateness prior to release, including (per guidance) using **technical or procedural means** to validate exported information attributes, and when the system cannot determine adequacy of external protections, using **procedural determination** supported by **periodic assessments/inspection/test evidence**, **agreements**, and **attribute mapping**.

This solution directly operationalizes the enhancement parameters:
- **ac-03.09_odp.01**: define the external receiving system/contractor endpoint
- **ac-03.09_odp.02**: define the controls to be provided by the outside system
- **ac-03.09_odp.03**: define the controls used to validate appropriateness (contractual assurance + periodic assessment results + attribute mapping)

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define eligible recipients (recipient registry)
- Create/maintain an authoritative **Recipient Accreditation Registry** (can be a GRC system, CMDB extension, or dedicated service).
- For each external receiving system/endpoint, record:
  - Endpoint identity (system name, domain/URL, API client ID, partner identifier, printer/service identifier if applicable)
  - Accreditation/authorization status and scope
  - Expiration date for periodic assessment evidence
  - Documented **security and privacy controls provided** (or control set reference)
  - Contractual assurance reference (contract/MoU clause IDs)
  - Data handling constraints the recipient commits to (retention, access restrictions, breach notification, etc.)

### 2) Define information release attributes and mapping rules
- Extend the application’s data classification model to include **release attributes** required for validation (e.g., confidentiality level, personal data category, encryption-in-transit/at-rest expectations, logging/audit expectations, retention limits, breach notification SLA, lawful basis/privacy constraints).
- Implement **attribute mapping** rules that translate each outbound “release type” (or data export job) into a set of **required recipient control attributes**.

### 3) Implement a release gate in the outbound workflow (policy-as-code)
- Integrate a **Controlled Release Gate** into the application’s outbound data export/share workflow (e.g., before API calls, file transfers, message publishing, or report delivery).
- For each outbound request, the gate must:
  1. Identify the **target external receiving system/endpoint** (ac-03.09_odp.01)
  2. Confirm a valid **contractual assurance** record exists for that recipient and data category (ac-03.09_odp.03)
  3. Confirm **periodic assessment evidence** is present and not expired (inspection/test results or equivalent) (ac-03.09_odp.03)
  4. Validate that the recipient’s recorded controls are **equivalent and appropriate** for the mapped required attributes (ac-03.09_odp.02 + attribute mapping)
  5. If validation fails or evidence is missing/expired, **deny release** and generate an exception ticket for human adjudication.

### 4) Evidence generation and retention (audit-ready)
- For every allowed release, store an immutable audit record containing:
  - Recipient identifier and accreditation scope
  - Contract reference(s)
  - Assessment evidence reference(s) and dates
  - Attribute mapping inputs and the resulting required-vs-provided control equivalence decision
  - Decision outcome (allow/deny) and approver (if exception)

### 5) Operational handling of exceptions
- Define an exception process where security/privacy/compliance reviews cases where equivalence cannot be automatically determined.
- Exceptions must still be backed by documented contractual assurance and/or assessment evidence, and must be time-bounded.

## WHO (roles responsible)
- **Application Owner / Engineering Lead**: implement the release gate integration and attribute mapping enforcement.
- **Information Security & Privacy (GRC) Lead**: maintain recipient registry, equivalence criteria, and evidence requirements.
- **Compliance/Legal**: ensure contractual assurance references and clauses support the required controls.
- **Security Operations / Audit**: review denied releases, manage evidence retention, and support periodic assessment evidence ingestion.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. The application cannot complete an outbound release to an external endpoint unless the **recipient registry** contains that endpoint as eligible.
2. For each allowed release, the system records:
   - contractual assurance reference
   - periodic assessment evidence reference(s) with valid dates
   - attribute mapping decision inputs and equivalence outcome
3. If assessment evidence is missing/expired or attribute mapping equivalence fails, the gate **denies release** and creates an exception workflow item.
4. A test suite demonstrates at least:
   - allow case (equivalent controls + current evidence)
   - deny case (expired evidence)
   - deny case (recipient lacks required control attributes)
   - exception case (human adjudication with documented evidence)
5. Evidence records are retained according to organizational audit retention policy and are tamper-evident (e.g., append-only storage or WORM/log signing).

### ac-3.10 — Audited Override of Access Control Mechanisms (Enhancement)

#### Dual-approval break-glass access override with audited execution

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 82%

## WHAT
Implement an emergency “audited override” capability for automated access control mechanisms that can be executed **only** under the organization-defined **override conditions** and **only** by the organization-defined **roles** (ISSO and designated duty officer), with **every override action fully audited**.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-3(10)** (Enhancement) requires: “Employ an audited override of automated access control mechanisms under **{{ ac-03.10_odp.01 conditions }}** by **{{ ac-03.10_odp.02 roles }}**.”
The guidance further states override conditions are defined by the organization and used only in limited circumstances, and that audit events/records are generated per **AU-2** and **AU-12**.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define and enforce override eligibility (conditions)
- Create an internal, system-enforced set of **override conditions** matching the provided parameters:
  - **Threat to human life**, OR
  - **Prevent loss of mission-critical capability during an active incident or declared emergency**.
- Implement a workflow pre-check that requires:
  - An **incident/emergency reference** (ticket/incident ID) and a **status** indicating “active incident” or “declared emergency”.
  - A **justification** field that explicitly selects one of the two condition categories above.
- Block override execution if the incident status/justification does not meet the defined conditions.

### 2) Enforce dual authorization (roles)
- Configure the override workflow to require **two independent approvals** before any override is executed:
  - Approval by the **ISSO**
  - Approval by the **designated duty officer**
- Ensure separation of duties in the workflow:
  - The same individual cannot satisfy both approvals.
  - Only identities mapped to the ISSO and duty officer roles can approve.

### 3) Restrict who can execute the override
- Implement the override as a **tightly scoped, time-bounded** action (e.g., temporary elevation or temporary access-policy change) rather than a general bypass.
- Ensure only the workflow’s final “execute” step can trigger the override, and that the execute step is restricted to:
  - the ISSO/duty officer identities, or
  - explicitly configured delegates acting under their control (if your governance permits delegates, document and restrict them).

### 4) Generate auditable evidence for every override (AU-2/AU-12 alignment)
- For each override execution, automatically generate an audit record containing at minimum:
  - **Who** requested, who approved (ISSO and duty officer), and who executed (if different)
  - **What** was overridden (resource/system, permission/effect, scope)
  - **When** (timestamp)
  - **Why** (selected condition category + justification text)
  - **Incident reference** (ticket/incident ID and status)
  - **Duration/expiry** (if time-bounded)
- Send audit events to the organization’s centralized logging/SIEM and ensure they are retained per AU-12 retention requirements.
- Ensure audit records are generated regardless of success/failure of the override attempt (at least for the attempt and decision outcome).

### 5) Document and operationalize
- Update the system’s access control procedures and security documentation to describe:
  - the defined override conditions (life-safety / mission-critical loss)
  - the authorized roles (ISSO and duty officer)
  - the dual-approval workflow
  - the required audit fields and where audit evidence is stored
- Train the ISSO and duty officer on the workflow and evidence requirements.

## WHO
- **Information System Security Officer (ISSO):** approves override requests and validates justification/incident context.
- **Designated Duty Officer:** provides the second independent approval and authorizes execution.
- **IAM/Platform Security Engineer (implementer):** implements workflow gating, role mapping, scoped override actions, and audit event generation.
- **SIEM/Logging Administrator (support):** ensures audit event routing and retention.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. **Condition enforcement:** Override execution is blocked unless the workflow includes an incident/emergency reference with status indicating “active incident” or “declared emergency” and a justification categorized as either “threat to human life” or “prevent loss of mission-critical capability.”
2. **Dual approval:** Every successful override requires approvals from **both** ISSO and designated duty officer, and the same identity cannot satisfy both approvals.
3. **Execution restriction:** Only the workflow’s authorized execution path can apply the override, and only identities mapped to the authorized roles (or explicitly permitted delegates) can execute.
4. **Audit completeness:** For each override attempt (success and failure), audit records are generated containing: requestor, both approvers, executed action details (resource/scope/permission), timestamp, condition category/justification, and incident reference.
5. **Audit routing/retention:** Audit events are forwarded to the centralized logging/SIEM and retained according to the organization’s AU-12 policy.


### ac-3.11 — Restrict Access to Specific Information Types (Enhancement)

#### Repository access controls for key/auth/selected system info types

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement enforcement so that **data repositories containing the organisation-defined information types** (parameter **ac-03.11_odp: “cryptographic keys, authentication information, and selected system information”**) are **restricted to authorised identities** and **not accessible broadly** (e.g., not via general database/app roles).

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-3(11)** (enhancement) requires: **“Restrict access to data repositories containing {{ insert: param, ac-03.11_odp }}.”** The control guidance explains the intent is to provide flexibility to restrict access to **specific information types** (e.g., cryptographic keys, authentication information, selected system information) rather than the entire repository.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Identify and label protected repositories and protected objects
- Create/maintain an authoritative mapping (in the system’s data/asset catalogue) of:
  - Repositories that contain **cryptographic keys** (e.g., secret stores, key tables, key material locations).
  - Repositories that contain **authentication information** (e.g., credential/credential-derivative stores, password hash stores, auth tokens/refresh tokens if stored).
  - Repositories that contain **selected system information** (map this to concrete repository/object types in your environment).
- For each repository, define the **protected scope** at the lowest practical level (schema/table/view, bucket/object prefix, secret path, etc.).

### 2) Enforce repository/type-level authorization (deny-by-default)
Implement technical controls so that access is granted only to authorised roles/groups for the **protected scope**:
- **Databases**: create dedicated DB roles for each protected information type; grant permissions only on the protected schemas/tables/views (and explicitly deny/omit broader privileges such as access to other schemas).
- **Object storage / file repositories**: use bucket/object policies with explicit allow lists for groups/roles; deny by default for protected prefixes/objects.
- **Secret/key/auth stores**: enforce access via the secret-management layer (or equivalent) using narrowly scoped service identities and secret-path policies (e.g., only allow read of specific secret paths; prevent listing where feasible).
- **Applications/services**: ensure service accounts used by the application for business functions do not have privileges to read protected scopes unless required.

### 3) Integrate with enterprise identity and privileged access
- Use the enterprise IdP for authorization decisions (RBAC/ABAC) and require privileged access to protected scopes to be granted only to the defined authorised roles.
- Ensure privileged access is mediated (e.g., step-up/MFA for privileged roles where supported by your platform).

### 4) Make access changes auditable and reviewable
- Implement access rule changes via configuration management / infrastructure-as-code (policy-as-code) so that:
  - Changes are reviewable (PR/approval workflow).
  - The resulting permissions can be exported as evidence.
- Configure audit logging for repository access attempts (both allowed and denied) for the protected scopes.

### 5) Evidence generation for audit readiness
Produce and retain evidence that demonstrates:
- The **protected information types** are defined and mapped to repositories/objects.
- The **enforcement** exists (permission/policy configuration exports).
- **Audit records** show access attempts to protected scopes are logged and that only authorised access is permitted.

## WHO
- **Application Owner / Data Owner**: approves the mapping of information types to repositories/objects and authorises which roles may access each protected scope.
- **IAM/Platform Security Engineer**: implements IdP group/role mappings and privileged access mediation.
- **Database/Storage Administrator (or Cloud Security Engineer)**: implements repository/type-level permissions and secret-path/object policies.
- **Security/Compliance Analyst**: validates evidence completeness (policy exports + audit logs) for AC-3(11).

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. For every repository/object identified as containing **cryptographic keys, authentication information, and selected system information**, there exists a documented mapping to the protected scope.
2. Technical enforcement is implemented such that:
   - Only authorised roles/groups can access the protected scope.
   - Access is **not** granted via broad repository-level permissions that would allow access to non-protected data.
3. Audit logs capture access attempts (allowed and denied) to the protected scopes and are retained per organisational logging requirements.
4. A configuration/policy export (from the implemented permission/policy mechanism) can be produced showing the allow/deny rules for the protected scopes.
5. A test performed by a non-authorised identity against each protected scope results in **denied** access and generates an audit event.

### ac-3.12 — Assert and Enforce Application Access (Enhancement)

#### Install-time app permission declaration with enforced allowlist

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 74%

## WHAT
Implement an application permission governance mechanism that (1) requires each application to declare which **system applications and functions** it needs at **install/deployment time** (from the defined set), (2) enforces that declared permission set immediately after installation by denying any unapproved access, and (3) requires approval before any **post-install permission/access changes** are allowed to take effect.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-3(12) enhancement requires asserting and enforcing application access for applications that need access to system applications/functions, ensuring the organization can block unauthorized access and that permission changes after installation are reviewed/approved before becoming effective. The organization-defined parameter explicitly includes: **user contacts, global positioning systems, cameras, keyboards, microphones, networks, phones, and other files**.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define the authoritative permission categories and mapping
- Create an organization-controlled permission taxonomy aligned to the parameter **ac-03.12_odp** (contacts, GPS/location, camera, keyboard input, microphone, network access, phone functions, other files).
- For each managed app, maintain an **approved permission profile** (the exact subset of categories the app is allowed to use).

### 2) Capture install-time declared permissions (source of truth)
- Require that all application installs occur via an enterprise-managed channel (e.g., MDM/enterprise app catalog/restricted deployment pipeline).
- During deployment, collect the app’s declared permission set from the platform/app metadata and/or the app’s manifest (where available) and store it as **install-time declared permissions**.
- Validate completeness by comparing the declared set to the app’s catalog record (e.g., feature-to-permission mapping maintained by the app owner/security).

### 3) Enforce access immediately after installation
- Apply OS/endpoint/mobile/app-management enforcement so that the device/runtime denies any permission category not present in the **approved permission profile**.
- Ensure enforcement is applied as part of the deployment workflow so there is no “first run” window where unapproved permissions are granted.

### 4) Gate post-install permission/access changes
- On every app update (or permission change event), compute a **permission diff** between:
  - previously approved profile vs.
  - newly declared permissions.
- If the diff includes any new/expanded categories, block the update from being activated (or roll back/hold deployment) until an approval workflow completes.
- Approval workflow must include: app owner justification, security/privacy review (as applicable), and explicit approval of the updated approved permission profile.

### 5) Auditability and evidence
- Log and retain evidence for each app deployment/update:
  - install-time declared permissions
  - approved permission profile
  - enforcement outcome (granted/denied per category)
  - permission diffs on update and approval record
- Send relevant events to SIEM for monitoring (e.g., “permission drift” alerts when runtime permissions exceed approved profile).

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Application Owner / Product Owner:** provides justification and feature-to-permission mapping; initiates approval requests for permission changes.
- **Security/Privacy Team:** reviews and approves/denies expanded permission profiles.
- **Endpoint/Platform Engineering (MDM/IT Ops):** implements deployment workflow, enforcement policies, and audit logging.
- **Compliance/Audit Lead:** ensures evidence retention and periodic review of permission governance.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. For every managed app installed through the controlled channel, the system records **install-time declared permissions** across the defined categories (contacts, GPS, camera, keyboard input, microphone, network, phone, other files).
2. After installation, runtime/OS/endpoint enforcement denies any permission category not included in the **approved permission profile**; evidence shows enforcement occurred immediately post-install.
3. When an app update introduces new permission categories, the update is **blocked/held** until the approval workflow completes; evidence includes permission diff and approval record.
4. Audit logs exist and can be exported showing: declared permissions, approved profile, enforcement outcome, and post-install change approvals for a sample of apps (including at least one app with a permission expansion and one without).


### ac-3.13 — Attribute-based Access Control (Enhancement)

#### Implement ABAC policy-as-code with enforcement + decision audit logs

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement an attribute-based access control (ABAC) capability that:
- Evaluates access using **subject attributes** (e.g., job function, identity assurance level, clearance Secret/Confidential, system role), **object/resource attributes** (resource classification), and **environment attributes** (on-prem location and approved time window).
- **Allows or denies** each access attempt based on those attribute matches.
- Produces **audit records** that capture the allow/deny outcome and the decision basis (attributes evaluated).

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-3(13)** enhancement requires: “Enforce attribute-based access control policy over defined subjects and objects and control access based upon {{ insert: param, ac-03.13_odp }}.” The provided parameter indicates the attributes to use (job function, identity assurance level, clearance, system role, resource classification, and environment such as on-prem location/time window). This solution ensures enforcement is consistent, auditable, and applies to every defined subject/object/action.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define ABAC scope (subjects/objects)
- Identify the **subjects**: user accounts (employees/contractors/partners), service accounts, and privileged operators that can access the application.
- Identify the **objects**: application resources (e.g., endpoints/actions, records, documents, data partitions) and define which resources are covered by ABAC.
- For each object type, define the **resource classification attribute** source (e.g., application metadata, CMDB/asset registry, document management classification tags).

### 2) Establish authoritative attribute sources
- **Job function**: HR system → IdP/app attribute claim.
- **Identity assurance level**: derive from authentication method used in the IdP (e.g., AAL2/AAL3) and include in issued tokens/session context.
- **Clearance (Secret/Confidential)**: clearance workflow → IdP/app claim.
- **System role**: mapping from RBAC roles to ABAC roles/attributes (or direct claim).
- **Environment (on-prem location/time window)**:
  - On-prem location: determine via server-side network context (e.g., ZTNA client posture + corporate egress IP ranges / internal network segment) rather than client-side signals.
  - Time window: define approved windows per policy (e.g., business hours or role-specific windows) and evaluate server-side.

### 3) Create ABAC policy as code
- Implement ABAC rules in a policy-as-code format (e.g., OPA/Rego, vendor policy DSL, or a centralized authorization service policy engine).
- Encode rules that require **all relevant attribute matches** for allow decisions, including:
  - subject clearance vs. resource classification
  - subject job function/system role vs. allowed actions on the object
  - identity assurance level meeting minimum requirements for the action
  - environment constraints (on-prem + time window)
- Ensure default behavior is **deny** when required attributes are missing/invalid.

### 4) Enforce ABAC at a central enforcement point
- Place enforcement at an application/API authorization layer (e.g., API gateway, authorization middleware, or a centralized authorization service) so every request/action is evaluated.
- For each request:
  - Extract subject attributes from the IdP token/session.
  - Retrieve object/resource classification from the application’s resource metadata (or a classification lookup service).
  - Compute environment attributes server-side (on-prem context + current time vs. allowed window).
  - Evaluate ABAC policy and return allow/deny.

### 5) Generate auditable decision logs
- For every allow/deny decision, log at minimum:
  - subject identifier (and account type)
  - object/resource identifier (or resource type)
  - action attempted (read/write/delete/admin)
  - evaluated attribute values or decision-relevant attribute identifiers (as permitted by privacy policy)
  - final decision (allow/deny)
  - policy rule identifier/version used
  - timestamp and enforcement point identifier
- Forward logs to the enterprise SIEM for retention and review.

### 6) Operationalize and evidence
- Store policy-as-code in version control with change management approvals.
- Provide configuration evidence (policy version, enforcement component configuration, attribute mapping configuration).
- Update the system security plan to describe ABAC enforcement and audit logging responsibilities.

## WHO
- **Application Security Architect / IAM Engineer**: define ABAC model, attribute mappings, and policy rules.
- **Backend/API Engineers**: implement enforcement integration at the API/application layer.
- **Security Operations / SIEM Engineer**: configure log forwarding, retention, and alerting for repeated denies.
- **System Owner**: approve ABAC scope and policy intent for covered subjects/objects.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. **Policy enforcement coverage**: For all defined subject/object/action combinations in scope, access decisions are made by the ABAC enforcement point (no bypass paths).
2. **Attribute-based decisions**: Allow decisions occur only when required subject attributes, resource classification, and environment attributes match the ABAC policy; otherwise access is denied.
3. **Default deny**: If required attributes are missing/invalid, the system denies access.
4. **Environment enforcement**: Access is denied when the request is outside the approved on-prem context or outside the approved time window.
5. **Auditability**: Every allow/deny decision generates an audit record containing decision outcome and decision-relevant attribute basis (and policy rule/version).
6. **Evidence available**: Policy-as-code repository history and deployed policy version/configuration evidence can be provided for audit.

### ac-3.14 — Individual Access (Enhancement)

#### Implement authenticated web SAR portal for individual PII access

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement the organization-defined **mechanisms** and **elements** for AC-3(14):
- A **web-based subject access request (SAR) portal** and an **authenticated application interface** for individuals.
- The interface must allow individuals to access their **PII elements** in system-of-record databases, including **data fields**, **processing purposes**, and **disclosure/transfer history** (where applicable).

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-3(14) enhancement requires: “Provide {{ac-03.14_odp.01}} to enable individuals to have access to {{ac-03.14_odp.02}}.”
The guidance further requires that access mechanisms support individuals reviewing their PII regardless of format, and that access decisions/limitations are handled appropriately (with privacy/legal input where needed).

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define the SAR “view” content model (privacy view model)
- Create a canonical schema for the SAR response UI/API that includes:
  - **PII data fields** (field name + value + source system/record context as appropriate)
  - **Processing purposes** (plain-language purpose statements mapped to internal processing records)
  - **Disclosure/transfer history** (only where applicable; include recipient categories/transfer mechanism metadata as permitted)
- Map each schema element to authoritative sources (system-of-record DBs and privacy metadata sources such as RoPA/data maps and transfer logs).

### 2) Build the web SAR portal workflow
- Implement a portal that supports:
  - **Request intake** (submission of SAR, identity verification steps, request reference)
  - **Status tracking** (request lifecycle)
  - **Fulfillment delivery** via the authenticated interface (view and, if allowed by policy, export/download)
- Ensure the portal is **web-based** and provides an **authenticated** interface for viewing.

### 3) Enforce identity binding and authorization on every retrieval
- Integrate with the enterprise IdP (central identity service) and require **strong authentication** for viewing/exporting.
- Bind the SAR request reference to the authenticated subject identity.
- On every data retrieval call:
  - Authorize that the requester identity matches the subject identifier used to query system-of-record data.
  - Apply server-side filtering so the portal can only return the individual’s own PII.

### 4) Implement least-privilege data access to system-of-records
- Use dedicated service accounts/roles for SAR fulfillment with **read-only** permissions where possible.
- Prefer privacy-safe approaches:
  - Query-time filtering by subject identifier
  - Read replicas or controlled extracts to reduce exposure
- Ensure the portal can retrieve PII elements across the relevant system-of-record databases.

### 5) Add audit logging for SAR and viewing/export actions
- Log at minimum:
  - SAR submission (who/when/request reference)
  - Identity verification completion
  - Authorization decision outcome
  - Record retrieval events
  - Any view and export/download actions
- Forward logs to the enterprise monitoring/SIEM and retain per organizational logging/retention policy.

### 6) Encode access limitations and legal/privacy review hooks
- Implement policy-driven rules for exemptions/limitations (e.g., where certain records may not be appropriate for disclosure).
- Provide an internal workflow step for privacy/legal counsel review when rules indicate limitations.
- Ensure the workflow produces an auditable decision record (approved/denied/partially fulfilled with reason codes).

## WHO
- **Application Owner / Product Owner**: owns SAR portal requirements and user experience.
- **Privacy Officer / Privacy Counsel**: defines permissible elements, purpose/transfer presentation rules, and limitation/exemption logic.
- **IAM Engineer**: integrates IdP authentication and step-up requirements.
- **Backend/Data Engineers**: implement least-privilege data retrieval and view model mapping.
- **Security Engineer**: ensures audit logging, monitoring integration, and secure configuration.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. The system provides a **web-based SAR portal** and an **authenticated interface** for individuals to access their records.
2. For a test subject, the authenticated user can view only their own PII elements, including:
   - **data fields**
   - **processing purposes**
   - **disclosure/transfer history** where applicable
3. Attempts to access another subject’s records fail authorization (no cross-subject data leakage).
4. SAR request lifecycle actions (submission, identity verification, fulfillment, view/export) generate audit events in the SIEM with request reference and subject identity.
5. When limitation rules are triggered, the workflow routes to privacy/legal review and records an auditable decision outcome.


### ac-3.15 — Discretionary and Mandatory Access Control (Enhancement)

#### Enforce MAC+DAC via PDP/PEP with labels, clearance, RBAC/ACL

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement **simultaneous mandatory access control (MAC)** and **discretionary access control (DAC)** for the in-scope application/data resources by using a centralized **policy decision point (PDP)** and **policy enforcement point (PEP)**. Subjects must have **clearance levels** and objects must have **security labels** (MAC). In addition, access must be constrained by **least-privilege RBAC roles/groups** and **ACLs** (DAC).

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-3(15) enhancement guidance states that **simultaneously implementing a mandatory access control policy and a discretionary access control policy** provides additional protection against unauthorized execution of code by users or processes acting on behalf of users, and helps prevent a single compromised user/process from compromising the entire system. The required outcome is that both MAC and DAC are defined and enforced over the covered subjects/objects.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define the covered scope and label/clearance model (MAC)
- Create an authoritative label taxonomy (e.g., `Confidential`, `Secret`) and a clearance taxonomy (e.g., `User`, `Confidential`, `Secret`) aligned to the organization’s data classification.
- Define the **MAC rule**: a subject may access an object only if `subject_clearance >= object_label` for the requested action.
- Bind this model to authoritative identifiers for **in-scope subjects** (users, service accounts, workloads) and **in-scope objects** (documents/records, API resources, database rows/partitions, message topics/queues, executable artifacts if applicable).

### 2) Define the DAC model (RBAC + ACL)
- Implement **RBAC**: map users/service accounts to roles/groups that represent least-privilege job functions.
- Implement **ACLs** for the same covered objects and actions (e.g., `read`, `write`, `execute`, `invoke-service`, `admin`), ensuring no broad “catch-all” permissions.
- Ensure DAC is action-specific and supports least privilege (e.g., separate roles for read vs execute).

### 3) Implement PDP/PEP enforcement with combined decision semantics
- Place a **PEP** in front of every authorization-relevant operation (API gateway, service mesh authorization, or application authorization middleware) so all access requests are mediated.
- The **PDP** evaluates policies in a deterministic order and produces a final allow/deny decision that reflects both policies:
  - **MAC check** using subject clearance vs object label.
  - **DAC check** using RBAC role/group membership and ACL permissions for the requested action.
- Enforce “deny overrides allow” semantics so that **if MAC denies, access is denied even if DAC would allow**, and **if DAC denies, access is denied even if MAC would allow**.

### 4) Integrate with identity and object labeling
- Integrate subject attributes from the enterprise IdP (e.g., clearance as a claim/attribute; roles/groups for RBAC).
- Ensure object labels are stored as authoritative metadata at object creation/update time (e.g., document classification metadata, dataset classification, resource label in the authorization service).
- For service-to-service calls, propagate the calling identity (workload identity) so the PDP can evaluate MAC and DAC consistently.

### 5) Produce auditable decision evidence
- For every authorization decision, emit structured audit events to the SIEM/logging pipeline including at minimum:
  - subject identifier, subject clearance
  - object identifier, object label
  - requested action
  - RBAC roles/groups used
  - ACL match result
  - final decision (allow/deny) and decision rationale (MAC fail vs DAC fail vs both)

### 6) Operationalize via policy-as-code and change control
- Store MAC and DAC policies in a version-controlled repository.
- Require change management approvals for policy changes.
- Add automated tests for policy rules (e.g., clearance vs label matrix; role/ACL permission cases).

## WHO
- **Solution/Platform Engineer (Lead):** designs PDP/PEP integration and policy evaluation logic.
- **IAM Engineer:** maps IdP attributes/claims to clearance and RBAC roles/groups.
- **Application Owner/Architect:** identifies in-scope subjects/objects and ensures all authorization paths route through the PEP.
- **Security Engineer/Compliance:** validates label taxonomy, policy-as-code governance, and audit evidence requirements.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. For all in-scope authorization requests, the system routes through the PEP and the PDP evaluates **both** MAC and DAC.
2. If `subject_clearance < object_label`, the PDP returns **deny** regardless of RBAC/ACL permissions.
3. If RBAC/ACL does not grant the requested action, the PDP returns **deny** regardless of MAC clearance.
4. Audit logs contain, for each decision, subject clearance, object label, requested action, MAC/DAC evaluation outcomes, and final allow/deny.
5. Policy-as-code repository exists for MAC and DAC with version history and change-control workflow.
6. Automated tests demonstrate correct deny/allow behavior for representative subject/object/action combinations.

### ac-4 — Information Flow Enforcement (Control)

#### Implement label-aware cross-domain flow enforcement at boundaries

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
