Implement technical information-flow enforcement at all cross-domain boundary points (within the system and between connected systems) so that only **approved flows** are permitted based on the organization’s **information flow control policies** (ac-04_odp). Enforcement must include:
- **Blocking spoofed “internal” traffic** unless explicitly permitted.
- **Restricting web requests** to go through the **internal web proxy**.
- **Preventing export-controlled information** from being transmitted to the Internet in the clear (and only allowing approved export destinations/paths).
- **Verifying write permissions** before accepting information from another security/privacy domain or connected system.
- **Applying label-based routing and (where required) regrading/label reassignment** at designated policy enforcement points.
- Applying the same flow enforcement to **control-plane traffic** (e.g., DNS and routing-adjacent services).

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-4 Information Flow Enforcement** requires the organization to “**enforce approved authorizations for controlling the flow of information within the system and between connected systems** … based on {{ insert: param, ac-04_odp }}.” The guidance further requires flow control for boundary protection (packet/message filtering), write-permission checks for incoming information from other domains, trusted enforcement at enforcement points, and coverage for control-plane traffic.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define the enforcement points and policy decision inputs
- Identify all boundary points where flows cross:
  - internal zone boundaries (e.g., app tier → data tier, user → admin functions)
  - connected systems boundaries (e.g., partner/vendor integrations, OT/IT gateways)
  - egress boundaries to Internet
  - proxy boundaries for web
- For each boundary, define the **policy decision inputs** aligned to ac-04_odp, at minimum:
  - source/destination identity (workload/service identity and zone/domain)
  - protocol/service (including DNS and routing-adjacent services)
  - information handling label(s) carried by the session/transaction (from DLP tags, document metadata, API payload classification, or session attributes)
  - direction (inbound vs outbound)
  - whether the flow is **write** (data modification) vs read/allow
  - export-controlled indicator(s)

### 2) Implement label-aware policy enforcement at the boundary
- Deploy/configure a boundary enforcement mechanism that can enforce **deny/permit/route** decisions based on the above inputs. Typical implementation pattern:
  - **ZTNA/SDP or service-to-service gateway** (for internal and connected-system flows)
  - **Next-generation firewall / packet-filtering** at network boundaries
  - **API gateway / ingress controller** for application/API entry points
  - **DNS enforcement** (allowlist resolvers; block unauthorized resolvers)
  - **Internal web proxy enforcement** (block direct outbound HTTP(S) except to the proxy)
- Configure rules so that:
  - **Only approved source→destination pairs** are permitted per label and service.
  - **Inbound write acceptance** from another domain is allowed only if the sender identity and label are authorized for write (i.e., implement the “verifying write permissions before accepting information from another security/privacy domain” requirement).
  - **Spoofed internal traffic** is blocked by default (e.g., drop traffic claiming internal origin via network segmentation rules and enforce identity at the access broker).
  - **Export-controlled flows**:
    - deny cleartext Internet egress for export-controlled labels by default
    - allow only approved egress destinations/paths and require approved transport protections (e.g., TLS) consistent with the organization’s export handling rules.
  - **Web traffic**:
    - enforce that web requests can only egress via the internal web proxy
    - ensure proxy routing rules are label-aware (export-controlled and other restricted labels follow the approved proxy/egress paths).

### 3) Apply the same enforcement to control-plane traffic
- Add explicit flow rules for:
  - DNS: restrict queries to approved resolvers; block direct resolver access
  - routing-adjacent/management services: restrict to approved management paths and destinations
- Ensure these rules are evaluated by the same policy enforcement mechanism (or by tightly coupled enforcement components) so control-plane traffic cannot bypass data-plane policy.

### 4) Implement label regrading/label reassignment at designated enforcement points (if required by ac-04_odp)
- Where the organization’s ac-04_odp requires regrading/reassignment:
  - implement a controlled regrading step at the designated policy enforcement point (e.g., gateway/DLP integration) that changes labels only according to approved rules
  - ensure the regraded label is what downstream enforcement uses
  - log the original label, regrading decision, and resulting label

### 5) Logging and audit evidence
- For every enforcement decision (permit/deny/route), generate audit logs including:
  - timestamp, boundary/enforcement point identifier
  - source/destination identities and zones/domains
  - protocol/service and direction
  - input label(s) and any regraded label(s)
  - action taken (permit/deny/route-to-proxy/route-to-guard)
  - reason code tied to the approved policy rule
- Send logs to the enterprise SIEM and retain per logging requirements.

## WHO
- **Security Architecture Lead**: owns boundary mapping, policy decision model, and rule design aligned to ac-04_odp.
- **Network/Security Engineering**: implements firewall/proxy/DNS enforcement and gateway policies.
- **Application/API Engineering**: implements ingress/API gateway enforcement and label propagation.
- **Data Protection/DLP Owner**: provides label sources and export-controlled handling indicators.
- **SOC/SIEM Engineer**: ensures logs are centralized, searchable, and retained.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. **Cross-domain enforcement coverage**: All defined boundary points (internal zone boundaries, connected-system boundaries, and egress boundaries) enforce approved permit/deny/route decisions based on ac-04_odp.
2. **Write-permission checks**: For inbound write flows from other domains/connected systems, unauthorized senders or unauthorized labels are rejected (documented test results).
3. **Web proxy restriction**: Direct outbound HTTP(S) bypass to the Internet is blocked; all web traffic for the scoped apps goes through the internal web proxy (packet/proxy logs).
4. **Export-controlled handling**: For export-controlled labeled data, cleartext Internet egress is denied; only approved destinations/paths are permitted (test cases + logs).
5. **Control-plane enforcement**: DNS and routing-adjacent management traffic follow the same policy enforcement (attempted bypass is blocked; logs show enforcement).
6. **Label-based routing/regrading**: When regrading is required by ac-04_odp, the label change occurs only at the designated enforcement point and downstream decisions use the resulting label (logs show original→new label).
7. **Auditability**: For at least one permitted and one denied attempt per boundary type, SIEM evidence exists showing the rule reason code and enforcement action.


### ac-4.1 — Object Security and Privacy Attributes (Enhancement)

#### Attribute-based flow enforcement using security+privacy object labels

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement an information-flow enforcement mechanism that uses **organization-defined security attributes** and **privacy attributes** associated with **information objects**, **source objects**, and **destination objects** to make **allow/deny** decisions. When a flow is not explicitly allowed by the combined security and privacy policy, the mechanism must **deny the flow and generate an alert**, using the defined information flow control policies as the basis for decisions.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-4(1)** enhancement requires: **“Use {{ac-4.1_prm_1}} associated with {{ac-4.1_prm_2}} to enforce {{ac-04.01_odp.09}} as a basis for flow control decisions.”**
This means enforcement must be driven by **security + privacy attributes** on the relevant objects (information, source, destination), not only by identity/role.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define the attribute schema and the decision rule (policy-as-code ready)
- Create/confirm the organization-defined attribute values for:
  - **Security attributes** (param **ac-4.1_prm_1**, incl. classification, releasability, owning enclave) per **ac-04.01_odp.01**.
  - **Privacy attributes** (param **ac-4.1_prm_1**, incl. PII type, processing, sharing) per **ac-04.01_odp.02**.
- Define the **information flow control policies** decision logic per **ac-04.01_odp.09** (e.g., allow only when **(source/destination security classification permits)** AND **(privacy sharing rules permit)**; otherwise deny and alert).

### 2) Establish authoritative tagging for the three object types
- **Information objects** (param **ac-04.01_odp.03** and **ac-04.01_odp.04**): ensure datasets/records/messages/files are tagged with both security and privacy attributes.
- **Source objects** (param **ac-04.01_odp.05** and **ac-04.01_odp.06**): ensure hosts/sensors/controllers/applications are tagged with security and privacy attributes.
- **Destination objects** (param **ac-04.01_odp.07** and **ac-04.01_odp.08**): ensure hosts/services/data stores/OT historians are tagged with security and privacy attributes.

Implementation approach (practical):
- Use existing enterprise sources of truth where possible:
  - CMDB/workload inventory for source/destination object labels.
  - Data classification/DLP or metadata-at-creation for information object labels.
- Create a single “attribute registry” (e.g., policy repository + label catalog) that maps object identifiers to their security/privacy attributes.

### 3) Implement an enforcement point that evaluates attributes per attempted flow
- Deploy an attribute-aware **Policy Decision Point (PDP)** and integrate it with the enforcement points that can block flows (e.g., network/edge gateway, API gateway, ZTNA/SASE policy enforcement, or application-level mediation where network visibility is limited).
- For each attempted flow, the PDP must:
  1. Retrieve the **security attributes** for the **information object**, **source object**, and **destination object**.
  2. Retrieve the **privacy attributes** for the same objects.
  3. Apply the decision rule from **ac-04.01_odp.09**:
     - **Allow** only if both security and privacy conditions permit.
     - **Deny** otherwise.

### 4) Deny + alert + record decision evidence
- On deny:
  - Block the flow.
  - Generate an alert/event that includes at minimum: source object, destination object, information object identifier (or label), the relevant security/privacy attributes, and the policy decision reason (security mismatch vs privacy sharing mismatch).
- Ensure denied-flow events are forwarded to SIEM/logging for auditability.

### 5) Operationalize label propagation and drift control
- Ensure that when information objects move between systems (e.g., dataset replication, message routing, API calls), the enforcement mechanism uses the correct label set (either carried as metadata or re-resolved from the attribute registry).
- Add a periodic reconciliation job to detect label drift (objects missing required attributes) and prevent “unknown label” bypass by defaulting to deny with alert.

## WHO
- **Security Architect / Compliance Engineer**: define attribute schema and the allow/deny decision rule aligned to **ac-04.01_odp.09**.
- **Data Governance / Privacy Officer**: validate privacy attribute definitions and sharing restrictions.
- **Platform/Network Security Engineer**: implement PDP + enforcement integration and deny/alert behavior.
- **Application Owner / Engineering Lead**: ensure information objects are labeled at creation/ingestion and that identifiers/metadata are available to the enforcement mechanism.
- **SIEM/Monitoring Engineer**: ensure denied-flow events are normalized and alertable.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. **Policy-driven allow/deny**: For at least one configured scenario, a flow is **allowed only when** both security and privacy attribute conditions permit; otherwise it is **denied**.
2. **Correct attribute inputs**: The enforcement decision uses attributes associated with the **information object**, **source object**, and **destination object** (per **ac-4.1_prm_2**).
3. **Deny + alert**: For denied flows, the system blocks the flow and generates an alert/event containing the decision reason (security vs privacy sharing).
4. **Audit evidence**: Denied-flow events are recorded and retrievable in centralized logs/SIEM with the attributes involved.
5. **No bypass on missing labels**: If required attributes are missing/unknown for any object involved, the system denies the flow and alerts (no silent allow).

### ac-4.2 — Processing Domains (Enhancement)

#### Enforce protected processing domains via domain+type policy engine

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement **protected processing domains** and enforce **domain and type enforcement** so that all inter-process/inter-service flow-control decisions are made using the organization’s **information flow control policies** (ac-04.02_odp) as the basis for allowing/denying:
- information access between domains and to/from information objects
- allowed inter-domain signalling (events/messages/callbacks)
- allowed process transitions (workflow/job handoffs)

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-4(2)** requires: “Use protected processing domains to enforce {{ param, ac-04.02_odp }} as a basis for flow control decisions.” The guidance specifies that protected domains can be provided via **domain and type enforcement**, where:
- processes are assigned to domains
- information is identified by types
- flows are controlled based on allowed information accesses, allowed signalling among domains, and allowed process transitions.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define the protected domain model (policy source of truth)
- Create a domain taxonomy aligned to your architecture boundaries (e.g., `HR-Apps`, `Finance-Apps`, `Public-API`, `Partner-Integration`, `Admin-Functions`).
- Define information types aligned to your enterprise data classification (e.g., `PII`, `Confidential-Finance`, `Operational-Metrics`).
- Encode the organization’s **information flow control policies** (ac-04.02_odp) as **policy-as-code** rules that specify allowed/denied:
  - source-domain → destination-domain/object for each information type and action
  - allowed signalling between domains
  - allowed process transitions between domains/states

### 2) Assign processes to domains (runtime domain identity)
- Ensure every service/process/workload has an explicit domain identity, for example:
  - Kubernetes: map namespaces/workloads to domains (namespace labels/annotations)
  - VM/bare metal: map service accounts to domains in a configuration registry
  - SaaS/multi-tenant apps: map tenant/workspace to domains via configuration
- Enforce that the application/platform can reliably determine the **source domain** for each request/job/event.

### 3) Identify and label information by type (data/object tagging)
- Enforce information-type tagging at creation/ingest time for:
  - files/objects (metadata)
  - message headers (e.g., event/message attributes)
  - database rows/records (row-level metadata or equivalent)
- Ensure the type label propagates through transformations and is included in any subsequent flow decision input.

### 4) Centralize flow-control decisions in a policy decision point (PDP)
- Route all inter-domain flows through a PDP (or an embedded policy enforcement library backed by the same policy rules).
- For each flow decision, the PDP must evaluate at minimum:
  - **source domain**
  - **destination domain/object**
  - **information type**
  - **action** (read/write/execute, signalling type, workflow transition)
- If policy permits: allow; if not: deny and fail closed.

### 5) Enforce signalling and process transitions as policy actions
- Treat inter-domain signalling mechanisms (events, message bus publishes, callbacks, notifications) as explicit actions requiring authorization.
- Treat workflow/job handoffs (e.g., “processing stage A → stage B”, “queue consumer domain change”) as explicit process transitions requiring authorization.

### 6) Produce audit evidence for allow/deny decisions
- Emit structured audit logs for each decision including:
  - decision (allow/deny)
  - rule/policy identifier
  - source domain, destination domain/object
  - information type
  - action
  - correlation/request IDs
- Integrate logs with the enterprise SIEM/monitoring platform.

### 7) Validate with automated tests and CI gates
- Add unit/integration tests that cover representative allow and deny cases from ac-04.02_odp.
- Add CI checks that block deployment if policy compilation/linting fails or if required test cases do not pass.

## WHO
- **Application Security Architect / Security Engineering Lead**: defines domain taxonomy, information types, and policy rule structure.
- **Platform/DevOps Engineer**: implements runtime domain identity mapping and PDP integration.
- **Application Developers**: ensure data/object tagging and that all inter-domain flows call the PDP.
- **Security Operations / SIEM Engineer**: validates audit log schema, retention, and SIEM ingestion.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. For each configured protected domain, processes/workloads can be mapped to a domain identity at runtime.
2. Information objects/messages/records are tagged with an information type at creation/ingest time and the tag is available to the PDP during flow decisions.
3. All inter-domain signalling and process transitions are mediated by the PDP and are denied when not explicitly allowed by the ac-04.02_odp policy rules.
4. Audit logs are generated for every allow/deny decision and include: decision, rule/policy ID, source domain, destination domain/object, information type, and action.
5. Automated tests demonstrate at least one representative **allowed** and **denied** flow per major domain pair and per major action category (access, signalling, transition) defined in ac-04.02_odp.


### ac-4.3 — Dynamic Information Flow Control (Enhancement)

#### Dynamic info-flow gate with mission/risk checks and SOC alerts

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement an enforcement mechanism for **dynamic information flow control** that **allows dynamic flows only when mission-critical conditions are met and the latest threat/risk assessment permits it**; otherwise it **denies the flow and immediately alerts the SOC**, while recording auditable decision evidence.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-4(3) Enhancement: Dynamic Information Flow Control** requires enforcing the organization-defined policy in **param: ac-04.03_odp**. The guidance states that changing conditions (e.g., **risk tolerance changes due to mission/business immediacy**, **threat environment changes**, and **detection of potentially harmful/adverse events**) must cause dynamic flows to be allowed or disallowed accordingly.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define the enforceable decision rule (policy-as-code)
- Create a machine-enforceable policy that implements the organization parameter:
  - **Allow dynamic flows only when**:
    1) **Mission-critical conditions are met** (e.g., emergency/change-window flag, incident response mode, approved mission state), AND
    2) **Latest threat/risk assessment permits** the flow for the relevant scope (app/service/zone/data class).
  - **Otherwise deny and alert SOC**.
- Include explicit **reason categories** for audit/SOC triage, at minimum:
  - `MISSION_CRITICAL_NOT_MET`
  - `RISK_NOT_PERMITTED`
  - (Optional) `ADVERSE_EVENT_DETECTED`

### 2) Implement a runtime enforcement point (PEP) for dynamic flows
- Place enforcement where dynamic flows can be mediated at runtime (choose one based on your stack):
  - **API gateway / service mesh** (for application-to-application dynamic calls), and/or
  - **ZTNA / micro-segmentation / next-gen firewall policy hooks** (for network-mediated dynamic sessions).
- The PEP must evaluate the policy **at connection/session establishment** and **revalidate** at a short interval or on relevant state-change events so that mid-session condition changes can trigger denial.

### 3) Integrate two live inputs into the policy decision (PDP)
- **Mission-critical state input**:
  - Source from an authoritative system (e.g., incident management/emergency mode service, change-window service, or a signed “mission state” event feed).
  - Ensure events are **time-bounded** and include an identifier (e.g., `mission_state_id`) and timestamp.
- **Latest threat/risk assessment input**:
  - Source from your risk/exposure engine output (or a curated “current risk posture” service).
  - Ensure the policy consumes an **assessment version/ID** (e.g., `risk_assessment_id`) and timestamp so the decision can prove it used the latest permitted assessment.

### 4) Detect potentially harmful/adverse events that require denial
- Configure the enforcement pipeline to treat specific adverse signals as a denial trigger (examples):
  - detection of anomalous behavior indicating elevated threat,
  - security event types mapped to “deny dynamic flows” (via SIEM/SOAR event feed or internal detection service).
- When such an event is detected, the PEP must deny dynamic flows and set reason category `ADVERSE_EVENT_DETECTED` (or map to one of the required categories).

### 5) SOC alerting on denied dynamic flows
- On every denied dynamic flow, emit an immediate, structured alert to the SOC/SIEM/SOAR with at least:
  - source/destination identifiers (service/app/host),
  - flow/session identifier,
  - decision outcome (`ALLOW`/`DENY`),
  - reason category,
  - `mission_state_id` and `risk_assessment_id` used,
  - timestamps.

### 6) Auditable enforcement decision records
- Log every enforcement decision (allow/deny) with:
  - the **inputs** used (mission-critical state ID and risk assessment ID/version),
  - the **policy version** (policy-as-code commit/version),
  - the **final decision** and reason category.
- Ensure logs are retained per enterprise logging requirements and are tamper-evident (e.g., append-only/WORM where feasible).

### 7) Documentation and governance artifacts
- Update system security documentation to describe:
  - the dynamic flow decision logic (policy rule),
  - where enforcement occurs (PEP location),
  - how mission-critical state and latest threat/risk assessment are obtained,
  - how SOC alerts are generated,
  - how runtime revalidation/condition changes are handled.

## WHO
- **Security Architect / Compliance Engineer**: define the enforceable decision rule and reason categories; ensure alignment to **ac-04.03_odp**.
- **Platform/Network/Integration Engineer**: implement PEP/PDP integration, runtime revalidation, and SIEM/SOAR alert wiring.
- **SOC Lead / Detection Engineer**: validate alert content, reason taxonomy, and adverse-event mappings.
- **Change Manager / App Owner**: ensure policy updates follow change control and are deployed to production.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. **Policy enforcement**: For a test dynamic flow request, when **mission-critical conditions are not met**, the system **denies** the flow and generates a SOC alert with reason `MISSION_CRITICAL_NOT_MET`.
2. **Risk gating**: When mission-critical conditions are met but the **latest threat/risk assessment does not permit**, the system **denies** and alerts with reason `RISK_NOT_PERMITTED`.
3. **Latest assessment evidence**: Each decision record includes the **risk assessment ID/version** and timestamp proving the decision used the latest assessment.
4. **Runtime condition change**: If mission/risk conditions change during an active session (or at revalidation), the system transitions to denial behavior per the configured revalidation mechanism.
5. **Adverse event handling**: When an adverse event mapped to denial is triggered, dynamic flows are denied and logged with `ADVERSE_EVENT_DETECTED` (or mapped to the required reason categories).
6. **Auditability**: Every allow/deny decision is recorded with policy version, mission state ID, risk assessment ID, decision outcome, and reason category; logs are tamper-evident and retained.
7. **SOC alert fidelity**: Denied-flow alerts arrive in SIEM/SOAR within the defined operational latency target (set by your SOC runbook) and contain actionable fields listed above.

### ac-4.4 — Flow Control of Encrypted Information (Enhancement)

#### TLS inspection gateway to block/terminate encrypted bypass attempts

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement encrypted-traffic (TLS) inspection at defined network choke points so encrypted/encoded information cannot bypass the organization’s information flow control mechanisms. Configure the inspection gateway to (1) inspect/decrypt TLS traffic, (2) apply content inspection security policy filters and data type identifiers (including handling for encoded data not recognized by filters), (3) block non-compliant encrypted traffic, (4) terminate communications sessions attempting to pass encrypted information, and (5) alert the SOC with auditable events.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 AC-4(4) (Enhancement) requires: “Prevent encrypted information from bypassing {{ ac-04.04_odp.01 }} by {{ ac-04.04_odp.02 }}.”
- {{ ac-04.04_odp.01 }} = “information flow control mechanisms” (content inspection security policy filters and data type identifiers)
- {{ ac-04.04_odp.02 }} = “blocking the flow of the encrypted information, terminating communications sessions attempting to pass encrypted information”
Control guidance further states encryption is extended to encoded data not recognized by filtering mechanisms—so the enforcement must treat such traffic as subject to inspection and policy enforcement.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Select inspection choke points
- Place approved TLS inspection gateways at egress points where encrypted traffic could otherwise evade content inspection (e.g., internet egress, inter-VPC egress, or SaaS access paths), using a hub-and-spoke or equivalent architecture.

### 2) Define inspection scope and policy mapping
- Configure the gateway to inspect TLS 1.2/1.3 sessions for the relevant ports/protocols.
- Map gateway inspection results to the organization’s **content inspection security policy filters** and **data type identifiers**.
- Add explicit handling for “encoded but unrecognized” payloads: treat unknown/unsupported encodings as inspectable and apply conservative enforcement (deny/block and terminate) rather than allowing pass-through.

### 3) Enforce allow-listed destinations
- Configure the gateway to connect only to explicitly approved destinations (allow-list by FQDN/IP ranges and service categories as defined by the organization).
- Ensure non-allow-listed destinations are blocked or only permitted if explicitly authorized by policy (default-deny posture for bypass-prone paths).

### 4) Implement block + terminate behavior
- Configure enforcement actions so that when policy indicates encrypted traffic would bypass the filters/data-type identifiers, the gateway:
  - **blocks the flow** (prevents forwarding), and
  - **terminates the communications session** (actively closes/ends the TLS/TCP session rather than only logging).

### 5) SOC alerting and audit evidence
- Integrate the gateway with the SIEM/SOC tooling to generate structured alerts/events for:
  - blocked encrypted sessions,
  - terminated sessions,
  - policy ID / rule ID that triggered enforcement,
  - destination (allow-list status),
  - reason codes (e.g., “encoded not recognized,” “data type mismatch,” “filter bypass attempt”).
- Ensure events are retained per enterprise logging/retention requirements.

### 6) Operationalize with change control and validation
- Manage gateway inspection policies as version-controlled configuration (policy-as-code where available).
- Require peer review/approval for policy changes.
- Run validation tests in a staging environment to confirm:
  - compliant TLS traffic is allowed to allow-listed destinations,
  - non-compliant encrypted/encoded traffic is blocked and sessions are terminated,
  - SOC alerts are generated with correct metadata.

## WHO
- **Network/Security Engineering Lead**: designs placement and gateway configuration.
- **Security Policy Owner (AppSec/InfoSec)**: defines/approves content inspection filters and data type identifiers and the “encoded but unrecognized” enforcement stance.
- **SOC/SIEM Engineer**: implements alert routing, event schema, and detections.
- **Change Management/Configuration Manager**: ensures controlled rollout and evidence collection.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. For encrypted TLS sessions that would bypass content inspection filters/data-type identifiers (including encoded payloads not recognized by filters), the gateway **blocks** the flow.
2. For those same sessions, the gateway **terminates** the communications session (no successful continuation after enforcement).
3. The gateway is configured with **allow-listed destinations**; attempts to reach non-allow-listed destinations are blocked/terminated per policy.
4. SOC/SIEM receives an alert/event for each enforcement action, containing at minimum: timestamp, destination, enforcement reason code, and policy/rule identifier.
5. Staging test results demonstrate correct behavior for at least: (a) compliant encrypted traffic, (b) encrypted traffic with disallowed destination, (c) encrypted traffic with encoded/unrecognized payload triggering conservative enforcement.


### ac-4.5 — Embedded Data Types (Enhancement)

#### Enforce max-2 embedded content depth with uninspectable block

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement an enforcement mechanism that limits embedded data types within other data types to **maximum 2 nesting levels** and **blocks** any embedded content that the organization’s inspection tools cannot unpack/analyze.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-4(5)** enhancement requires: “**Enforce {{ param, ac-04.05_odp }} on embedding data types within other data types.**” The organization-defined parameter **ac-04.05_odp (limitations)** states: **limit embedding to a maximum of 2 levels; prohibit any embedded data type beyond inspection tool capability.** The guidance notes that embedding can reduce flow control effectiveness and that limitations should consider **levels of embedding** and **prohibit levels beyond inspection tool capability**.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Create the authoritative limitations list (input to enforcement)
- Maintain a version-controlled “**Embedding Limitations List**” that includes:
  - Allowed container/file types for inspection (e.g., archive formats, document containers) that the inspection service can reliably unpack.
  - Explicitly **blocked/uninspectable embedded types** (formats/containers the tooling cannot unpack/analyze).
  - The rule: **max embedding depth = 2 levels**.
- Ensure the list is referenced by the enforcement component (not just documentation).

### 2) Implement a recursive inspection gate with hard stops
- Add/enable a centralized “**Content Inspection Gate**” in the application’s file/content ingestion and release paths (e.g., upload endpoints, API file ingestion, email/file transfer integration, export/release workflows).
- The gate must:
  - Recursively parse/unpack embedded objects.
  - Track embedding depth and **stop at depth > 2**.
  - If depth would exceed 2, return **BLOCK**.
  - If an embedded object is of a type not supported by the inspection tooling (per the limitations list), return **BLOCK** with reason “uninspectable embedded type.”

### 3) Integrate enforcement into the actual data flow
- Enforce at points where content can enter or leave the system:
  - Inbound: before storing, indexing, or forwarding uploaded content.
  - Outbound: before releasing content to users/partners or before exporting bundles.
- If the application supports multiple services (web/API/microservices), ensure all relevant upload/export paths call the same gate (or a shared library/service) to avoid bypass.

### 4) Make decisions auditable
- For every inspection decision, write an audit event including:
  - Content identifier (hash/ID), source/destination context, user/service account.
  - Detected embedding depth.
  - Embedded type(s) encountered.
  - Decision: **ALLOW** or **BLOCK**.
  - Rule version of the limitations list.
  - Reason code (e.g., “depth_exceeded_max_2” or “uninspectable_embedded_type”).
- Forward audit events to the enterprise SIEM/logging platform.

### 5) Operationalize with change control
- Treat the limitations list and inspection rules as configuration with:
  - Versioning, approval workflow, and rollback.
  - Periodic review to reflect inspection tooling capability changes.

## WHO
- **Security Engineering / AppSec**: define the limitations list and implement the inspection gate integration.
- **Application Owner**: ensure all ingestion/release paths route through the gate.
- **Cybersecurity Operations / SOC**: monitor audit events and handle incidents for repeated blocked attempts.
- **Compliance/GRC**: validate evidence (limitations list, rule versioning, audit logs) for AC-4(5).

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. **Max depth enforcement**: Any content containing embedded data types that would result in **embedding depth > 2** is **blocked** and logged with reason “depth_exceeded_max_2”.
2. **Tool capability prohibition**: Any embedded object whose type is listed as **uninspectable** (or cannot be unpacked/analyzed by the inspection tooling) is **blocked** and logged with reason “uninspectable_embedded_type”.
3. **Coverage**: All configured inbound upload and outbound release/export paths for the application invoke the inspection gate (no bypass routes).
4. **Auditability**: For each allow/block decision, the system produces an audit log entry containing depth, embedded type(s), decision, and limitations list rule version.
5. **Evidence readiness**: A reviewer can retrieve the limitations list version and corresponding audit logs demonstrating enforcement for test cases.

### ac-4.6 — Metadata (Enhancement)

#### Cryptographically bound metadata tags for flow control decisions

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 82%

## WHAT
Implement metadata-driven information flow control where the metadata tags used for allow/block decisions are (1) **authoritative** (stored in an access-controlled metadata store), (2) **integrity-protected** (cryptographically bound to the data payload), and (3) **verified at enforcement points** before any routing/release.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-4(6) Metadata (Enhancement)** requires: **“Enforce information flow control based on {{ insert: param, ac-04.06_odp }}.”** The organization-defined parameter **ac-04.06_odp = metadata** specifies **“metadata tags bound to data payload using cryptographic integrity checks and access-controlled metadata stores.”**

The guidance further requires considering: **trustworthiness of metadata**, **data integrity (protect against unauthorized changes to metadata tags)**, and **binding of metadata to the data payload using sufficiently strong binding techniques with appropriate assurance**.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define the authoritative metadata tags and flow rules
- Create a tag taxonomy (e.g., `data_classification`, `regulatory_jurisdiction`, `tenant_id`, `retention_class`, `allowed_destinations_group`).
- Define which tags are **authoritative for flow decisions** (the ones used by the policy engine).
- Define allow/block rules mapping tag values to destinations/actions (e.g., “if `data_classification=Confidential` then allow only to destinations in group X”).

### 2) Implement an access-controlled metadata store
- Store:
  - tag definitions (schema, allowed values)
  - policy mappings (tag-value → allowed destinations/actions)
- Enforce least privilege:
  - only a small set of services/roles can write/update tag definitions and policy mappings
  - enforcement services can read the authoritative policy mappings
- Ensure the store is protected with strong access controls and is auditable.

### 3) Cryptographically bind metadata tags to each payload (“security envelope”)
- For each data item that can be routed/released, generate an envelope that includes:
  - the **payload identifier** (stable ID)
  - the **metadata tag values** used for flow decisions
  - a **payload hash** (or include payload bytes in the integrity computation)
  - envelope version and algorithm identifiers
  - an **integrity protection** value (e.g., digital signature or MAC) computed over **both** payload hash and metadata tag values
- Use KMS/HSM-backed keys with rotation.
- Ensure the envelope is created/updated only by trusted components (not by untrusted clients).

### 4) Verify metadata integrity at enforcement points
- At every enforcement point (API gateway, service-to-service middleware, secure file transfer/release service, messaging publish/consume boundary):
  - parse the envelope
  - verify the cryptographic integrity check
  - if verification fails: **deny the flow** (or restrict to a quarantine path) and record an integrity-failure event
  - if verification succeeds: use the **verified tag values** to evaluate the allow/block policy
- Do not allow enforcement decisions based on unverified metadata.

### 5) Enforce policy using the verified tags
- Evaluate allow/block rules using the authoritative metadata store’s policy mappings.
- Ensure the decision is deterministic and based only on verified tag values.

### 6) Audit evidence generation
Log at minimum:
- data/payload identifier
- verified metadata tag values used for the decision
- destination/action requested
- enforcement decision (allow/deny/quarantine)
- integrity verification result (success/failure) and failure reason category
- actor/service identity that initiated the request

### 7) Operational failure handling
- If integrity verification fails:
  - deny/restrict the flow
  - route to an incident/quarantine workflow
  - alert on repeated failures

## WHO
- **Application Security Engineer / Backend Engineer**: implement envelope creation, verification, and enforcement integration.
- **Security Architect**: define tag taxonomy, authoritative tags, and policy mappings.
- **Platform/Cloud Security Engineer**: implement KMS/HSM key management and access-controlled metadata store permissions.
- **SOC / Monitoring Engineer**: configure audit log pipelines and alerting for integrity failures.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. **Binding**: For a test payload, the system produces an envelope where the integrity check covers both payload hash and metadata tag values.
2. **Tamper detection**: If an attacker modifies metadata tag values in transit/storage, enforcement verification fails and the flow is denied/quarantined.
3. **Policy correctness**: For known tag-value combinations, enforcement decisions match the policy mappings stored in the access-controlled metadata store.
4. **No unverified decisions**: Enforcement points reject requests where metadata integrity verification is not performed or fails.
5. **Auditability**: Audit logs include payload ID, verified tag values, destination/action, decision outcome, and integrity verification result.
6. **Access control**: Only authorized roles/services can update tag definitions and policy mappings in the metadata store; enforcement services can read but not write.


### ac-4.7 — One-way Flow Mechanisms (Enhancement)

#### Deploy and operate a hardware data diode for one-way domain flows

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Months
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement a **hardware-based one-way information flow mechanism** (e.g., data diode / unidirectional security gateway) at the boundary between the **higher-impact/classified** domain and the **lower-impact/unclassified** domain, enforcing the **permitted direction** and preventing reverse export.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-4(7)** (Enhancement) requires: **“Enforce one-way information flows through hardware-based flow control mechanisms.”** The guidance notes these mechanisms (data diode / unidirectional gateway) are used to **prevent data export from higher-impact/classified domains** while permitting import from lower-impact/unclassified domains.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define permitted one-way flows (policy + scope)**
   - Document which direction is permitted (e.g., **Lower → Higher**) and which data/protocols/ports are allowed.
   - Explicitly state that **reverse direction (Higher → Lower)** is prohibited for the defined data exchange.
   - Include scope boundaries: environments (prod/test/DR), systems, and interfaces covered by the diode.

2. **Architect the placement so the diode is the only path for the exchange**
   - Update network/security architecture diagrams to show the diode as the **only** permitted cross-domain path for the relevant traffic.
   - Ensure there is **no alternate routing/overlay/VPN/tunnel** path that could bypass the diode (e.g., no shared VLAN peering, no overlay networks, no alternate gateways).

3. **Procure and configure the hardware one-way mechanism**
   - Select a diode/unidirectional gateway that supports the required **L3/L4** characteristics (and any required constrained L7 behavior, if applicable).
   - Configure directionality using the device’s supported modes (e.g., **TX-only/RX-only/unidirectional**).
   - Disable or physically/firmware-block any reverse-direction interfaces/features on the device.

4. **Enforce and validate adjacent controls to prevent bypass**
   - Apply network ACLs/security groups and routing controls so only the diode interfaces can carry the permitted cross-domain traffic.
   - Confirm that firewall rules and routing do not create an alternate path for Higher → Lower traffic.

5. **Enable audit-capable logging from the diode**
   - Configure the diode/unidirectional gateway to export logs (e.g., syslog) to the enterprise SIEM/logging platform.
   - Define retention and log fields needed to demonstrate:
     - **Allowed direction succeeds** (Lower → Higher)
     - **Prohibited direction is blocked** (Higher → Lower)

6. **Run controlled verification tests and capture evidence**
   - Perform a test from **Higher → Lower** using a harmless payload matching the prohibited exchange criteria; verify it is **blocked** and capture diode logs.
   - Perform a test from **Lower → Higher** using a permitted payload; verify it **succeeds** and capture diode logs.
   - Record test procedure, results, timestamps, and device configuration identifiers.

7. **Operationalize with procedures and change control**
   - Create/maintain procedures for:
     - diode health checks
     - handling link failures
     - performing controlled changes (with security sign-off)
   - Store device configuration exports, serial numbers, interface mappings, and approved change records in configuration management.

## WHO
- **Network/Security Architect**: defines permitted direction and designs placement to prevent bypass.
- **Network Engineer / OT-IT Boundary Engineer (as applicable)**: configures the diode and adjacent routing/ACL controls.
- **Security Operations (SOC) / SIEM Engineer**: configures log forwarding, verifies log ingestion, and ensures retention.
- **System Owner / CISO-designated approver**: approves policy scope and test results.

## Acceptance criteria (audit-ready)
1. A hardware one-way mechanism (data diode/unidirectional gateway) is installed between the specified higher-impact and lower-impact domains.
2. The diode is configured for the documented permitted direction (e.g., Lower → Higher) and reverse direction is blocked by hardware configuration.
3. Architecture/routing evidence shows the diode is the **only** cross-domain path for the defined exchange (no bypass routes).
4. Diode logs in SIEM demonstrate:
   - permitted-direction transfers succeed
   - prohibited-direction attempts are blocked
5. Device configuration (including directionality settings), serial/interface mapping, and test evidence are stored in configuration management and retained per the security plan.
6. Operational procedures exist for ongoing enforcement and are followed during change management.

### ac-4.8 — Security and Privacy Policy Filters (Enhancement)

#### Inline security+privacy policy filters at covered egress and zone crossings

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement **organization-defined security and privacy policy filters** as an **inline decision point** for the specific **information flows** covered by AC-4(8):
1) **Network egress** from internal enclaves to external endpoints/cloud-adjacent services.
2) **Cross-domain transfers** between **Secret and Confidential** on-prem zones.

For each covered transfer, the system must evaluate **both**:
- **Security policy filters** (e.g., file type/size limits, structured field/range checks, prohibited content patterns).
- **Privacy policy filter** (e.g., PII detection/minimisation patterns).

If a filter fails or the data does not meet the rules, the transfer must be **quarantined** according to the organization-defined actions, and **time-bound notifications** must be sent.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-4(8) enhancement guidance requires that **approved security or privacy policy filters** be used to address **data structures and content** and that organizations define **information flows** for which enforcement is applied. It further specifies enforcement outcomes (e.g., quarantine/block/deny) and **notifications within 1 hour** when filter processing fails.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define the enforcement points so no covered flow bypasses inspection
- Route all covered egress and cross-zone transfers through an inspection gateway/service (e.g., secure web gateway + CASB inline, ZTNA broker with inline inspection, or a dedicated data transfer proxy).
- Enforce routing at the network/app layer so that direct-to-cloud/partner paths are blocked or redirected to the inspection point.

### 2) Implement the organization-approved filter sets (policy-as-code)
Create two filter engines (or one engine with two rule sets) configured from the organization-defined parameters:
- **Security policy filter** rules (ac-04.08_odp.01 / ac-4.8_prm_1 / ac-4.8_prm_4):
  - File type allow/deny lists and **maximum file length/size**.
  - Structured data validation: **maximum field sizes** and **field range checks** (schema-aware).
  - Prohibited content patterns (keyword/phrase patterns) and hidden content detection where applicable.
- **Privacy policy filter** rules (ac-04.08_odp.02 / ac-4.8_prm_1):
  - PII detection patterns (e.g., HIPAA/GDPR identifiers and sensitive personal data patterns).
  - Privacy minimisation behavior (e.g., block/deny or redaction/quarantine per the organization’s privacy policy).

### 3) Enforce “both pass” decision logic per transfer
- For each transfer attempt, compute:
  - `security_result` (pass/fail)
  - `privacy_result` (pass/fail)
- Allow the transfer **only if** `security_result == pass` **AND** `privacy_result == pass`.

### 4) Implement quarantine/fail-closed behavior on filter processing failure
Use the organization-defined actions:
- **Security filter processing failure** (ac-04.08_odp.06):
  - **Quarantine the data** and **deny the transfer**.
  - Notify **system owner + security operations within 1 hour**.
- **Privacy filter processing failure** (ac-04.08_odp.07):
  - **Quarantine the data** and **block release**.
  - Notify **privacy officer + security operations within 1 hour**.

Engineering requirement: treat “engine unavailable/timeout/parse error” as a **fail-closed** condition that triggers the quarantine/deny/block behavior above (so filter processing failure is not silently bypassed).

### 5) Generate audit evidence tied to decisions
For every covered transfer, log at minimum:
- Flow identifier (which covered flow was attempted)
- Security filter outcome and which rule(s) matched (or failure reason)
- Privacy filter outcome and which PII pattern(s) matched (or failure reason)
- Final enforcement action (allow/deny/quarantine/block release)
- Timestamp(s) sufficient to prove the **1-hour** notification SLA
- Correlation IDs linking gateway decision logs to notification tickets/alerts

### 6) Operationalize notifications
- Integrate gateway events with the organization’s alerting/ticketing/SIEM workflow.
- Implement automated routing to:
  - Security operations and system owner for security failures.
  - Security operations and privacy officer for privacy failures.
- Ensure the notification mechanism can demonstrate delivery within **1 hour** (e.g., alert timestamp + acknowledgement timestamp).

## WHO
- **Security Architect / Data Protection Engineer**: defines filter logic, fail-closed behavior, and evidence requirements.
- **Network/Security Engineering**: implements routing so covered flows traverse inspection.
- **Privacy Officer (or delegated privacy lead)**: validates privacy filter patterns and minimisation behavior.
- **SOC/Security Operations**: receives and triages alerts; ensures SLA adherence.
- **System Owner**: receives security failure notifications and coordinates remediation.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. **Coverage**: All configured covered flows (egress and Secret↔Confidential transfers) traverse the inspection point; attempts to bypass are blocked or redirected.
2. **Both-pass enforcement**: For test transfers, the system allows only when **both** security and privacy filters pass.
3. **Structured validation**: Structured payload tests demonstrate enforcement of file/field size limits and field range checks.
4. **Content/PII detection**: Prohibited keyword and PII pattern test cases trigger the correct enforcement action.
5. **Fail-closed**: When the filter engine is forced into failure (e.g., timeout/unavailable/parse error), the system quarantines and denies/blocks per the organization-defined actions.
6. **1-hour notifications**: For both security and privacy failure scenarios, notifications are generated and recorded with timestamps showing delivery within **1 hour** to the required recipients.
7. **Audit evidence**: Logs include correlation IDs and outcomes sufficient to reconstruct each decision and enforcement action during an audit.

### ac-4.9 — Human Reviews (Enhancement)

#### Human review approval gates for Confidential/Secret flow decisions

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement an enforcement mechanism that **pauses or blocks** information flows/accesses when they match the organization-defined **information flows** and **conditions**, and requires a **human review decision** (approve/deny) before the action can proceed. The mechanism must also **record auditable evidence** linking each decision to the specific flow and trigger condition.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-4(9) Human Reviews (Enhancement)** requires: “**Enforce the use of human reviews for {{ac-04.09_odp.01}} under the following conditions: {{ac-04.09_odp.02}}.**”
- **ac-04.09_odp.01 (information flows):** Data flows involving **cross-domain transfers, external sharing, and access to Confidential or Secret datasets**.
- **ac-04.09_odp.02 (conditions):** When **automated policy enforcement cannot reliably determine authorization** for Confidential/Secret data flows, **or** when required by **ITAR/EAR/CMMC-L2/L3 or HIPAA/GDPR**.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define the enforcement scope (flow catalog)
- Create/maintain an authoritative catalog of the application’s **information flows** that correspond to **ac-04.09_odp.01**, including:
  - Cross-domain transfers (e.g., prod→partner domain, region-to-region, internal→DMZ→external)
  - External sharing (e.g., email/API export to external tenants, S3/object sharing, file download links)
  - Access to **Confidential/Secret** datasets (e.g., database queries, document retrieval, API responses)
- For each flow, define:
  - Source system/service
  - Destination system/service/domain
  - Dataset classification mapping (Confidential/Secret)
  - Enforcement point (where the system can actually allow/deny)

### 2) Implement trigger evaluation (conditions)
Configure the enforcement layer so that it triggers human review when **ac-04.09_odp.02** is true, specifically:
- **Automation cannot reliably determine authorization**:
  - Policy engine returns “unknown/insufficient context”
  - Confidence score below an agreed threshold
  - Missing required attributes (destination, dataset label, purpose, legal basis)
  - Conflicting policy outcomes
- **Regulatory requirement**:
  - Map ITAR/EAR/CMMC-L2/L3 and HIPAA/GDPR requirements to concrete triggers (e.g., dataset tags + destination type + action type such as export/share/download)

### 3) Add a human-review approval gate in the enforcement path
- For each triggered request, the system must **pause/block** the action at the enforcement point and create an approval task.
- The approval task must include at minimum:
  - Flow identifier (from the catalog)
  - Trigger condition(s) (automation uncertainty and/or regulatory basis)
  - Requester identity (user/service account)
  - Proposed action (approve/deny context)
  - Dataset classification (Confidential/Secret)
  - Destination/domain and relevant metadata
  - Business justification provided by requester (if applicable)
- Only after a **named human reviewer** records an **approve/deny** decision should the enforcement point release the action.

### 4) Record and retain auditable evidence
- Log every triggered event and decision with immutable audit fields:
  - Timestamp of trigger
  - Trigger condition(s)
  - Flow identifier
  - Request/transaction ID
  - Reviewer identity and decision
  - Reason/notes for the decision
  - Outcome (released/blocked)
- Forward logs to the enterprise SIEM and retain per organizational logging/retention policy.

### 5) Operationalize reviewer assignment and workflow controls
- Configure workflow routing so only authorized reviewers (role-based) can approve.
- Enforce separation of duties where feasible (e.g., requester cannot be the sole approver for the same flow type).
- Ensure the workflow is integrated with the application’s change/config governance so triggers and flow definitions remain current.

## WHO
- **Application Security Architect / IAM Engineer:** designs enforcement points, trigger logic, and reviewer workflow integration.
- **Data Governance / Privacy Officer:** validates dataset classification mapping and regulatory trigger mapping (ITAR/EAR/CMMC-L2/L3, HIPAA/GDPR).
- **Security Operations / Compliance Analyst:** validates audit evidence fields and retention; monitors for repeated trigger events.
- **System Owner / Engineering Lead:** implements and maintains the integration in the application/enforcement layer.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. For any request matching **ac-04.09_odp.01** (cross-domain/external sharing/Confidential-Secret dataset access) **and** meeting **ac-04.09_odp.02** (automation cannot reliably decide OR regulatory trigger), the system **does not complete the action** until a human reviewer records an approval.
2. For non-triggering requests, the system may proceed automatically (no human review required).
3. Every triggered event produces an auditable record containing: flow ID, trigger condition(s), requester identity, reviewer identity, decision, and final outcome.
4. Audit logs are exported to SIEM and can be used to reconstruct: “who approved what, for which flow, under which trigger, and when.”
5. At least one test case per trigger type demonstrates correct pause/block behavior and correct approval gating.

### ac-4.10 — Enable and Disable Security or Privacy Policy Filters (Enhancement)

#### Privileged, policy-gated enable/disable of security & privacy filters

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement a capability for privileged administrators to enable/disable only the organization-defined **security filters** (DLP, web content filtering, malware/IOC blocking) and **privacy filters** (privacy consent/redaction) **for specific data flows**.

The enable/disable action must be permitted **only when**:
- **Security filters:** the data flow is approved in the **security domain matrix** and the change is within **system authorization**.
- **Privacy filters:** the destination requires privacy controls for personal data **and** the administrator has an **approved privacy impact assessment**.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-4(10)** requires the system to provide the capability for privileged administrators to enable/disable the organization-defined security/privacy policy filters **only under the defined conditions** (AC-4.10_prm_1 and AC-4.10_prm_2).

## HOW (specific steps)
### 1) Define an authoritative, machine-checkable filter toggle policy
- Create a **toggle catalogue** listing the organization-defined filters:
  - Security: **DLP**, **web content filtering**, **malware/IOC blocking** (ac-4.10_prm_1 / ac-04.10_odp.01)
  - Privacy: **privacy consent/redaction** (ac-4.10_prm_1 / ac-04.10_odp.02)
- Create a **security domain matrix** that enumerates approved data flows and allowed security-domain pairs and data classifications.
- Encode the conditions exactly as rules:
  - Security filter toggling allowed **only** for approved data flows involving **Confidential or Secret** data and authorized security domains (ac-4.10_prm_2 / ac-04.10_odp.03).
  - Privacy filter toggling allowed **only** when destination requires privacy controls for personal data and an **approved privacy impact assessment** exists (ac-04.10_odp.04).

### 2) Enforce privileged-only enable/disable via an approval-gated workflow
- Implement an admin-facing workflow (UI/API) where privileged admins submit a request to enable/disable filters for a **specific data-flow identifier**.
- Require the request to include references to:
  - the **approved data-flow entry** in the security domain matrix
  - the **system authorization scope** (the change must be within system authorization)
  - for privacy filters: the **privacy impact assessment ID** that is approved for the destination
- The workflow must validate the request against the machine-checkable toggle policy before any change is applied.

### 3) Apply changes only through controlled enforcement-point integration
- Integrate the workflow with the actual enforcement points that implement the filters (e.g., DLP platform, secure web gateway/CASB, email/web malware/IOC blocking, privacy consent/redaction service).
- Apply changes using least-privilege service accounts and configuration APIs/automation (no ad-hoc manual console changes).
- Ensure the system applies the selected filters **based on data flow type, source/destination security domains, and other security/privacy relevant features** (per guidance).

### 4) Produce audit evidence for every toggle
For each enable/disable action, emit an auditable record to SIEM/log storage containing at minimum:
- admin identity (who)
- data-flow identifier and source/destination security domains
- which filters were enabled/disabled (from the toggle catalogue)
- before/after configuration state
- references to the approved security-domain matrix entry and system authorization scope
- for privacy filters: privacy impact assessment ID
- timestamp and request/approval identifiers

### 5) Add operational guardrails
- Maintain an explicit mapping of “approved data flows” and “approved privacy impact assessments” as authoritative sources of truth.
- Reject any request that does not match the defined conditions (fail closed).

## WHO
- **Security Architect / IAM & Access Control Lead:** defines the security domain matrix rules and toggle catalogue.
- **Privacy Officer / Privacy Governance Lead:** ensures privacy impact assessments are created/approved and referenced.
- **Platform/Integration Engineer:** implements the workflow, policy validation, and enforcement-point integrations.
- **SIEM/Logging Engineer:** ensures audit events are correctly formatted, retained, and monitored.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. **Privileged restriction:** Non-privileged users cannot enable/disable any security/privacy filters; attempts are denied and logged.
2. **Security condition enforcement:** Enabling/disabling security filters succeeds only when the requested data flow is an approved entry in the security domain matrix and the change is within system authorization; otherwise it is rejected.
3. **Privacy condition enforcement:** Enabling/disabling privacy filters succeeds only when the destination requires privacy controls for personal data and a valid approved privacy impact assessment ID is provided; otherwise it is rejected.
4. **Filter selection correctness:** The system only allows enabling/disabling filters that are defined in the organization-defined toggle catalogue and applicable to the selected data flow.
5. **Auditability:** Every successful and failed toggle attempt generates an audit record containing the required fields (admin identity, data-flow ID, filters changed, before/after state, and approval references).
6. **Fail-closed behavior:** If policy validation cannot be completed (e.g., missing/invalid matrix entry or missing/invalid privacy impact assessment), the change is not applied.

### ac-4.11 — Configuration of Security or Privacy Policy Filters (Enhancement)

#### Privileged admin-configurable security/privacy filter sets (audited)

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement a capability for privileged administrators to configure **organization-defined security or privacy policy filter sets** (wordlists, regex rules, and allow/deny lists) and to **select/apply** the appropriate filter set **per system and per data classification**.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-4(11)** (Enhancement) requires: “Provide the capability for privileged administrators to configure {{ param, ac-4.11_prm_1 }} to support different security or privacy policies.” The control guidance further expects documentation and examples for configuring security/privacy policy filters (e.g., inappropriate-word lists checked by policy mechanisms).

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define the filter-set model and inventory
- Create a structured filter-set schema for **ac-4.11_prm_1**: configurable filter sets per **system ID** and **data classification**, containing:
  - **Security filter sets** (ac-04.11_odp.01): malware/exploit/prohibited-content keyword/regex rules.
  - **Privacy filter sets** (ac-04.11_odp.02): PII/PHI keyword/regex blocklists with **allow/deny exceptions**.
- Maintain an inventory/catalog listing:
  - Filter set name/ID, version, category (security/privacy), target system(s), target data classification(s), and last updated timestamp.

### 2) Implement privileged configuration workflow (policy-as-code)
- Provide an admin UI and/or API endpoint that allows only **privileged administrators** to:
  - Create/update filter sets (wordlists/regex/allow-deny rules).
  - Select which filter set version applies to a given **system + data classification**.
- Enforce a controlled workflow:
  - Changes must be made through a versioned process (e.g., pull-request/review) rather than direct edits in production.
  - Require approval by designated security/privacy owners (roles defined by the organization).

### 3) Add automated validation and safety checks
Before a filter set can be activated:
- Validate rule syntax (regex compilation, character encoding, size limits).
- Prevent unsafe regex patterns (e.g., catastrophic backtracking checks / timeouts in a test harness).
- Run deterministic test cases (“known test vectors”) for:
  - Expected matches (e.g., representative prohibited-content terms; representative PII/PHI terms).
  - Expected non-matches (including configured exceptions/allow conditions).
- Produce a validation report artifact stored with the filter set version.

### 4) Deploy/activate filter sets with auditable change records
- When a filter set is activated for a system/classification, record an audit event containing:
  - Admin identity (who), timestamp (when), filter set ID + version (what), target system/classification (where), and change outcome (validation pass/fail).
- Ensure the enforcement mechanism consumes the activated filter set (e.g., via a signed configuration bundle, API pull, or configuration deployment pipeline) so the selected policy is actually applied.

### 5) Provide configuration documentation and examples
- Store documentation alongside the filter-set templates including:
  - How to format wordlists/regex rules.
  - Examples for security filters (malware/exploit/prohibited-content) and privacy filters (PII/PHI with exceptions).
  - How exceptions/allow conditions are represented and tested.

## WHO
- **Privileged security/privacy administrators**: author and approve filter-set changes.
- **Security engineering / platform team**: implement the filter-set schema, validation pipeline, deployment mechanism, and audit logging.
- **System/data classification owners**: approve which filter set applies to which system/classification.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. The system provides a capability for privileged administrators to configure **ac-4.11_prm_1** filter sets (wordlists/regex/allow-deny) and to associate them to **system + data classification**.
2. Security filter sets include configurable malware/exploit/prohibited-content keyword/regex rules; privacy filter sets include configurable PII/PHI blocklists with exceptions.
3. Only privileged roles can create/modify/activate filter sets; non-privileged users cannot change active configurations.
4. Every activation/change produces audit evidence showing: admin identity, filter set ID/version, target system/classification, timestamp, and validation outcome.
5. Validation pipeline blocks activation on invalid/unsafe rules and produces a stored validation report.
6. Documentation and examples for configuring security/privacy policy filters are available and linked to the filter-set templates/catalog.

### ac-4.12 — Data Type Identifiers (Enhancement)

#### Cross-domain data type gateway using signature/token + semantic validation

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 82%

## WHAT
Implement a “Data Type Gateway” (or equivalent enforcement component) at each cross-security-domain information transfer boundary to:
1) identify the data type using **data type identifiers** (file signatures and/or token-based identifiers), and
2) allow the transfer **only after** the content is validated **syntactically and semantically** against the **approved data format specifications**.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-4(12) requires that when transferring information between different security domains, systems use **data type identifiers** (param **ac-04.12_odp**) to validate data essential for information flow decisions. Guidance specifies that identification/validation must be based on defined specifications for each allowed data format, and that **filename and number alone are not used**. Content must be validated syntactically and semantically to ensure it is the proper data type.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define approved data format specifications (per allowed data type)
- Create a versioned catalog of allowed data formats (e.g., “Approved Invoice XML v1.2”, “Approved Customer CSV v3”).
- For each format, define:
  - **Syntactic rules**: schema/structure, required fields, field types, allowed ranges/regex, encoding constraints.
  - **Semantic rules**: business-rule consistency (cross-field constraints, allowed enumerations, referential checks where applicable), and any content meaning checks required for safe information flow decisions.

### 2) Define the approved identifier-to-spec mapping (data type identifiers)
- For each approved data format, define the **allowed identifiers** used for identification:
  - **File signatures** (e.g., magic bytes, canonical header markers, container markers, or hash-of-canonical-header patterns).
  - **Token-based identifiers** (e.g., transfer metadata labels or signed format tokens).
- Support **multiple internal file signatures/tokens** when required by the specification.
- Ensure the mapping is explicit: **identifier(s) → specific approved specification version**.

### 3) Implement the gateway enforcement logic (fail-closed)
At each inbound transfer request to the boundary:
1. **Extract identifiers from the payload** (do not rely on filename/extension/count):
   - Read the payload header/structure to compute/verify file signature(s).
   - Verify token-based identifiers (including issuer/signature checks if tokens are signed).
2. **Map identifiers to an approved specification**:
   - If no mapping exists, reject.
3. **Syntactic validation**:
   - Validate against the specification’s schema/structure rules.
4. **Semantic validation**:
   - Validate content meaning/business rules against the specification.
5. **Decision**:
   - If any step fails (identifier mapping, syntactic, or semantic), **block the transfer**.
   - If all pass, allow the transfer.

### 4) Audit evidence generation
For every transfer attempt (allowed and blocked), emit audit records that include:
- Source security domain and destination security domain
- Data type identifier(s) observed (or token claims) and the mapped specification version
- Results of **identifier validation**, **syntactic validation**, and **semantic validation** (pass/fail)
- Reason codes tied to the specific validation failure (e.g., “signature mismatch”, “schema invalid”, “semantic rule X violated”)

### 5) Operationalization
- Store the specification catalog and identifier mapping in a controlled, versioned configuration store.
- Use change management for updates (review/test/approval) and ensure the gateway uses the latest approved versions.
- Provide a test suite of representative valid/invalid payloads per data type to prevent regressions.

## WHO
- **Application Security Architect / Security Engineer**: define the approved specifications and identifier mapping approach; approve validation rules.
- **Backend/Platform Engineer**: implement the gateway component and validation pipeline.
- **Security Operations / Compliance**: review audit evidence format and validation failure reason codes.
- **Change Manager**: ensure controlled release of specification/identifier updates.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. For cross-domain transfers, the gateway identifies data type using **file signature(s) and/or token-based identifiers** and does not accept transfers based on filename/type alone.
2. Transfers are **blocked** when:
   - identifiers cannot be mapped to an approved specification, or
   - syntactic validation fails, or
   - semantic validation fails.
3. For each allowed data type, the gateway validates content against the corresponding **approved data format specification** (including semantic rules).
4. Audit logs are generated for 100% of transfer attempts and include pass/fail outcomes for identifier validation, syntactic validation, and semantic validation, with reason codes.
5. The implementation supports multiple internal signatures/tokens for a given approved data format where configured.


### ac-4.13 — Decomposition into Policy-relevant Subcomponents (Enhancement)

#### Cross-domain transfer gateway with policy-relevant transfer context

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement a cross-domain information transfer mechanism (e.g., secure email/file/portal/API) that requires a structured **Transfer Context** containing the policy-relevant subcomponents defined for AC-4(13) and submits that context to **policy enforcement mechanisms before the transfer completes**.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-4(13) enhancement requires that when transferring information between different security domains, the organization **decomposes information into policy-relevant subcomponents** ({{ insert: param, ac-04.13_odp }}) for **submission to policy enforcement mechanisms**. The guidance explains that this enables policy enforcement mechanisms to apply **filtering, inspection, and/or sanitization** based on differentiators such as **source/destination, certificates/labels, classification, attachments, and other security/privacy component differentiators**.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define the Transfer Context schema (policy-relevant subcomponents)
Create a canonical schema for `transfer_context` that includes at minimum the organization-defined subcomponents in `ac-04.13_odp`:
- `source_domain`
- `destination_domain`
- `classification` (Secret/Confidential)
- `data_type`
- `handling_requirements`
- `attachments_metadata`
- `certificate_or_label_information`

### 2) Enforce pre-transfer gating in the transfer gateway
For every cross-domain transfer initiated through the gateway:
- Require the client/app to provide (or the gateway to derive/validate) the `transfer_context`.
- The gateway must **pause** the transfer until it receives a policy decision from the policy enforcement mechanism.
- The policy enforcement mechanism must evaluate the `transfer_context` and return an action such as: **allow**, **block**, or **inspect/sanitize-then-allow**.

### 3) Integrate with policy enforcement mechanisms
Implement an internal policy decision integration (policy engine / policy-as-code / DLP/secure transfer policy layer) that:
- Accepts `transfer_context` as input.
- Applies rules for filtering/inspection/sanitization based on the subcomponents.
- Returns an enforcement decision to the gateway.

### 4) Capture audit evidence of decomposition and decision
Log, per transfer attempt:
- The submitted `transfer_context` (or a hashed/redacted representation where appropriate).
- The policy decision returned (allow/block/sanitize-then-allow).
- Any sanitization/inspection actions taken.
- Correlation identifiers linking the transfer request to the decision.

### 5) Operationalize roles and documentation
Document:
- How `source_domain` and `destination_domain` are determined (IdP groups, recipient portal target, endpoint mapping, etc.).
- How classification, data type, handling requirements, attachments metadata, and certificate/label information are obtained/validated.
- The gateway-to-policy-engine integration design and configuration.
- Responsibilities for maintaining the schema, policy rules, and mappings.

### 6) Prevent bypass paths (scope control)
Ensure cross-domain transfers for the in-scope application/data types must go through the gateway (e.g., restrict direct outbound channels such as unmanaged email/file share routes for those flows, or route them through the same gateway). This is necessary so the “before transfer completes” requirement is consistently met.

## WHO
- **Application Owner / Security Architect**: define the Transfer Context schema and mapping to security domains.
- **Platform/Integration Engineer**: implement the transfer gateway pre-transfer gating and policy-engine integration.
- **Cybersecurity Policy/Compliance Lead**: define policy rules that use the subcomponents for filtering/inspection/sanitization.
- **SOC/SIEM Engineer**: ensure audit logging, retention, and correlation with monitoring.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. For every cross-domain transfer initiated via the gateway, the system submits a `transfer_context` containing all required subcomponents from `ac-04.13_odp` **before** the transfer is completed.
2. The gateway does not complete the transfer when the policy engine returns **block**; when **sanitize-then-allow** is returned, the transfer completes only after sanitization/inspection is performed.
3. Audit logs exist for each transfer attempt showing: submitted `transfer_context` (or controlled representation), policy decision, and correlation IDs.
4. Policy enforcement rules demonstrably use at least: `source_domain`, `destination_domain`, `classification`, and `attachments_metadata` (and certificate/label information where applicable) to make decisions.
5. Documented design/configuration and responsibilities are available for audit (including how each subcomponent is derived/validated).


### ac-4.14 — Security or Privacy Policy Filter Constraints (Enhancement)

#### Enforce fixed UTF-8, 10KB, alphanumeric-only payloads at boundaries

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement a single enforcement point at every cross-domain information transfer boundary (API gateway and/or message broker) that **only accepts** messages that match the organization-defined **fully enumerated** security/privacy policy filters:
- **Fixed schema / fixed tags / no free-form fields**
- **UTF-8** encoding
- **Max 10KB** payload size
- **Alpha-numeric only** character content
- **Reject special characters**

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-4(14)** enhancement requires that when transferring information between different security domains, the system implements **organization-defined security or privacy policy filters** requiring **fully enumerated formats** that restrict **data structure and content** (e.g., fixed schema/field sets, file/payload size limits, field length/character restrictions, and schema validation).

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Standardize the enforcement boundary
- Route all cross-domain transfers through a controlled ingress/egress layer (e.g., API gateway for HTTP/REST and/or message broker for async events).
- Ensure no alternate network path bypasses this layer (enforce via routing, firewall rules, and/or broker topic ACLs).

### 2) Create and version the approved enumerated filters
- Implement two rule sets aligned to the organization-defined parameters:
  - **Security policy filters**: enforce **XML/JSON schema with fixed tags** (no free-form fields), **max 10KB payload**, **UTF-8**, **alpha-numeric only**, **no special characters**.
  - **Privacy policy filters**: enforce **JSON schema with fixed field set** (no free-form fields), **max 10KB payload**, **UTF-8**, **alpha-numeric only**, **no special characters**.
- Store schemas and rule-set metadata (rule IDs, schema versions, allowed fields/tags) in a policy repository used by the enforcement component.

### 3) Implement deterministic validation and hard deny
For each inbound cross-domain message:
