- **Payload size check**: reject if canonical payload bytes exceed **10KB**.
- **Encoding check**: reject if payload is not valid **UTF-8**.
- **Schema structure check**:
  - Security transfers: validate against the **XML/JSON schema with fixed tags**.
  - Privacy transfers: validate against the **JSON schema with fixed field set**.
  - Reject messages with missing required fields, unknown/extra fields, or any structure not matching the enumerated schema.
- **Content character check**:
  - Enforce **alpha-numeric only** across the defined character fields.
  - Define and apply a strict “special character” rule (reject punctuation/symbols/whitespace as applicable to your field definitions).
- If any check fails: **deny** the transfer and return a deterministic error code (e.g., `FILTER_SCHEMA_FAIL`, `FILTER_SIZE_FAIL`, `FILTER_ENCODING_FAIL`, `FILTER_CHARSET_FAIL`).

### 4) Audit logging for accept/reject decisions
- Log every decision at the boundary with:
  - timestamp, source/destination domain identifiers
  - rule-set ID and schema version
  - which constraint failed (without echoing sensitive payload content)
  - correlation ID / message ID
- Forward logs to the enterprise SIEM for retention and correlation.

### 5) Update system security plan and privacy plan references
- Explicitly reference the boundary enforcement component and the specific policy artifacts (rule-set IDs, schema versions, and enforcement behavior) in the **system security plan** and **privacy plan**.

## WHO
- **Security Architect / Compliance Engineer**: define rule-set IDs, special-character policy definition, and ensure documentation updates to security/privacy plans.
- **Platform/API Gateway or Messaging Engineer**: implement the enforcement logic, schema validation, and size/encoding/content checks.
- **App Owner / Integration Engineer**: ensure all cross-domain producers/clients conform to the fixed schemas.
- **SOC/SIEM Analyst**: validate alerting/dashboards for reject-rate anomalies.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. For each cross-domain transfer route, messages that do **not** match the enumerated schema (extra/missing fields, wrong structure) are **rejected**.
2. Messages exceeding **10KB** are **rejected**.
3. Non-UTF-8 payloads are **rejected**.
4. Messages containing **special characters** in the defined character fields are **rejected**; only alpha-numeric characters are accepted.
5. Every accept/reject decision generates an audit log entry containing rule-set ID, schema version, and failure reason (when rejected), and logs are visible in SIEM.
6. The system security plan and privacy plan explicitly reference the boundary enforcement mechanism and the enumerated filter artifacts.


### ac-4.15 — Detection of Unsanctioned Information (Enhancement)

#### Policy-driven inspection gateway for cross-domain transfer blocking

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Months
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement an inspection-and-enforcement point for **all transfers of information between security domains**. The system must **examine the transferred information** for the presence of **organization-defined unsanctioned information** (malicious code, unauthorized release data, and harmful executable content) and **prohibit the transfer** when such information is detected, in accordance with the organization-defined **Cross-Domain Security Transfer Policy and Data Transfer Privacy Policy**.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-4(15)** requires that, when transferring information between different security domains, the organization **examines the information** for **{{ insert: param, ac-04.15_odp.01 }}** and **prohibits the transfer** in accordance with **{{ insert: param, ac-4.15_prm_2 }}**.

The guidance defines unsanctioned information to include **malicious code**, **information inappropriate for release**, and **executable code that could disrupt or harm destination services/systems**.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Identify and force all cross-domain transfer paths through the inspection point
- Inventory all mechanisms that move data between security domains for this application/system (e.g., web upload/download, API payloads, file transfer, email/SaaS connectors, message bus publishing/subscribing, batch export jobs).
- Configure routing so that **no cross-domain transfer bypasses** the inspection point (e.g., proxy/connector enforcement, egress/ingress policy, service-to-service gateway, or application-layer mediation).

### 2) Implement payload examination aligned to the defined unsanctioned categories
Create a detection pipeline that evaluates each transfer payload (and relevant embedded content) for:
- **Malicious code**: malware scanning with archive handling (extract/scan nested content), and where feasible sandbox/detonation for high-risk artifacts.
- **Unauthorized release data**: data classification/entitlement checks using the organization’s data labels and privacy constraints (e.g., block if payload contains regulated/PII/proprietary content that is not authorized for the destination domain).
- **Harmful executable content**: detection/blocking of run-capable formats and active content (e.g., scripts/macros, executables, script-bearing documents) and embedded executable indicators.

### 3) Enforce “prohibit transfer” using policy-as-code
- Encode the **Cross-Domain Security Transfer Policy and Data Transfer Privacy Policy** as machine-enforceable rules (policy-as-code) that map:
  - **source domain → destination domain**
  - **detected unsanctioned category** (ac-04.15_odp.01)
  - **required action** (deny/prohibit transfer)
- Ensure the enforcement decision is deterministic and version-controlled with change control.

### 4) Fail closed and provide controlled outcomes
- If inspection cannot be completed (scanner unavailable, policy engine unreachable), configure the enforcement point to **fail closed** for cross-domain transfers containing potentially unsanctioned content.
- For detected unsanctioned information, take one of the prohibited-transfer outcomes (e.g., reject with error, quarantine for security review) consistent with the organization’s policy; the key requirement is that the **transfer is prohibited**.

### 5) Produce audit evidence tied to the policy rationale
For each blocked/prohibited transfer, log at minimum:
- transfer/session identifier, source domain, destination domain
- payload identifier and **hash** (or equivalent integrity reference)
- detection categories triggered (malicious code / unauthorized release data / harmful executable content)
- the specific policy rule/section from **ac-4.15_prm_2** that caused the prohibition
- action taken (blocked/quarantined/rejected) and timestamp

## WHO
- **Application/Integration Owner**: ensures all cross-domain transfer paths are routed through the inspection point.
- **Security Engineering / Platform Team**: implements the inspection gateway, detection pipeline, and policy-as-code enforcement.
- **Information Security / Privacy Officer**: validates the mapping of detection outcomes to the organization-defined policies (ac-4.15_prm_2).
- **SOC/SIEM Operations**: ensures logs are forwarded, retained, and searchable for audit.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. **Coverage**: All configured cross-domain transfer mechanisms for the application/system are routed through the inspection point (no bypass paths remain for production flows).
2. **Detection**: For test payloads containing:
   - malicious code,
   - unauthorized-release data (per data labels/entitlements), and
   - harmful executable content,
   the inspection point detects the relevant category.
3. **Enforcement**: For each test payload above, the system **prohibits the transfer** (destination receives nothing) according to the organization-defined policy (ac-4.15_prm_2).
4. **Auditability**: Each prohibited transfer produces audit logs containing the triggered policy rule/section and detection category.
5. **Fail-closed behavior**: When inspection/policy enforcement is unavailable, cross-domain transfers that would be subject to AC-4(15) are blocked/prohibited per the configured fail-closed policy.


### ac-4.17 — Domain Authentication (Enhancement)

#### Authenticate source/destination domains and log endpoints end-to-end

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement domain authentication for information transfers by **uniquely identifying and cryptographically authenticating both the source (sender) and destination (receiver) domain points** using the organization-defined domain types in **AC-4(17)**: **organisation, system, application, service, and individual**. Ensure the application automatically applies these authenticated domain labels to transfers and records them for forensic reconstruction.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-4(17) requires: **“Uniquely identify and authenticate source and destination points by {{ insert: param, ac-04.17_odp }} for information transfer.”** The guidance emphasizes that successful domain authentication depends on **system labels distinguishing among systems, organizations, and individuals** involved in preparing/sending/receiving/disseminating information, enabling **forensic reconstruction, policy compliance attribution, and personal data lineage**.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define the domain label scheme (use ac-04.17_odp)
- Create/confirm the organization’s required domain label format and registry referenced by **`ac-04.17_odp`**.
- The scheme must support at least these domain types:
  - **organisation**
  - **system**
  - **application**
  - **service**
  - **individual**
- Ensure labels are **unique, unambiguous, and distinguishable** across the above types.
- Maintain a controlled registry (owners, review cadence, mapping rules from identity claims/endpoints to domain labels).

### 2) Authenticate individuals and bind them to the transfer
- For user-driven transfers, require authentication via the enterprise IdP.
- Bind the **individual** domain label to the authenticated session/token (e.g., from IdP claims) and ensure the application uses that bound identity when initiating the transfer.

### 3) Authenticate systems/services and bind them to the transfer
- For service-to-service transfers (APIs, messaging, microservices), require workload identity (e.g., mTLS identity, signed service assertions, or short-lived tokens) so the caller and receiver are authenticated.
- Map the authenticated caller/receiver identity to the **system/application/service** domain labels using the registry.

### 4) Enforce domain authentication at the transfer boundary
- Implement a single enforcement point in the application stack (e.g., API gateway, service middleware, or message ingress handler) that:
  - Extracts authenticated source identity (individual + calling service/system/application as applicable)
  - Extracts authenticated destination identity (target service/system/application)
  - Validates that required domain labels exist and are valid per the registry
  - Rejects transfers when domain labels cannot be authenticated/validated (fail closed)

### 5) Record authenticated source and destination in audit logs
- For every information transfer event, write audit fields that include **both authenticated source and authenticated destination domain labels** for the relevant domain types.
- Ensure logs are structured (e.g., JSON) and include correlation identifiers so the SOC can reconstruct end-to-end flows.
- Ensure audit records are retained per enterprise logging requirements and are forwarded to the SIEM.

### 6) Failure handling procedure (operational behavior)
- Define application behavior when domain authentication fails:
  - deny/reject the transfer (or quarantine it, if business requires) and emit an audit event indicating the failure reason
  - ensure the failure is observable in SIEM
- Provide runbook guidance for operators on how to respond and how to remediate label/identity mapping issues.

## WHO
- **Application Security Engineer / Architect**: implement label scheme integration, enforcement logic, and audit schema.
- **Backend/Platform Engineers**: integrate with IdP/workload identity, middleware/gateway enforcement, and logging.
- **IAM/Identity Team**: ensure individual authentication and token/claim availability for mapping to **individual** domain labels.
- **SOC/Monitoring Team**: validate audit fields and SIEM ingestion/correlation.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. For a test transfer initiated by an authenticated user, the audit record contains **authenticated source** domain labels (including **individual**) and **authenticated destination** domain labels.
2. For a test service-to-service transfer, the audit record contains authenticated **system/application/service** domain labels for both source and destination.
3. Transfers with missing/invalid authenticated identities (e.g., expired token, invalid workload identity, unknown endpoint identity) are **rejected or quarantined** and produce an audit event indicating domain authentication failure.
4. Domain labels used in audit logs conform to the organization-defined scheme referenced by **`ac-04.17_odp`** and are unique/unambiguous across domain types.
5. Audit events are ingested into SIEM and can be correlated end-to-end using a correlation identifier.


### ac-4.19 — Validation of Metadata (Enhancement)

#### Enforce metadata validation/filters on cross-domain transfers

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement organization-defined **security or privacy policy filters on metadata** for every **cross-domain transfer**, validating metadata against **DoD domain-transfer rules** and ensuring metadata is **bound/consistent with the payload’s classification**. Non-compliant metadata must be **corrected or blocked** and the system must generate **audit evidence** of validation/filtering outcomes.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-4(19)** (Enhancement) requires: “When transferring information between different security domains, implement {{ insert: param, ac-4.19_prm_1 }} on metadata.” The organization-defined parameter **ac-4.19_prm_1** specifies applying metadata validation filters per DoD data handling policy, including **domain transfer rules** and **binding to payload classification**.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define the metadata validation filters (policy-as-code inputs)
- Create an authoritative metadata schema for transfers that includes (at minimum) the fields required by your **ac-04.19_odp.01** and **ac-04.19_odp.02** guidance:
  - DoD metadata domain-transfer rules: **classification, releasability, system of record, required security markings**.
  - Privacy filters: **HIPAA minimum necessary**, **GDPR lawful basis tags**, and **redaction of personal data fields in metadata**.
- Define how the system determines the **effective payload classification** (e.g., from a trusted payload label/header or payload label service) and how metadata must be **bound/consistent** with that classification.
- Decide and document the organization’s stance on metadata vs bound payload data for filtering (per AC-4(19) guidance): whether metadata and its bound payload are treated distinctly or as part of the payload.

### 2) Implement enforcement at the transfer boundary
- Identify all cross-domain transfer mechanisms used by the application (e.g., API egress, message bus publishing, managed file transfer, secure email gateway, object storage replication, event streaming).
- Route cross-domain transfers through a **metadata-aware validation component** (gateway/interceptor/library) that performs, for each transfer:
  1. **Extract metadata** from the transfer envelope (and, if applicable, metadata embedded in the payload).
  2. **Validate DoD metadata domain-transfer rules** against the source→destination domain mapping.
  3. **Validate binding to payload classification**:
     - Derive effective payload classification from the trusted payload label.
     - Compare metadata classification/releasability/system-of-record/markings to the derived payload classification.
     - If mismatched, apply deterministic correction rules where allowed; otherwise **block**.
  4. **Apply privacy filters to metadata**:
     - Enforce HIPAA minimum-necessary reduction (allowlist required metadata fields for the transfer purpose).
     - Require/validate GDPR lawful-basis tags for the processing purpose.
     - **Redact personal-data fields in metadata** per the organization’s redaction rules.
  5. **Enforce outcome policy**:
     - If required markings/tags cannot be validated or redaction/correction cannot be performed safely, **block** the transfer.
     - Otherwise **correct-and-forward** with corrected metadata.

### 3) Produce audit evidence for each transfer
For every cross-domain transfer attempt (allowed/corrected/blocked), log an auditable record including:
- Transfer identifier, source domain, destination domain, timestamp.
- Metadata validation results (which rules were evaluated).
- Actions taken: validated OK / corrected fields (and which) / redacted fields (and which) / blocked reason.
- The effective payload classification used for binding validation.
- Correlation identifiers to SIEM/monitoring.

### 4) Operationalize and test
- Add automated tests that cover:
  - Correct metadata passes.
  - Incorrect classification/releasability/markings is corrected or blocked.
  - Missing/invalid GDPR lawful-basis tags are blocked.
  - Personal-data fields in metadata are redacted.
  - Metadata/payload classification mismatch triggers correction or block.
- Ensure the validation component is enabled in all production cross-domain transfer paths.

## WHO
- **Application Security Engineer / Architect**: define metadata schema, rule logic, and binding approach.
- **Platform/Integration Engineer**: implement the metadata-aware validation component and integrate it into all cross-domain transfer paths.
- **Privacy Officer / Compliance Lead**: confirm HIPAA minimum-necessary and GDPR lawful-basis/redaction rules.
- **Security Operations (SIEM) Engineer**: ensure audit logs are forwarded, retained, and searchable.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. For every cross-domain transfer path, the system performs metadata validation using **ac-4.19_prm_1** filters before delivery.
2. For transfers with metadata classification/releasability/system-of-record/markings inconsistent with the derived payload classification, the system either **corrects** metadata deterministically or **blocks** the transfer (no silent pass-through).
3. For transfers containing personal data fields in metadata, the system **redacts** those fields per the configured privacy rules.
4. For transfers requiring GDPR lawful basis, missing/invalid lawful-basis tags result in **block** (or deterministic correction only if explicitly allowed by policy).
5. For each transfer attempt, the system produces an audit record showing: rules evaluated, outcome (allowed/corrected/blocked), and specific correction/redaction/block reasons.


### ac-4.20 — Approved Solutions (Enhancement)

#### Enforce NSA-approved cross-domain solutions for Confidential/Secret flows

- **Category:** Manual
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Days
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement and operate cross-domain information-flow enforcement using **only NSA-approved cross-domain solutions and configurations** for **Confidential and Secret** data flows across security domains. Maintain an auditable baseline of the approved solution/configuration and ensure the running enforcement matches it.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-4(20)** enhancement requires: **“Employ {{ ac-04.20_odp.01 }} to control the flow of {{ ac-04.20_odp.02 }} across security domains.”**
- **ac-04.20_odp.01** = *solutions in approved configurations*
- **ac-04.20_odp.02** = *information* (here: *Confidential and Secret data*)
This must be enforced across domains using approved cross-domain solutions/configurations, with policy/design/configuration alignment and audit evidence.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Create the approved cross-domain enforcement inventory (authoritative)
- Build an inventory that lists, for each security-domain boundary crossing used by the application:
  - the **cross-domain solution name/vendor/model** and **version**
  - the **approved configuration profile ID/name** used for **Confidential/Secret** flows
  - the **direction(s)** (Domain A→B, B→A)
  - the **data types** (Confidential/Secret) and **protocols/applications** the enforcement covers
- Source the “approved” status from the organization’s NSA-approved cross-domain solution/configuration list (per guidance).

### 2) Update cross-domain information flow enforcement policy (policy-as-evidence)
- In the cross-domain policy/guidance document, add an appendix that explicitly maps:
  - **Confidential/Secret** flows → **approved solution(s)** → **approved configuration profile(s)**
  - allowed/denied flow rules per boundary and per protocol/application
- Ensure the policy states that **only** those approved solutions/configurations are used for Confidential/Secret cross-domain flows.

### 3) Implement configuration baseline enforcement (prevent drift)
- For each deployed cross-domain enforcement component:
  - store the **approved configuration** as the “golden baseline” (e.g., version-controlled templates)
  - implement automated deployment/verification so the **running configuration** matches the approved baseline
  - block or require change-control approval for any configuration changes that would deviate from the approved profile

### 4) Ensure audit logging is attributable to the approved enforcement
- Configure the cross-domain enforcement components to emit centralized logs that include at minimum:
  - component identifier (gateway/relay instance)
  - **policy/profile name** (the approved configuration profile used)
  - decision outcome (allowed/denied)
  - timestamps and relevant session/flow identifiers
- Route logs to the enterprise SIEM/log platform used for audit evidence.

### 5) Produce system security plan (SSP) evidence with named responsibilities
- Update the application/system SSP to include:
  - the cross-domain enforcement approach for Confidential/Secret flows
  - the approved solution/configuration profile(s) used
  - named roles responsible for: baseline maintenance, configuration compliance verification, and audit log review/retention

### 6) Operational procedures for enforcement verification
- Create/extend runbooks for operations teams that instruct how to:
  - verify the deployed configuration matches the approved baseline (using the automated compliance checks)
  - confirm enforcement is active (e.g., log generation and decision logging)
  - handle exceptions via the approved change process (no unapproved solutions/configurations)

## WHO
- **Security Architect / Compliance Architect**: owns the approved-solution mapping, policy updates, and SSP updates.
- **Network/Platform Engineering (Cross-domain enforcement owners)**: implements the approved configuration baselines and drift prevention.
- **SOC/SIEM Operations**: ensures logs are centralized, retained, and reviewable for audit.
- **Change Management / Configuration Management**: enforces that deviations from approved profiles follow approved change control.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. Cross-domain policy includes an explicit mapping: **Confidential/Secret flows** → **NSA-approved solution(s)** → **approved configuration profile(s)**.
2. For every deployed cross-domain enforcement instance used for Confidential/Secret flows, there is evidence (inventory + configuration records) showing the **exact approved profile** applied.
3. Automated configuration compliance checks demonstrate the **running configuration matches** the approved baseline (no drift) at deployment and on a defined periodic cadence.
4. Centralized audit logs show enforcement decisions with fields identifying the **component** and the **approved policy/profile name**, and logs are retained per enterprise requirements.
5. SSP contains the enforcement approach, approved solution/profile references, and named responsibilities for baseline maintenance and audit evidence handling.


### ac-4.21 — Physical or Logical Separation of Information Flows (Enhancement)

#### Implement VRF/VLAN + firewall zones + per-class VPN tunnels

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement logical separation of information flows for the application’s network traffic by mapping required traffic categories (inbound vs outbound, service requests vs responses, Secret vs Confidential, and IT vs OT control traffic) to dedicated logical constructs (VLANs/VRFs, firewall security zones, and separate VPN tunnels per traffic type/classification). Enforce separation with stateful firewall policies and bind each VPN tunnel to the appropriate routing domain/zone.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-4(21)** (Enhancement) requires: “Separate information flows logically or physically using {{ insert: param, ac-4.21_prm_1 }} to accomplish {{ insert: param, ac-04.21_odp.03 }}.”
- **Organization-defined mechanisms/techniques**: VLANs/VRFs, dedicated firewall zones, separate VPN tunnels, distinct routing domains (**ac-4.21_prm_1**).
- **Required separations**: inbound vs outbound, service requests vs responses, Secret vs Confidential, IT vs OT control traffic (**ac-04.21_odp.03**).
The solution must ensure traffic is not commingled in transit and that flow control is achievable via the intended transmission paths.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define the traffic separation matrix (design artifact)
Create a single, version-controlled mapping document/table that states for each traffic type:
- Traffic category (inbound vs outbound; requests vs responses; Secret vs Confidential; IT vs OT control)
- Source/destination systems or network segments
- Required logical separation construct (VLAN/VRF, firewall zone, routing domain)
- Allowed communication paths (explicitly allowed flows only)
- Default-deny behavior for all other paths

### 2) Implement logical separation using VLANs/VRFs and routing domains
- Create **separate VLANs/VRFs** for at least **Secret** and **Confidential** traffic.
- Create **separate VRFs/routing instances** (or equivalent routing domains) for **IT vs OT control traffic** where policy requires stronger separation than VLANs alone.
- Ensure routing between VRFs is either:
  - Not permitted, or
  - Permitted only via explicitly approved firewall-mediated paths (no broad route leaking).

### 3) Create dedicated firewall security zones per traffic category
- Define firewall security zones aligned to the traffic matrix, at minimum:
  - Inbound entry zone
  - Service request processing zone
  - Service response return zone
  - Secret zone
  - Confidential zone
  - IT vs OT control zones (as applicable)
- Configure **stateful inspection** and enforce **default-deny** between zones.
- Create explicit, directionally correct allow rules only for required flows.

### 4) Separate VPN tunnels per traffic type and classification
- Create **distinct VPN tunnels** for each required separation dimension (at minimum: Secret vs Confidential; and inbound/outbound or request/response as required by the traffic matrix).
- Bind each tunnel to:
  - The correct address pools
  - The correct VRF/routing domain
  - The correct firewall zone(s)
- Ensure that traffic arriving via a given tunnel can only reach destinations in the corresponding VRF/zone.

### 5) Validate enforcement with testable evidence
- Perform connectivity tests that verify:
  - Allowed flows succeed (e.g., Confidential inbound → request processing → response return)
  - Disallowed flows fail (e.g., Secret traffic cannot reach Confidential VRF destinations; OT control traffic cannot reach IT user-plane zones)
- Capture firewall logs showing zone/VRF/tunnel identifiers for both allowed and denied attempts.

### 6) Operationalize and maintain
- Store network diagrams (VRF/VLAN topology, firewall zones, VPN tunnel bindings) and configuration exports in the configuration management system.
- Establish a change process so that any new service endpoints or routes require updates to the traffic matrix and firewall/VPN bindings.

## WHO
- **Network Security Engineer / Network Architect**: designs VRF/VLAN, firewall zones, and VPN tunnel bindings.
- **Firewall/VPN Administrator**: implements and validates stateful firewall rules and tunnel configuration.
- **Application Owner / System Owner**: provides required traffic categories (what is inbound/outbound, request/response, Secret/Confidential, IT/OT control).
- **Security/Compliance Engineer**: verifies evidence meets AC-4(21) expectations and supports audit readiness.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. A version-controlled **traffic separation matrix** exists mapping each required traffic category (**ac-04.21_odp.03**) to the selected mechanisms (**ac-4.21_prm_1**).
2. **Secret and Confidential** traffic traverse **separate VRFs/routing domains** and are enforced by **firewall security zones** with **default-deny** between non-approved zones.
3. **Inbound vs outbound** and **service requests vs responses** are enforced via distinct firewall zones and directionally correct stateful rules (responses return only through the approved return path).
4. **Separate VPN tunnels** exist per required traffic type/classification and are bound to the correct VRF/zone such that cross-classification commingling is blocked.
5. Evidence exists (firewall logs and test results) demonstrating at least one allowed path and one denied cross-path attempt for each required separation dimension.


### ac-4.22 — Access Only (Enhancement)

#### Access-only multi-domain terminal with enforced no-transfer separation

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 74%

## WHAT
Implement an “access-only” user access device (dedicated workstation/terminal or VDI session broker) that allows a single user device to view information from multiple security domains, while **preventing any information flow between those domains**.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-4(22)** requires: “Provide access from a single device to computing platforms, applications, or data residing in multiple different security domains, while preventing information flow between the different security domains.” The guidance describes an access-only solution (e.g., a terminal) that “assuredly keeps the information separate,” with no mechanisms that allow users to transfer data/information between domains.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define the domain separation model
- Create a mapping of **Security Domain A/B/C** to:
  - Which applications/data each domain can display.
  - Which user roles may access each domain.
  - Which transfer capabilities must be blocked between domains (clipboard, file save/export, printing, downloads, drag-and-drop, removable media, shared folders).

### 2) Build a dedicated access-only endpoint profile
- Use a hardened OS image or VDI/terminal template with:
  - Minimal local software (only what is required for access).
  - Centralized management (e.g., enterprise endpoint management) and immutable/controlled configuration.
  - Full-disk encryption and restricted local admin.

### 3) Enforce separation at the session boundary (no shared transfer primitives)
- Render each security domain in a **separate isolated execution context** (choose one consistent pattern):
  - Separate VDI sessions per domain, or
  - Separate browser/container instances per domain.
- Explicitly disable or block cross-context transfer mechanisms:
  - **Clipboard sharing disabled** (no copy/paste across domains).
  - **Drag-and-drop disabled**.
  - **Downloads/export to local storage blocked** (or forced to a domain-specific controlled sink that does not enable cross-domain movement).
  - **Printing blocked or restricted** to domain-specific print queues only (or fully blocked if not required).
  - **Shared folders/network drives disabled** for cross-domain use.
  - **Removable media access disabled** (USB/other media) for the access-only profile.

### 4) Deny-by-default with auditable enforcement
- Configure the access-only platform so that any attempt to use transfer mechanisms results in:
  - A **technical denial** (no data movement), and
  - A **log event** indicating the attempted action and the domain context.

### 5) Centralize logging and evidence for audits
- Forward endpoint/VDI/browser-container logs to the SIEM.
- Ensure logs include at minimum:
  - User identity, timestamp.
  - Domain accessed (A/B/C).
  - Any denied transfer attempt type (clipboard/export/print/download/drag-drop/removable media).

### 6) Operationalize exceptions (if any)
- If printing or other controlled outputs are required, implement **domain-specific allowlists** (e.g., only allow printing from Domain A to a Domain A print queue) and require documented approval.
- Ensure exceptions do not create a path for cross-domain data movement.

## WHO (roles responsible)
- **Security Engineering / IAM & Endpoint Security Team:** designs the domain separation model and hardening configuration.
- **Platform/VDI/Endpoint Administrators:** implement and maintain the access-only endpoint/VDI templates and policy-as-code.
- **SOC / Monitoring Team:** validates SIEM ingestion and detection coverage for denied transfer attempts.
- **System Owner / Data Owner:** approves domain mappings and any controlled-output exceptions.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. From a single access-only device, a user can open/view content in multiple security domains.
2. Cross-domain transfer is technically prevented:
   - Clipboard copy/paste across domains fails.
   - Save/export/download to local storage is blocked (or constrained to a domain-specific non-cross-domain sink).
   - Drag-and-drop across domains is disabled.
   - Printing is blocked or restricted to domain-specific queues.
   - Removable media is disabled for the access-only profile.
3. Every denied transfer attempt generates an audit log entry in SIEM with user, domain context, and action type.
4. The access-only endpoint/VDI configuration is reproducible (template/policy-as-code) and can be shown as the deployed configuration during an audit.


### ac-4.23 — Modify Non-releasable Information (Enhancement)

#### Enforce redaction/masking on cross-domain transfers via policy gateway

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement an enforcement mechanism for cross-domain information transfers that **modifies non-releasable information** using the organization-defined **modification action** (default: **redaction and masking**) before the information enters the receiving security domain.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-4(23)** (Enhancement) requires: when transferring information between different security domains, **modify non-releasable information** by implementing the organization-defined **modification action** (parameter **ac-04.23_odp**). Modification actions include **masking, permutation, alteration, removal, or redaction**.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define the cross-domain transfer routes that must be mediated
- Identify all application-to-application / application-to-storage / application-to-messaging / application-to-analytics / application-to-partner/vendor transfer paths that cross security domains (e.g., different trust zones, tenants, partner environments, or regulated vs non-regulated segments).
- For each route, define:
  - **Sending domain** and **receiving domain**
  - Trigger conditions for “non-releasable” (e.g., data classification/labels, field-level release rules, user/service authorization context)
  - Whether the transfer includes **content**, **metadata**, **previews/thumbnails**, **exports**, **logs**, or **attachments**.

### 2) Create a redaction/masking specification aligned to ac-04.23_odp
- For each sensitive field/data type that may be non-releasable, define the transformation:
  - **Masking format** (e.g., `****1234`, `[REDACTED]`)
  - **Removal vs redaction** rules (e.g., remove entire element vs replace with token)
  - Determinism requirements (if correlation is needed, use consistent tokenization; otherwise use non-recoverable redaction)
  - Handling for structured data (JSON/XML), documents (PDF/Office), and message bodies/attachments.

### 3) Implement enforcement in the transfer path (gateway/transform service)
- Deploy/configure a **secure transfer gateway** (or integrate a transformation service) that performs **content-aware inspection and transformation** on every mediated cross-domain transfer.
- Ensure the gateway applies the redaction/masking specification **before** the payload is delivered to the receiving domain.
- Ensure the gateway also covers secondary representations where applicable (e.g., previews/thumbnails) so that non-releasable information is not exposed via derived views.

### 4) Make policy decisions auditable and non-sensitive
- Configure the gateway to log evidence of modification **without recording original sensitive values**.
- Audit log fields should include (at minimum):
  - Transfer route identifier (route ID)
  - Rule/specification ID that triggered modification
  - Transformation type (e.g., redaction, masking)
  - Timestamp, sender identity (user/service), receiving domain
  - A non-sensitive indicator of what changed (e.g., counts, hashes of transformed segments, or pattern IDs)

### 5) Operational ownership and change control
- Assign named roles responsible for:
  - Maintaining the redaction/masking specification (Security/Data Governance)
  - Operating and monitoring the gateway (App/Platform Ops)
  - Approving changes to release rules/specifications (InfoSec/Compliance)
- Integrate specification updates into existing change/config management so modifications are version-controlled and reviewable.

### 6) Test and validate effectiveness
- Execute test cases for each defined cross-domain route and data type:
  - Verify non-releasable fields are **redacted/masked** in the receiving domain.
  - Verify original values are not recoverable from the receiving payload or derived views.
  - Verify audit logs show that modification occurred (rule IDs/transformation indicators).

## WHO
- **Information Security / Data Governance Lead**: defines non-releasable criteria and the redaction/masking specification (ac-04.23_odp).
- **Application/Platform Security Engineer**: implements gateway/transform integration and transformation logic.
- **App/Platform Operations**: monitors gateway enforcement and audit log pipeline.
- **Compliance/Auditor Liaison**: validates audit evidence and test results.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. For every identified cross-domain transfer route, the system enforces redaction/masking for non-releasable information **before** delivery to the receiving domain.
2. For each sensitive field/data type marked non-releasable, the receiving-domain payload contains only the defined **redaction/masking** output (per ac-04.23_odp) and does not expose original values.
3. Audit logs contain evidence of modification for each mediated transfer (route ID, rule/spec ID, transformation type, timestamp, sender/receiving domain) and do **not** include original sensitive values.
4. Test results demonstrate that derived representations (e.g., previews/thumbnails/exports) do not leak non-releasable information for the tested routes.
5. Redaction/masking rules are version-controlled, change-reviewed, and deployed through the organization’s standard change/config management process.

### ac-4.24 — Internal Normalized Format (Enhancement)

#### Canonical parse/regenerate at cross-domain integration boundaries

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement a “parse → normalize (canonical internal model) → regenerate (destination spec)” processing step at every integration boundary where information crosses between security domains.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-4(24) requires that when transferring information between different security domains, the system **parses incoming data into an internal normalized format** and then **regenerates the data to be consistent with its intended specification**. This is intended to prevent malicious or malformed formatting from being misinterpreted and to reduce classes of data exfiltration caused by unexpected data formats.

## HOW (specific approach)
1. **Identify cross-domain transfer points**
   - Create/maintain an inventory of all data flows that cross security domains (e.g., API calls from one zone to another, message broker consumers/producers, ETL jobs, file drops, and any inter-environment transfers such as dev→prod if treated as different security domains).
   - For each flow, record: source domain, destination domain, data type(s), and the destination’s expected specification (schema/field rules/encoding).

2. **Define canonical internal models per data type**
   - For each data type transferred (e.g., Customer, Invoice, Asset, Alarm/Event), define a canonical internal representation with strict typing and canonicalization rules, such as:
     - Date/time: ISO-8601 with explicit timezone handling
     - Numbers/decimals: canonical decimal representation (no locale-dependent formatting)
     - Encoding: enforce UTF-8 and normalize character forms where applicable
     - Enumerations: map to controlled values
     - Structural rules: required/optional fields and allowed nesting
   - Version the canonical model (e.g., `canonicalModelVersion`) and store it with the integration contract.

3. **Enforce parse-and-normalize at the boundary**
   - At the entry point to the destination domain (API gateway handler, message consumer, ETL transform start, etc.), implement strict parsing/validation into the canonical internal model.
   - Use “fail fast” behavior:
     - Reject or quarantine messages/files that cannot be parsed or validated.
     - Do not allow partially parsed objects to proceed to business logic.

4. **Regenerate output strictly to the destination specification**
   - After internal processing (or immediately if the boundary is purely a transfer), regenerate the outgoing payload **only** from the canonical internal model.
   - Ensure regeneration matches the destination specification exactly (schema, field presence, ordering if required, encoding, and formatting rules).

5. **Safe failure + audit evidence**
   - On parse/regenerate failure:
     - Route to a quarantine mechanism (restricted access storage/queue) or reject the transaction.
     - Emit an auditable event containing at minimum: correlation/transaction ID, source flow ID, canonical model version, destination contract version, parse outcome (success/failure), and error category.
   - Ensure logs are forwarded to the enterprise SIEM/observability stack.

6. **Operationalize via configuration-as-code**
   - Store canonical model definitions and destination contract mappings in version control.
   - Deploy validation/regeneration logic via the standard CI/CD pipeline with change management.

## WHO
- **Application Integration Engineer / Backend Engineer**: implement boundary parsing, canonical models, and regeneration logic.
- **Security Architect**: confirm which flows are “cross-domain” and review canonicalization rules for defensive handling.
- **App Owner / Data Owner**: approve destination specifications and canonical model versions.
- **SOC/SecOps**: triage quarantine/rejection events and monitor for repeated malformed attempts.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
- For each identified cross-domain flow, there is a boundary implementation that:
  1) parses incoming payloads into a canonical internal model,
  2) uses that canonical model for subsequent processing, and
  3) regenerates outgoing payloads to match the destination specification.
- For invalid/unparseable inputs, the system does not forward data to the destination domain; it rejects or quarantines and records an auditable event with correlation ID.
- Canonical model and destination contract versions are logged for each transaction.
- Automated tests exist for at least: valid payloads, schema violations, encoding/format edge cases (e.g., date/decimal), and regeneration correctness.
- Evidence (logs/events) is available in SIEM/observability for parse success/failure and regeneration outcomes.

### ac-4.25 — Data Sanitization (Enhancement)

#### Policy-enforced data sanitization on cross-domain transfer

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement an automated, policy-driven “sanitize-on-transfer” mechanism that irreversibly sanitizes data whenever information is transferred between different security domains, using the organization’s defined sanitization policy.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 AC-4(25) (Enhancement) requires that when transferring information between different security domains, the organization **sanitizes data to minimize spillage of sensitive information** in accordance with **the organization’s data sanitization policy**.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define the cross-domain transfer inventory (transfer map)
- Identify and document every pathway where data moves between security domains for this application/system, including: API calls, message bus topics/queues, file transfers, database replication, backup/restore flows, SaaS/cloud connectors, email/file gateway handoffs, and removable media workflows (if applicable).
- For each pathway, record: source domain, destination domain, data types/classifications, storage medium involved (e.g., SSD/HDD, encrypted volume, object storage), and whether data is persisted at the destination.

### 2) Map sanitization methods to policy requirements
- Create a configuration mapping that translates the organization’s sanitization policy into enforceable actions per transfer pathway and data type.
- At minimum, support policy-selected sanitization methods appropriate to the medium (e.g., secure erase/cryptographic erase for storage; destruction for media where required by policy).

### 3) Enforce sanitization before/as part of transfer
Implement enforcement in the system’s transfer tooling so transfers cannot complete without the required sanitization step (or a policy-documented exception).
- **For storage-backed transfers (e.g., replication/backup staging):**
  - Ensure staging areas are encrypted and that cryptographic erasure (key destruction) is performed per policy when staging is no longer needed.
  - For decommissioned or re-imaged devices/instances used in the transfer workflow, trigger secure wipe/cryptographic erase via the enterprise device lifecycle tooling.
- **For application-level transfers (e.g., API/messaging):**
  - Ensure any temporary buffers, caches, and message persistence locations are cleared according to policy (e.g., overwrite/secure delete where supported; otherwise cryptographic erasure of encryption keys for ephemeral storage).
- **For cloud/object storage transfers:**
  - Apply policy-driven key lifecycle so that once data is transferred and no longer required in the source domain, the relevant keys are destroyed to make residual data unrecoverable.

### 4) Generate auditable evidence for every sanitization event
For each transfer pathway where sanitization is required, log and retain evidence including:
- Transfer identifier (correlation ID), source/destination domain identifiers
- Data classification/type
- Sanitization method used (as defined by policy mapping)
- Target identifiers (device/volume/object/bucket/prefix/staging location)
- Workflow identity (service account/operator identity) and timestamp
- Verification outcome (e.g., wipe completion status, key destruction confirmation, or policy-defined verification signal)

### 5) Integrate with operational controls
- Ensure the enforcement logic is deployed as part of the application/system configuration (infrastructure-as-code where possible) and is included in the system’s security configuration baseline.
- Provide a runbook for operators describing how to troubleshoot failed sanitization steps and how to request/approve policy exceptions (if your organization allows exceptions).

## WHO
- **Application Owner / System Owner:** accountable for ensuring the transfer map and policy mapping cover all cross-domain pathways.
- **Security Engineering / IAM & Platform Engineering:** implement the policy-as-code enforcement and audit logging.
- **Infrastructure/Cloud Operations (or OT gateway team if applicable):** ensure device lifecycle and storage lifecycle actions (secure wipe/cryptographic erasure) are triggered and verified.
- **Cybersecurity/Compliance:** validate that the implemented methods align with the organization’s data sanitization policy.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. A documented **transfer map** exists for the application/system showing all cross-domain data transfer pathways.
2. For each pathway requiring sanitization, the system enforces the policy mapping such that transfers **cannot complete** without the required sanitization action (or a documented, approved exception path).
3. Sanitization actions are **irreversible** per the organization’s policy method selection (e.g., cryptographic erasure via key destruction or secure erase completion).
4. For at least one test transfer per pathway, the system produces audit records containing: transfer ID, source/destination domains, method, target identifiers, workflow identity, timestamp, and verification result.
5. Audit records are retained in accordance with enterprise logging/retention requirements and are retrievable for assessment.

### ac-4.26 — Audit Filtering Actions (Enhancement)

#### Per-message content filter action/outcome audit at domain boundaries

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement audit logging for **content filtering actions and results** for **each individual message** that is inspected when information is transferred between **security domains** (cross-domain boundaries). Each audit record must capture:
- the **filtering action** taken (e.g., allowed, modified/sanitized, blocked/quarantined, replaced), and
- the **filtering outcome** (e.g., passed policy, failed policy, final disposition),
- linked to the **specific message** via an unambiguous correlation identifier.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-4(26) Audit Filtering Actions** requires that when transferring information between different security domains, the organization **records and audits content filtering actions and results** for the information being filtered. Guidance emphasizes that content filter reports support troubleshooting (e.g., why content was modified or why it failed filtering) and that audit events are defined in **AU-2** and audit records are generated in **AU-12**.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Identify and scope cross-domain filtering points
- Inventory all cross-domain transfers where content filtering occurs (e.g., email gateway, secure web gateway, secure file transfer gateway, API gateway/WAF if it performs payload inspection).
- For each boundary, define the **enforcement point** and the **authoritative policy set** applied at that boundary.

### 2) Configure the filtering mechanism to emit per-message action + outcome
For each boundary enforcement point:
- Enable content filtering and ensure it produces **per-message** logging.
- Configure log fields (or equivalent) to include:
  - **message identifier** (e.g., Message-ID, transaction ID, session ID, file reference/hash, or gateway-provided unique ID),
  - **filtering action** (allowed/modified/blocked/quarantined/replaced),
  - **filtering outcome** (policy pass/fail and/or final disposition),
  - **reason codes / rule name / policy version** sufficient for troubleshooting why content was modified or blocked.
- Ensure the same message identifier is preserved/propagated to downstream disposition records (e.g., quarantine portal, delivery logs, ticketing) so investigators can correlate.

### 3) Normalize and forward to SIEM/log platform with correlation
- In the logging pipeline (SIEM ingestion or log management), normalize the above fields into a consistent schema (e.g., `boundary_id`, `message_id`, `filter_action`, `filter_outcome`, `rule_name`, `policy_version`, `reason_code`, `timestamp`).
- Add enrichment where feasible (e.g., boundary name, source/destination domain, sender/recipient metadata) without breaking privacy requirements.

### 4) Ensure audit record generation and retention
- Verify that audit events are generated for both **action** and **outcome** per message and are retained according to organizational audit retention requirements.
- Protect audit logs with least privilege and integrity controls (e.g., restricted write access, tamper-evident storage, alerting on ingestion gaps).

### 5) Validate with automated test messages
- Create a small test suite that sends representative messages through each boundary with known expected dispositions:
  - one that should be **allowed**,
  - one that should be **modified/sanitized**,
  - one that should be **blocked/quarantined**.
- For each test, assert that SIEM contains a record with:
  - the correct `message_id`,
  - correct `filter_action`,
  - correct `filter_outcome`,
  - and a non-empty `reason_code`/`rule_name` for modified/blocked cases.
- Fail the validation if any message lacks action/outcome fields or cannot be correlated.

## WHO
- **Security Engineering / Platform Security Team**: boundary inventory, policy mapping, gateway/WAF configuration, log schema definition.
- **SIEM/Observability Engineering**: normalization, ingestion pipelines, correlation, retention/integrity settings.
- **Application Owners / Network Security Team**: confirm where cross-domain transfers occur and that message identifiers are preserved.

## Acceptance criteria
1. For each configured cross-domain boundary, a test message produces **at least one audit event** containing both:
   - `filter_action` and
   - `filter_outcome`,
   correlated to the **same per-message identifier**.
2. For modified/blocked/quarantined test messages, audit records include **troubleshooting fields** (e.g., `rule_name` and/or `reason_code` and `policy_version`).
3. Audit events are forwarded to the SIEM/log platform with a **consistent schema** and are retained for the required period.
4. Automated validation confirms correct correlation and disposition for allow/modify/block scenarios; no test message results in missing action/outcome fields.


### ac-4.27 — Redundant/Independent Filtering Mechanisms (Enhancement)

#### Active-active dual independent content filters per data type

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Months
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement redundant and independent content filtering mechanisms for each defined data type that crosses security domains (e.g., via secure email/web/file-transfer gateways or cross-domain API/data exchange points). Both mechanisms must actively inspect content and enforce policy decisions so that a single filtering failure cannot stop enforcement.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-4(27) requires that when transferring information between different security domains, content filtering solutions provide **redundant and independent filtering mechanisms for each data type**. Independence means different code base/libraries (e.g., different vendor stacks) and **multiple independent system processes**. Redundancy eliminates a single point of failure.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Identify cross-domain transfer points and data types**
   - Inventory each cross-domain path used by the application (e.g., secure email gateway, secure web gateway, secure file transfer/data-loss-prevention gateway, cross-domain API proxy).
   - For each path, define the **data types** to be filtered (e.g., email attachments, document uploads/downloads, file transfer payloads, specific MIME types, image/document categories, log exports).

2. **Define per-data-type filtering policy and decision rule**
   - For each data type, define the enforcement action and consensus rule, such as: **block/quarantine if either filter flags** (fail-closed) or another explicitly documented rule.
   - Ensure the policy is explicit about what happens when one filter is unavailable (must not allow bypass).

3. **Deploy two redundant, independent filtering engines per data type**
   - **Engine A**: implement content filtering using one vendor/product stack or separately developed code base and libraries.
   - **Engine B**: implement content filtering using a different vendor/product stack or separately developed code base and libraries.
   - Run **each engine as separate system processes/services** (separate containers/VMs, separate service accounts, separate update pipelines) so that a crash/dependency failure in one does not disable the other.
   - Ensure both engines receive the same content stream (or an equivalent canonical representation) for inspection.

4. **Configure fail-closed enforcement**
   - Configure the cross-domain enforcement point so that if **Engine A is down/unresponsive**, Engine B still enforces the policy for the affected data types (and vice versa).
   - Avoid “soft-fail” modes that allow traffic to pass when one engine fails.

5. **Implement audit logging for filtering decisions**
   - For each transfer, log at minimum: timestamp, source/destination domains, data type, content identifiers (hash/ID), which engine(s) evaluated, engine verdicts, and the final enforcement decision.
   - Forward logs to the enterprise SIEM and retain per organizational logging requirements.

6. **Operational independence and change control**
   - Maintain separate operational boundaries for Engine A and Engine B (distinct owners/teams if feasible, distinct deployment pipelines, and independent health checks).
   - Use configuration management/change control so that filter rules and engine configurations are versioned and reproducible.

## WHO
- **Security Architecture Lead**: defines per-data-type policy and consensus/fail-closed decision rule.
- **Platform/Network Security Engineer**: implements the dual-engine gateway/proxy configuration and fail-closed behavior.
- **Application Owner / Integration Engineer**: ensures the correct cross-domain transfer points and data-type mappings are wired to the gateway/proxy.
- **SOC/Monitoring Engineer**: validates SIEM ingestion, alerting, and audit log completeness.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. For each defined **data type** crossing a security domain, there are **two active filtering mechanisms** (Engine A and Engine B) that both inspect the content during normal operation.
2. Engines are **independent**: they use different code bases/libraries (e.g., different vendor stacks) and run as **separate processes/services**.
3. **Fail-closed** is demonstrated: when Engine A is intentionally stopped/unreachable, Engine B continues to enforce the policy (no bypass) for the affected data types; repeat with Engine B stopped.
4. Audit evidence exists for test transfers showing: both engines evaluated (or at least the surviving engine enforced), engine verdicts, and the final enforcement decision, with logs present in SIEM.
5. Configuration/rules are version-controlled and can be reproduced for audit (e.g., Git-backed config or equivalent configuration management evidence).

### ac-4.28 — Linear Filter Pipelines (Enhancement)

#### Non-bypassable linear content filtering at cross-domain gateway

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement a **single, sequential (linear) content filter pipeline** that is **non-bypassable** for **all cross-domain information transfers** (between security domains/levels) and ensure the **filter outcome is enforced using both discretionary and mandatory access controls**.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-4(28) enhancement requires that when transferring information between different security domains, the system **implements a linear content filter pipeline** that is **enforced with discretionary and mandatory access controls**. The guidance emphasizes that **linear pipelines** help avoid **bypass/non-invocation issues** that can occur with **parallel filtering architectures**.

## HOW (specific approach)
### 1) Define and scope “cross-domain transfer”
- Create an inventory of all information flows that cross security domains (e.g., web/API egress, file transfer, email, message bus topics, inter-service calls that cross trust boundaries).
- For each flow, define the **source domain**, **destination domain**, **protocol**, and **required inspection point**.
- Document which flows are in-scope for AC-4(28) and which are explicitly out-of-scope (with rationale).

### 2) Enforce a single choke point (non-bypassable invocation)
- Route all in-scope cross-domain traffic through a **single inspection gateway/proxy** (or an active/standby pair) that is the **only permitted path** between domains.
- Implement network controls so there is **no alternate route** that can deliver content without passing the gateway (e.g., firewall/security group/route-table/SD-WAN policy restrictions; disable or remove direct connectivity paths).
- Ensure the gateway is configured so the inspection pipeline is invoked for every transfer attempt (no conditional bypass paths).

### 3) Configure the gateway with a strictly linear pipeline
- Implement the content inspection as a **single ordered chain** (example stages; exact stages depend on your platform/policy):
  1. Normalize/defragment
  2. Decode/unwrap
  3. Classify (data type/sensitivity)
  4. Inspect (e.g., DLP/malware/content rules)
  5. Policy decision (mandatory + discretionary evaluation)
  6. Enforce action (allow/quarantine/block/strip)
- **Avoid parallel filtering branches** for the same content type. If the platform supports multiple engines, configure them so the overall decision is produced by a **single sequential workflow** and cannot be skipped.
- Configure **fail-closed** behavior: if inspection cannot complete, default to **block/quarantine**.

### 4) Bind discretionary and mandatory access controls to the same decision
- Discretionary enforcement: integrate the gateway with enterprise identity/authorization context (e.g., user/service account identity, role/ABAC attributes) to determine whether the sender/receiver is permitted to transfer the content.
- Mandatory enforcement: apply policy based on content classification and cross-domain rules (e.g., “confidential data may not leave Domain A to Domain B”, “regulated content must be quarantined”).
- Ensure **mandatory deny/quarantine cannot be overridden** by discretionary allow.

### 5) Produce audit evidence for every transfer attempt
- Log at the gateway for each attempt: source/destination domain, identity context, pipeline invocation status, classification/inspection results, mandatory decision, discretionary decision, and final enforcement action.
- Forward logs to SIEM with tamper-resistant retention per enterprise logging policy.

### 6) Document placement and guarantee of invocation
- Update the system security documentation (and/or SSP artifacts) with:
  - Where the gateway sits in the data path
  - How routing prevents bypass
  - The linear pipeline stage order
  - Fail-closed behavior
  - How discretionary and mandatory decisions are combined

## WHO
- **Security Architect / Compliance Engineer**: define scope, decision logic requirements, and acceptance criteria.
- **Network/Security Engineering**: implement routing/firewall rules to make the gateway the only path.
- **Platform/Application Security Engineer**: configure the gateway linear pipeline and policy decision enforcement.
- **SIEM/Logging Engineer**: ensure audit logging, forwarding, and retention.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. **Scope coverage**: All in-scope cross-domain flows are routed through the inspection gateway (validated via network flow/routing review and traffic tests).
2. **Non-bypassable**: No alternate network path exists that allows payload delivery without gateway inspection (validated by attempting direct connections and confirming blocked/failed attempts).
3. **Linear pipeline**: Gateway inspection is configured as a single ordered chain; there is no parallel branch that can bypass inspection for the same content type (validated by configuration review and test cases that would fail if bypass were possible).
4. **Always invoked**: For every transfer attempt, the gateway records pipeline invocation; if inspection fails, the system enforces **fail-closed** (block/quarantine) (validated by induced inspection failure tests).
5. **Discretionary + mandatory enforcement**: For test cases where discretionary would allow but mandatory denies, the final action is deny/quarantine; discretionary cannot override mandatory (validated by policy test matrix).
6. **Audit evidence**: For each test transfer attempt, logs include pipeline invocation, inspection/classification outcome, mandatory decision, discretionary decision, and final enforcement action (validated by SIEM query and log completeness checks).

### ac-4.29 — Filter Orchestration Engines (Enhancement)

#### Policy-driven orchestration for cross-domain content filtering

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement a cross-domain “content filter orchestration engine” that executes content filtering actions in an approved, deterministic sequence for every cross-domain transfer, distinguishes orchestration errors (anomalous actions/unexpected termination) from policy non-compliance, and emits successful completion reports in the required order.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-4(29) Filter Orchestration Engines** requires that when transferring information between different security domains, the organization **employ content filter orchestration engines** to ensure that filtering is **coordinated/sequenced**, **completes successfully**, and produces **content filter reports** showing expected filtering actions completed successfully, including correct sequencing. The guidance defines **errors** as anomalous actions or unexpected termination of the content filter process (not merely content being rejected for policy reasons).

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create workflow definitions per transfer type**
   - For each cross-domain transfer type supported by the application (e.g., file upload, message relay, API payload forwarding), define an **approved step sequence** that maps to the organization’s approved cross-domain data handling policy (AC-04.29_odp “policy for content-filtering actions is defined”).
   - Represent the sequence as a workflow graph with explicit ordering constraints (e.g., scan/classify → policy decision → redact/quarantine or allow → deliver → finalize report).

2. **Implement a single orchestration entrypoint for all cross-domain transfers**
   - Route all cross-domain transfer requests through one orchestration service/component (the “engine”) rather than ad-hoc/manual chaining.
   - The engine must instantiate a **workflow run** per transfer using the workflow definition version tied to the approved policy.

3. **Enforce correct sequencing at runtime**
   - Use workflow state management so that each step can only execute when prior required steps have completed successfully.
   - Add guards that prevent out-of-order execution (e.g., step preconditions based on workflow state).

4. **Implement error semantics required by the guidance**
   - Treat as **orchestration errors**: anomalous actions and **unexpected termination** of the content filter process.
   - Treat as **policy outcomes** (not orchestration errors): content being blocked/quarantined due to policy non-compliance.
   - On orchestration error, the engine must **stop further “success” reporting** and record the failure state.

5. **Generate completion evidence in the required sequence**
   - Produce a **final completion report** per transfer plus stage-level reports (if used) that include:
     - Transfer ID / correlation ID
     - Workflow definition version (and policy mapping version)
     - Step-by-step status and timestamps
     - Explicit indication of whether the workflow completed successfully vs failed due to orchestration error
   - Ensure the report content reflects the **actual execution path** and that “successful completion” evidence is emitted only when the workflow reaches the defined successful terminal state.

6. **Make execution records audit-ready and tamper-evident**
   - Forward orchestration logs and completion reports to the organization’s immutable/audit log mechanism (e.g., write-once storage or cryptographically signed records) and retain per enterprise logging retention requirements.

7. **Operationalize via CI/CD and change control**
   - Store workflow definitions and policy mappings in version control.
   - Require change management approval for workflow/policy changes so the engine continues to follow the approved cross-domain data handling policy.

## WHO
- **Application/Integration Architect**: defines workflow sequences per transfer type and maps them to the approved cross-domain data handling policy.
- **Software Engineer / Integration Engineer**: implements the orchestration engine, runtime sequencing enforcement, error semantics, and report generation.
- **Security/Compliance Engineer**: validates that orchestration error vs policy non-compliance semantics align with AC-4(29) guidance and that evidence meets audit expectations.
- **DevOps/Platform Engineer**: integrates immutable logging, monitoring/alerting, and CI/CD controls.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. For each supported cross-domain transfer type, the orchestration engine executes filtering steps in the **approved order** defined in the workflow.
2. If the content filter process terminates unexpectedly or an anomalous action occurs, the engine records an **orchestration failure** and does **not** emit a “successful completion” report.
3. For successful runs, the engine emits a **completion report** (and stage reports if configured) that includes step statuses/timestamps and matches the **required sequence**.
4. Every completion report is traceable to a unique transfer/correlation ID and includes the workflow/policy version used.
5. Orchestration execution evidence is retained in an audit-ready, tamper-evident manner (per enterprise logging controls).

### ac-4.30 — Filter Mechanisms Using Multiple Processes (Enhancement)

#### Dual-engine content filtering for cross-domain transfers

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement content filtering for cross-domain information transfers using **multiple independent processes** (at least two filtering engines/stages) so that a transfer is **not allowed** if one process fails or is bypassed.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-4(30)** (Enhancement) requires: “When transferring information between different security domains, implement content filtering mechanisms using multiple processes.” The guidance states this reduces the likelihood of a **single point of failure**.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Identify cross-domain transfer paths
- Inventory all application-mediated flows that move data between security domains (e.g., web/API gateway, email relay, file transfer service, message bus topics/queues, SaaS connector, secure access gateway).
- For each path, define the source/destination security domains and the enforcement point(s).

### 2) Deploy/enable a multi-process filtering enforcement pattern
- At the enforcement point, configure **two independent filtering processes** (separate services/processes with distinct rule engines and log identifiers). Examples of independence patterns:
  - Engine A: malware scanning (or sandbox detonation)
  - Engine B: DLP/content classification (or URL/content policy)
- Ensure the engines are not a single shared component (e.g., not the same underlying service instance with only different rule toggles).

### 3) Enforce “AND/Quorum” decision logic with fail-closed behavior
- Configure the transfer decision so the final verdict is **allow only if both engines complete successfully** and meet policy.
- If Engine A or Engine B:
  - fails to respond,
  - is unhealthy,
  - times out,
  - returns an error,
  - or is administratively disabled for the path,
  then the system must **block or quarantine** the transfer (no bypass).

### 4) Implement verifiable audit evidence per transfer attempt
- For each transfer attempt, generate audit records that include at minimum:
  - transfer identifier (message ID / transaction ID / file hash / request ID),
  - source and destination security domains,
  - per-engine verdicts (Engine A result, Engine B result),
  - per-engine status (completed/failed/timeout) and timestamps,
  - final decision (allow/block/quarantine).
- Ensure logs are forwarded to the enterprise SIEM with consistent fields for correlation.

### 5) Document the multi-process filtering design and failure handling
- Update the system security plan and architecture/design documentation to describe:
  - which enforcement point(s) apply to which cross-domain paths,
  - the two independent filtering processes and their independence rationale,
  - the AND/quorum logic and fail-closed behavior,
  - operational response when one engine fails (e.g., SOC notification, incident ticketing, temporary quarantine behavior).

### 6) Operational ownership and change control
- Assign operational ownership for the gateway/enforcement point and each filtering engine.
- Ensure configuration changes to filtering engines or decision logic are controlled and traceable (so the multi-process requirement cannot silently degrade).

## WHO
- **Application/Platform Security Engineer**: designs the multi-process filtering architecture and decision logic.
- **Gateway/Integration Engineer**: implements configuration and integration with the enforcement point(s).
- **SOC/Monitoring Lead**: validates alerting and audit log ingestion; monitors engine health and quarantine events.
- **System Owner / Authorizing Official (or delegate)**: ensures the approach is reflected in the system security plan and approved.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. For at least one representative cross-domain transfer path, the enforcement point performs **two independent filtering processes** and records per-engine verdicts.
2. If one engine is forced into failure (e.g., stopped, misconfigured to error, or timed out), the final outcome is **block or quarantine** (no allow).
3. For a sample of transfer attempts, audit records in SIEM show:
   - transfer ID,
   - both engine statuses/verdicts,
   - final decision,
   - timestamps sufficient to prove both processes were invoked.
4. System security plan/architecture documentation explicitly states the multi-process filtering design and fail-closed behavior for cross-domain transfers.



### ac-4.31 — Failed Content Transfer Prevention (Enhancement)

#### Block failed cross-domain transfers via reject-and-quarantine gateway

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement an enforcement mechanism at every cross-domain information transfer boundary so that any content that fails required filtering/validation checks is **rejected** and **never delivered** to the receiving security domain. Optionally store failed artefacts in a **quarantine** location that is not reachable from the receiving domain.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-4(31) Failed Content Transfer Prevention** requires that when transferring information between different security domains, the organization **prevents the transfer of failed content to the receiving domain**. Guidance notes that content failing filtering checks can corrupt the system if transferred.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define in-scope transfer boundaries and choke points
- Identify each application-to-application, application-to-messaging, API, file, and data-pipeline path that crosses security domains (e.g., prod→partner, SaaS→on-prem, IT→OT, dev→prod if treated as separate domains).
- For each path, ensure there is a single enforceable boundary component (gateway/proxy/relay/ingress service) that all traffic must traverse.

### 2) Enforce “validate before accept”
- Configure the boundary component to perform all required checks **before** the receiving domain accepts/queues/stores the payload.
- Implement checks appropriate to the transfer type, such as:
  - Malware scanning / content disarm and reconstruction (where applicable)
  - File type/size limits and schema validation
  - DLP/classification checks (e.g., allowed destinations for data labels)
  - Integrity/signature verification for signed artefacts
  - API request/response validation (JSON schema, required fields, canonicalization)

### 3) Implement reject-and-quarantine behavior
- On any validation failure, the boundary must:
  - Return a hard failure to the sender workflow (e.g., HTTP 4xx/5xx with non-sensitive error details; SMTP/file transfer rejection).
  - Ensure the payload is **not written** to any receiving-side inbox/share/queue/topic/storage that would make it available to the receiving domain.
- If quarantine is used:
  - Store failed artefacts in a quarantine repository with strict access controls limited to security operations.
  - Ensure the receiving domain cannot retrieve or process quarantined content.

### 4) Make bypass difficult and detectable
- Block direct routes that could bypass the boundary (network rules, service routing, firewall policies, API gateway enforcement).
- Add detections for bypass attempts and repeated failures (e.g., traffic to destination endpoints that does not traverse the gateway; spikes in blocked transfer events).

### 5) Produce audit evidence for “attempted → blocked → not delivered”
- Log a single correlated event per transfer attempt including:
  - Transfer ID, sender identity/service, destination domain, interface/protocol
  - Validation rule(s) triggered and failure reason category
  - Final outcome explicitly indicating **blocked/rejected/not delivered**
- Ensure logs are forwarded to the SIEM and retained per enterprise logging requirements.

### 6) Operationalize configuration control
- Manage gateway/boundary rules as version-controlled configuration (policy-as-code) with change approvals.
- Maintain a documented mapping of each in-scope transfer to its enforcing boundary component and validation ruleset.

## WHO
- **Application Owner / Integration Architect**: identifies in-scope cross-domain transfers and defines required validation rules per transfer type.
