3. Devices with **strong full-disk encryption** result in **cryptographic erase** (verified by MDM event/action type and/or OS/MDM-reported sanitization method).
4. Devices without strong full-disk encryption result in an **approved purge/wipe** method aligned to **NIST SP 800-88 Rev. 1** (verified by MDM event/action type).
5. Audit logs/events exist for each wipe attempt, containing at minimum: device identifier, timestamp, wipe method/type, and outcome status, and are retained in the SIEM/log store.
6. The wipe trigger is based on **device unlock failures**, not application/account login failures (verified by attempting app sign-in failures without triggering device wipe).


### ac-7.3 — Biometric Attempt Limiting (Enhancement)

#### Enforce max 5 unsuccessful biometric attempts in IdP auth flow

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Days
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Configure the centralized enterprise identity provider (IdP) and the application’s biometric sign-in integration so that **no more than {{ac-07.03_odp}} (default: 5) unsuccessful biometric logon attempts** are allowed. After the threshold is reached, **further biometric attempts are blocked/denied** and the user is directed to the **documented post-limit behavior**.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-7(3)** enhancement requires: “**Limit the number of unsuccessful biometric logon attempts to {{ insert: param, ac-07.03_odp }}**.” Guidance notes biometrics are probabilistic and organizations must select an appropriate attempt limit and define what happens after the limit is reached.

## HOW (specific steps)
1. **Identify the biometric entry points** used for this application’s authentication (e.g., Windows Hello for Business, mobile biometric sign-in, biometric-capable login UI) and confirm they ultimately authenticate through the **enterprise IdP**.
2. In the IdP authentication policy, set the **biometric unsuccessful attempt limit** to **{{ac-07.03_odp}}** (default 5). Use the IdP’s equivalent setting (names vary, e.g., factor retry limit / max auth attempts / MFA attempt limit / step-up failure threshold).
3. Configure **post-limit behavior** to match documented organizational procedures (e.g., deny biometric factor and require an alternative authentication method such as password + MFA, or trigger account lockout per existing access control policy). Ensure the user-facing outcome is deterministic once the threshold is reached.
4. Ensure **audit logging** is enabled for authentication failures and that logs include:
   - user identifier
   - timestamp
   - failure outcome for biometric verification
   - a distinct event indicating the **attempt limit reached / biometric blocked**
5. Implement **configuration management evidence**:
   - apply the setting via controlled change management (or policy-as-code/IaC if supported)
   - capture configuration exports/snapshots showing the configured value and last-modified metadata
6. Update/confirm documentation artifacts for auditability:
   - access control / biometric procedure explicitly states the **{{ac-07.03_odp}} unsuccessful attempt limit** and the **post-limit behavior**
   - system security plan and system design reference the IdP enforcement mechanism for biometric attempt limiting

## WHO
- **IAM/Identity Engineer**: implement IdP policy setting, post-limit behavior, and audit logging configuration.
- **Application Security/Architect**: verify the application’s biometric integration routes through the IdP and that the user experience aligns with documented post-limit behavior.
- **Security Compliance/Assessor Liaison**: validate evidence package (policy config export, change record, and log examples).

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. The IdP configuration enforces **{{ac-07.03_odp}} unsuccessful biometric attempts** for the in-scope biometric login flow.
2. After the {{ac-07.03_odp}}-th unsuccessful attempt, **additional biometric attempts are blocked/denied** and the user is routed to the **documented** alternative behavior.
3. Authentication audit logs show **each unsuccessful biometric attempt** and include a **distinct “limit reached/blocked”** event.
4. Evidence exists showing the configured value equals **{{ac-07.03_odp}}** (default 5) and is traceable to a controlled change (ticket/change record and configuration export/snapshot).
5. Documentation (procedure + security plan/design) explicitly references the limit value and enforcement point (IdP).

### ac-7.4 — Use of Alternate Authentication Factor (Enhancement)

#### IdP alternate-factor recovery with 5-in-15 rolling throttle

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Days
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Configure the enterprise identity provider (IdP) to allow **only the approved alternate authentication factors**—**(1) FIDO2/WebAuthn hardware security keys** and **(2) OTP from an approved authenticator app**—to be used **only after** a user exceeds the organization-defined consecutive invalid-attempt threshold for their **primary** authentication factor. When alternate factors are used, enforce a **rolling limit of 5 consecutive invalid alternate-factor attempts within 15 minutes**, counting failures during alternate authentication and blocking further alternate attempts until the rolling window clears.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-7(4) enhancement guidance requires that alternate authentication factors:
- Support **availability** by allowing recovery from lockout.
- Allow a user to bypass lockout using **additional authentication factors**.
- Be constrained so that alternate-factor use does not enable unlimited guessing; specifically, the organization-defined parameters require enforcement of a **limit on consecutive invalid logon attempts** for alternate factors (e.g., **5** attempts in **15 minutes**) and a defined **time period**.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Identify primary vs alternate factors per user/app**
   - In the IdP, define which factor(s) are considered the user’s **primary authentication factor(s)** for the target application(s).
   - Ensure the alternate-factor policy is triggered only when the alternate factor is **different from the primary** (e.g., if primary is password, alternate cannot be password; if primary is security key, alternate cannot be security key).

2. **Define the approved alternate factors** (use the organization-defined parameters)
   - Configure the IdP to allow only:
     - **Hardware security key (FIDO2/WebAuthn)**
     - **OTP from an approved authenticator app**
   - Disable/deny any other alternate factors (e.g., SMS OTP, email OTP, knowledge-based recovery) for this recovery path.

3. **Gate alternate-factor availability after primary threshold**
   - Implement logic: after the user exceeds the organization-defined **consecutive invalid attempts** for the **primary** factor, the IdP permits authentication using the approved alternate factors.
   - Ensure the “moment” alternate factors become available is auditable.

4. **Enforce rolling alternate-factor throttling**
   - Implement a counter that increments on **each failed alternate authentication attempt**.
   - Enforce: **block further alternate-factor attempts after 5 consecutive invalid attempts within any rolling 15-minute period**.
   - Ensure the counter behavior is rolling (not fixed windows) and resets only when the rolling 15-minute window clears.

5. **Audit logging and SIEM export**
   - Enable audit logs/events sufficient to verify:
     - failed primary attempts and the point at which alternate factors become available
     - each failed alternate authentication attempt
     - the timestamps used to validate the **rolling 15-minute window**
     - the block/unblock events for alternate-factor attempts
   - Export to SIEM and retain per enterprise logging retention requirements.

6. **Operational documentation for recovery**
   - Create/maintain runbooks for:
     - user self-service recovery steps using the approved alternate factors
     - helpdesk escalation when alternate-factor throttling blocks access (e.g., wait for rolling window to clear; re-enrollment steps for authenticator app/security key)
   - Document the exact behavior: when alternate factors are enabled, and the 5-in-15 rolling enforcement.

## WHO
- **Identity & Access Management (IAM) Engineer**: implement IdP configuration/policy-as-code and validate auth flows.
- **Security Operations / SOC**: validate SIEM event mapping, alerting (optional), and retention.
- **Application Owner / Security Architect**: confirm primary vs alternate factor definitions for the application(s) and approve recovery runbooks.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. **Alternate factors are restricted**: Only FIDO2/WebAuthn security keys and authenticator-app OTP are accepted as alternate factors for the recovery path; other factors are rejected.
2. **Different-from-primary enforced**: If a user’s primary factor is X, alternate factor X is not accepted during alternate recovery.
3. **Primary threshold gating works**: Alternate factors become available only after the user exceeds the organization-defined consecutive invalid attempts for the primary factor.
4. **Rolling throttle enforced**: After **5** consecutive failed alternate attempts within any **rolling 15-minute** period, the IdP blocks further alternate attempts; after the rolling window clears, alternate attempts are allowed again.
5. **Failures are counted during alternate auth**: Each failed alternate attempt increments the alternate-factor failure counter.
6. **Audit evidence exists**: Logs show timestamps and outcomes sufficient to reconstruct the 5-in-15 rolling window and the transition from primary failure to alternate availability.
7. **Tested in non-prod and promoted**: End-to-end tests (including timing-based cases) pass in test/staging before production rollout.

### ac-8 — System Use Notification (Control)

#### IdP pre-auth warning with required acknowledgement gate

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Days
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Configure the enterprise identity provider (IdP) to display an **approved system use notification banner** to **human users** before granting access via the login interface, and to require **explicit acknowledgement** (e.g., checkbox/button) before the user can proceed. For publicly accessible access paths, also display the **approved public access notice** with privacy-consistent limits and authorized-use language.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-8 System Use Notification** requires that system use notifications be displayed **before granting access via logon interfaces with human users**, and that organizations consider whether a **secondary/public notice** is needed for access after initial network logon. The guidance also requires that the banner remains until the user **acknowledges the conditions** and takes an **explicit action** to log on/continue. Banner content must be **approved** (privacy office input and legal review).

This solution directly implements the “messages or warning banners displayed before individuals log in” and the “remains on screen until acknowledged and explicit action to proceed” requirements at the centralized authentication layer.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Obtain and version banner content**
   - Create two approved text variants:
     - **System use notification** (includes: US Government system identity; monitoring/recording/auditing; unauthorized use prohibited; consent to monitoring/recording; penalties).
     - **Public access notice** (includes: monitoring/recording limited to security/fraud prevention; no unauthorized profiling; authorized uses only; privacy-consistent limits and authorized-use description).
   - Route for **privacy office input** and **legal review/approval**; store approvals and the **banner version/hash** for audit.

2. **Implement acknowledgement gate in the IdP login flow**
   - In the IdP (e.g., Microsoft Entra ID / Okta / Ping), configure a **pre-authentication step** (or required consent/terms step) that:
     - Renders the **approved banner** before credentials are accepted or before the session is issued.
     - Requires an **explicit user action** (e.g., “I acknowledge and consent” checkbox + “Continue” button).
     - Prevents progression until acknowledgement is completed (no auto-redirect).
   - Ensure the banner is shown for **all human user login paths** that grant access to the application (including admin portals and any SSO entry points).

3. **Apply correct banner variant based on access context**
   - Configure routing/conditional logic so that:
     - Internal/authenticated access uses the **system use notification**.
     - Publicly accessible entry points (where applicable) show the **public access notice** (and then the system use notification as required by your access flow design).

4. **Multi-language support (where needed)**
   - If organizational needs require multiple languages, implement locale-based rendering while ensuring the **approved text content** (and version) remains consistent.

5. **Record acknowledgement evidence for auditability**
   - Ensure the IdP authentication/audit logs capture at minimum:
     - User identifier, timestamp, banner version/hash, and a flag indicating **acknowledgement completed**.
   - Forward IdP logs to the SIEM/logging platform and retain per enterprise logging retention policy.

6. **Document the integration**
   - Maintain system design/configuration documentation describing:
     - Where the banner is displayed (IdP pre-auth step),
     - Which login interfaces are covered,
     - How the acknowledgement gate blocks access,
     - How public access notice is selected (if applicable).

## WHO
- **Identity & Access Management (IAM) Engineer**: implement IdP configuration and logging integration.
- **Privacy Officer**: review privacy-consistent messaging for the public notice.
- **General Counsel / Legal Counsel**: approve banner content.
- **Security/Compliance Analyst**: verify audit evidence and maintain control mapping.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. For every human login interface covered by SSO/IdP, the approved **system use notification** is displayed **before access is granted**.
2. The banner remains visible until the user performs an **explicit acknowledgement** and clicks **Continue/Log in**; users cannot proceed without acknowledgement.
3. For publicly accessible entry points (where applicable), the approved **public access notice** is displayed and includes the required privacy-consistent limits and authorized-use language.
4. IdP logs/SIEM records show acknowledgement completion with **banner version/hash** and timestamp for each successful login.
5. Banner content approvals (privacy + legal) and the implemented banner version are documented and retrievable for audit.


### ac-9 — Previous Logon Notification (Control)

#### Show last successful logon date/time on IdP interactive sign-in

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** High
- **Effort:** Days
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Configure the enterprise Identity Provider (IdP) interactive sign-in experience to display, immediately after a **successful human logon**, the **date and time of the user’s most recent successful logon**.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-9** requires: “Notify the user, upon successful logon to the system, of the date and time of the last logon.” The control guidance further specifies this is for **human user interfaces** and that the notification helps users recognize inconsistencies with their last access.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Identify the authoritative logon source**
   - Ensure the IdP maintains per-user **last successful sign-in** timestamps (not failed attempts).
   - Confirm the IdP field is updated only on successful authentication.

2. **Enable/implement the sign-in notification in the IdP UI flow**
   - In the IdP’s interactive sign-in experience (e.g., sign-in page customization / sign-in policy / authentication flow customization), add a post-success message that renders:
     - **Last successful logon date**
     - **Last successful logon time**
   - Ensure the message is shown **only after successful authentication** (not on error/denied pages).

3. **Standardize formatting and time zone**
   - Define an organization rule for display (e.g., include year, minutes, and a configured time zone such as UTC or the organization’s standard time zone).
   - Apply that rule in the IdP template so the message is consistent across applications using the IdP.

4. **Scope to all human UI access paths using the IdP**
   - Verify all web/portal/mobile app sign-ins for this application route through the IdP interactive flow.
   - For any access path that bypasses the IdP (e.g., legacy local auth), document as out-of-scope for this implementation and handle via a compensating approach (separate item).

5. **Evidence generation for auditability**
   - Capture evidence that the notification displays the correct **date and time** after successful login:
     - Automated UI test screenshots for a test user.
     - Correlation to IdP audit/sign-in logs showing the same last successful sign-in timestamp.
   - Store evidence per environment (prod/test) with change/version identifiers.

6. **Operationalize via configuration management**
   - Manage the sign-in template/policy as code (where supported) or via controlled configuration exports.
   - Ensure changes follow the organization’s change management process.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **IAM/Identity Engineer**: implement IdP sign-in customization and time formatting.
- **Application Owner / Security Engineer**: validate that the application’s human UI login paths use the IdP.
- **QA / Automation Engineer**: implement UI tests and evidence capture.
- **Compliance/Audit Liaison**: review evidence package for AC-9.

## Acceptance criteria
1. After a **successful** login via the application’s human UI (through the IdP), the user sees a message containing **both date and time** of their **most recent successful logon**.
2. The displayed timestamp matches the IdP’s authoritative **last successful sign-in** record for that user (within the defined formatting/time zone rules).
3. The message does **not** appear on failed logon attempts or authentication errors.
4. The behavior is consistent across all application login entry points that use the IdP interactive sign-in flow.
5. Evidence exists (screenshots and correlated IdP logs) demonstrating correct display in at least one non-production environment and (after release) production.

### ac-9.1 — Unsuccessful Logons (Enhancement)

#### Per-user failed-login counter reset and post-success notification

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** High
- **Effort:** Days
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement AC-9(1) by maintaining a per-user count of **unsuccessful logon attempts since the last successful logon**, resetting it to **zero immediately after a successful logon**, and **notifying the user upon successful logon** with the number of failures since the last success.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-9(1) requires: **“Notify the user, upon successful logon, of the number of unsuccessful logon attempts since the last successful logon.”** This enables users to recognize inconsistencies between expected and observed login behavior.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define the “unsuccessful logon” event and correlation key
- Use the centralized identity provider (IdP) or authentication service’s event data for **failed authentication**.
- Correlate events to a **user identifier** (e.g., immutable user ID / subject claim), not a mutable username.

### 2) Implement a per-user counter store with atomic increment/reset
- Create a secure counter store (e.g., managed Redis/SQL) with:
  - Key: `userId`
  - Value: `failedAttemptsSinceLastSuccess`
  - Atomic operations:
    - `INCR` on each failed authentication
    - `SET 0` (or delete+recreate) on each successful authentication
- Ensure the counter update is **transactionally consistent** with the authentication outcome (at minimum: increment on failure events; reset on success events).

### 3) Wire authentication events to counter updates
- Subscribe to IdP/authentication events (webhooks/event bus) for:
  - **Authentication failure** → increment counter for that user.
  - **Authentication success** → read current counter value, then reset to 0.
- Ensure the “read then reset” sequence uses a single atomic operation pattern where possible (e.g., Lua script/transaction) so the displayed value matches the most recent period.

### 4) Inject the counter value into the successful login session
- After a successful logon, pass the counter value (captured before reset) into the session as a user-visible message parameter.
- Render the notification **immediately after successful sign-in** in the application’s post-login landing page (or IdP-hosted page if the login UI is IdP-managed).
- Display only to the authenticated user (no shared/admin visibility).

### 5) Ensure correct “since last successful logon” period semantics
- The counter must be reset on every successful logon, so subsequent failures accumulate only until the next success.
- If multiple concurrent sessions are possible, ensure the reset occurs on the success event and the displayed value corresponds to the counter value at that success moment.

### 6) Produce audit evidence
- Log (to SIEM/audit log) at least:
  - userId, timestamp, success/failure event type
  - counter value used for the notification
  - reset action timestamp
- Retain logs per existing enterprise log retention policy.

## WHO
- **Application/Identity Engineer**: implement event subscriptions, counter store, session injection, and UI notification.
- **Security Engineer**: review audit logging, privacy considerations, and ensure only the authenticated user receives the message.
- **IAM/IdP Administrator**: configure IdP event emission and hosted login customization (if applicable).

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. For a given user, after a successful logon, the UI displays **N**, where **N equals the number of failed authentication attempts since the user’s previous successful logon**.
2. After the successful logon, the stored counter for that user is **reset to 0** (verified via logs or controlled test environment).
3. Each subsequent failed attempt increments the counter by 1 until the next successful logon.
4. The notification is shown **every time** a user successfully logs in and is shown **only to that user**.
5. Audit logs exist for each success notification showing the counter value used and the reset action.
6. End-to-end test demonstrates correct behavior across at least: normal login, multiple consecutive failures, and success after failures.


### ac-9.2 — Successful and Unsuccessful Logons (Enhancement)

#### Post-login user notification of last 24h success/failure counts

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** High
- **Effort:** Days
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
When a user successfully logs on, the application (via the centralized identity/auth layer) must notify the user of:
- the number of **successful logons** during the configured time period (**ac-09.02_odp.01 = both**), and
- the number of **unsuccessful logon attempts** during the configured time period (**ac-09.02_odp.02 = 24 hours**),
with both values shown **immediately after the successful logon**.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 AC-9(2) requires: “Notify the user, upon successful logon, of the number of {{ insert: param, ac-09.02_odp.01 }} during {{ insert: param, ac-09.02_odp.02 }}.”
The guidance explains that showing these counts helps users recognize whether the pattern of logons is consistent with their activity.

## HOW (specific approach)
Implement a centralized, auditable “post-login notification” feature that computes rolling counts from authoritative authentication events and renders them in the user’s post-auth experience.

### 1) Define the event source and what counts as “unsuccessful”
- Use the enterprise identity/access system’s authoritative sign-in/audit event stream (e.g., IdP sign-in logs) as the source of truth.
- Ensure the event stream includes outcome (success/failure) and a timestamp.
- Define (in configuration) what constitutes “unsuccessful logon attempts” for your environment (e.g., failed password, failed MFA, denied/blocked sign-in). Use the same definition for all users.

### 2) Compute rolling 24-hour counts at successful login time
- On each **successful** authentication event for user U at time T, compute:
  - `success_count = count(success events for U where event_time in [T-24h, T])`
  - `failure_count = count(failure events for U where event_time in [T-24h, T])`
- Use event timestamps from the authoritative log source (not client time) to avoid clock skew.
- Handle concurrency by computing counts at the moment of the successful login event (or within a short bounded window) and using the same T reference.

### 3) Inject the computed values into the post-login user experience
- Immediately after successful login, render a notification (banner/modal) on the application landing page (or the IdP-provided post-login page) that displays both counts together.
- The notification must be shown only after successful authentication.
- Ensure the values are passed securely (e.g., via server-side session variables or signed tokens/claims) so users cannot tamper with the numbers.

### 4) Provide auditability/evidence
- Log an internal audit record for each successful login notification that includes:
  - user identifier (or stable subject ID),
  - successful login timestamp T,
  - computed `success_count` and `failure_count`,
  - correlation ID linking the notification to the successful auth event.
- Retain logs according to enterprise logging retention standards.

### 5) Apply to all relevant interactive logon paths
- Ensure the notification triggers for every user-facing authentication flow that results in a successful logon to this application (web, mobile, SSO redirect flows, etc.).
- Exclude only non-user/service-account flows if your organization defines them as out of scope; otherwise include them consistently.

## WHO
- **Application Security Engineer / IAM Engineer**: implement the event query + count computation and the secure injection into the post-login UI.
- **Application Owner**: approve the user-facing wording and ensure the notification is enabled for the application’s supported login flows.
- **SOC/SIEM Engineer**: validate the event source mapping and retention/audit evidence.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. For a test user, after a successful login at time T, the UI displays:
   - the number of successful logons in the prior 24 hours, and
   - the number of unsuccessful logon attempts in the prior 24 hours,
   matching counts computed from the authoritative authentication event logs.
2. The notification appears **only** after successful authentication (no display after failed logins).
3. The displayed counts use the configured time period **24 hours** and are based on authoritative event timestamps.
4. For each successful login notification, an internal audit log entry exists with the computed counts and a correlation ID to the successful auth event.
5. The implementation works across the application’s supported interactive login paths (at least the primary web login flow and one additional supported flow, if applicable).

### ac-9.3 — Notification of Account Changes (Enhancement)

#### IdP sign-in notification digest for security account changes (24h)

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement an automated “account change digest” that is shown to the user immediately after each **successful logon**, listing any changes to the user’s **security-related characteristics or parameters** that occurred within the configured **time period**.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST **AC-9(3)** (Enhancement) requires: **“Notify the user, upon successful logon, of changes to {{param ac-09.03_odp.01}} during {{param ac-09.03_odp.02}}.”**
The organization-defined parameters specify:
- **ac-09.03_odp.01**: security-related characteristics/parameters (e.g., **MFA status, password reset events, privilege/group membership, security policy/role assignments**)
- **ac-09.03_odp.02**: **past 24 hours**

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
Implement at the **central enterprise Identity Provider (IdP)** so it applies consistently across the enterprise application(s) that use centralized authentication.

### 1) Define authoritative event sources and mappings
- Identify the IdP/audit sources that record authoritative changes for:
  - **MFA status/method changes**
  - **Password/credential reset events**
  - **Privilege/group membership changes**
  - **Security policy/role assignment changes** (map to the closest available IdP constructs such as role assignments, group-based access, conditional access/policy changes that affect the user)
- Create an **event mapping** document that maps each required change type to the exact IdP audit event(s) and fields (e.g., event type, timestamp, target user, actor/admin, affected role/group/policy).

### 2) Implement sign-in-time hook/workflow
- Configure an IdP **post-authentication hook/action** that triggers on **successful logon**.
- In the hook:
  1. Capture the **successful logon timestamp** and the **user identifier**.
  2. Query the authoritative audit/event store for events where:
     - **target user = current user**
     - **event timestamp is within [logon_time - 24h, logon_time]**
  3. Filter to only the mapped **security-related characteristics/parameters** from ac-09.03_odp.01.
  4. Generate a concise digest containing (at minimum):
     - Change category (MFA / password reset / group/privilege / policy/role)
     - When it occurred (timestamp)
     - What changed (e.g., MFA enabled/disabled, group/role name, policy/role assignment)
     - (If available) actor/admin who made the change
  5. If no qualifying changes exist, show a “No security-related account changes in the last 24 hours” message (to avoid ambiguity).

### 3) Deliver the notification securely and consistently
- Render the digest in the **post-login user experience** (e.g., IdP post-login page or application landing page fed by the IdP result), ensuring:
  - It appears **immediately after successful logon**
  - It is delivered over the authenticated session (avoid unauthenticated email where spoofing risk is higher)
  - It does not expose sensitive details beyond what is necessary for user awareness

### 4) Ensure auditability and verifiability (evidence)
- Log the following as an auditable record each time the digest is generated:
  - user id
  - logon timestamp
  - time window used (start/end)
  - event IDs included (or a hash/reference list)
  - whether the digest was empty or populated
- Retain IdP configuration exports and the event mapping document as evidence.

### 5) Align with the organization’s security plan
- Update the system/security documentation to explicitly state:
  - the notification behavior
  - the authoritative event sources
  - the 24-hour time window
  - the user-facing delivery mechanism

## WHO
- **Identity & Access Management (IAM) Engineer**: implement IdP hook/action, event mappings, and notification rendering.
- **Security Architect/Compliance Lead**: validate that the mapped event categories satisfy ac-09.03_odp.01 and that the 24-hour window satisfies ac-09.03_odp.02; approve evidence package.
- **SOC/SIEM Engineer** (optional but recommended): ensure audit logs are forwarded and searchable for verification.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. For a test user, after a **successful logon**, the user receives a notification containing all qualifying **security-related account changes** that occurred in the **previous 24 hours**.
2. Changes outside the 24-hour window do **not** appear in the digest.
3. The digest content is derived from **authoritative IdP audit/event records** (event IDs/timestamps match the authoritative source).
4. Each digest generation produces an auditable record showing the time window and the event references used.
5. If no qualifying changes occurred, the user receives a clear “no changes” message (or equivalent) rather than an empty/failed notification.


### ac-9.4 — Additional Logon Information (Enhancement)

#### Post-success login banner with last location and device identifier

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** High
- **Effort:** Days
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement an immediate, on-screen notification shown to the user **upon successful logon** that displays the organization-defined additional information: **last successful logon location (IP address and approximate city/region) and device identifier**.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-9(4)** requires: “Notify the user, upon successful logon, of the following additional information: {{ insert: param, ac-09.04_odp }}.” The guidance clarifies that “user location” can be derived from system-determined information such as **IP addresses** and that the notification content is organization-defined.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
Implement the notification at the **central authentication layer** (recommended) so it triggers consistently and appears before the user can access application functionality.

1. **Define the exact notification fields and wording**
   - Use the organization-defined parameter **ac-09.04_odp** with content: “Last successful logon location (IP address and approximate city/region) and device identifier”.

2. **Capture authoritative sign-in context at successful authentication**
   - Ensure the identity system (IdP) or authentication service records, for each successful sign-in:
     - **Source IP address**
     - **Approximate city/region** derived from IP geolocation
     - A **device identifier** associated with the user’s last successful logon (e.g., managed device ID/name from endpoint management, or an IdP device claim from device registration).

3. **Compute “last successful logon” (not current, not failed)**
   - On each successful logon, retrieve the user’s **previous successful** sign-in record.
   - Populate the notification payload from that previous record:
     - IP address + approximate city/region
     - device identifier

4. **Render the notification immediately after successful logon**
   - Configure a **post-authentication action** (or equivalent) that displays the message on the first landing page/session after authentication.
   - Ensure the message is shown **before** redirecting the user to the application’s authenticated areas.

5. **Handle missing/unknown device or location data (without weakening the control)**
   - If device identifier or geolocation cannot be determined, display a controlled placeholder (e.g., “Unknown device” / “Unknown location”) while still showing the required fields structure.
   - Document the conditions under which placeholders occur.

6. **Generate auditable evidence**
   - Log an event that ties together:
     - user identifier
     - timestamp of successful logon
     - the notification payload fields (IP, city/region, device identifier) used for the message
     - correlation ID/session ID
   - Ensure logs are retained per enterprise logging retention policy so auditors can verify the notification was generated/displayed for successful logons.

7. **Document system design and configuration**
   - Update system security documentation to describe:
     - where the notification is generated (IdP/auth layer)
     - how “last successful logon” is determined
     - how IP geolocation and device identifier are obtained
     - how the message is displayed immediately after success
     - how evidence is logged and retained

## WHO
- **Identity & Access Management (IAM) Engineer**: implement IdP/auth-layer configuration, device claim mapping, and post-auth notification action.
- **Application Owner / Security Architect**: approve notification behavior and wording; ensure it appears before app access.
- **Security Operations / SIEM Engineer**: configure logging, correlation, and retention for audit evidence.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. For a successful user logon, the user is shown an on-screen message **immediately after successful authentication** (before access to authenticated application functions).
2. The message includes exactly the organization-defined content for **ac-09.04_odp**:
   - **Last successful logon location**: IP address and approximate city/region
   - **Device identifier**
3. The location/device values correspond to the user’s **previous successful** logon (not the current one and not failed attempts).
4. For each successful logon where the notification is triggered, an auditable log record exists containing the notification payload fields and correlation identifiers.
5. System documentation is updated describing the design/configuration and evidence generation for auditors.

### ac-10 — Concurrent Session Control (Control)

#### Enforce 2 concurrent sessions for privileged & mission-critical service accounts

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Days
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement a technical concurrency limit so that each **covered account** (per AC-10_odp.01) is prevented from having more than **2 concurrent sessions** (AC-10_odp.02) at any time.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-10 requires: **“Limit the number of concurrent sessions for each {{account and/or account types}} to {{number}}.”**
The guidance clarifies this applies to **system accounts** (privileged system accounts and service accounts), and the organization must define which accounts are covered and ensure the system enforces the limit.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define the covered identities (account scoping)
- Create/maintain an authoritative list of **covered accounts** in IAM (e.g., Entra ID/Okta groups or account-type tags):
  - Privileged system accounts (e.g., domain admins, local admins)
  - Service accounts for mission-critical applications
- Ensure the mapping is auditable (group membership history or attribute-based rules).

### 2) Configure IdP/session concurrency enforcement to cap at 2
- In the enterprise identity provider (IdP) used by the application (or the access path used by administrators/service accounts), configure **session concurrency control** for the covered identity sets:
  - Set **maximum concurrent sessions = 2** per account (or per identity) as required by AC-10_odp.02.
  - Configure deterministic behavior when the limit is reached (e.g., deny the new session and/or terminate the oldest session) and document which behavior is selected.
- Apply the rule to the relevant authentication flows used by the covered accounts (e.g., interactive sign-in, admin portal access, and any SSO flows used by service accounts where applicable).

### 3) Ensure enforcement is actually applied to the application/admin access paths
- Validate that the application and its admin functions rely on the IdP for authentication/session establishment.
- For any admin access path that bypasses the IdP (legacy protocols, direct local auth, etc.), route those access paths through an IdP-fronted gateway/broker where feasible (e.g., bastion/jump host with IdP integration) so concurrency enforcement is still effective.

### 4) Logging, monitoring, and evidence generation
- Enable logging for:
  - Successful session creation for covered accounts
  - Blocked/denied or terminated sessions due to concurrency limit
- Forward logs to SIEM and retain per enterprise log retention policy.
- Produce audit evidence:
  - Export/config snapshot of the concurrency policy configuration (policy-as-code if available)
  - Periodic configuration report showing the limit remains set to **2**
  - Test evidence (see acceptance criteria)

### 5) Operational procedures for exceptions and troubleshooting
- Create a runbook describing:
  - How to request a temporary exception (approval workflow)
  - How to verify current active sessions for a given covered account
  - How to respond when a third session attempt is blocked (e.g., identify and close stale sessions)

### 6) Testing (must be repeatable)
- Test with a covered account:
  - Start Session 1 and Session 2 (both must succeed)
  - Attempt Session 3 (must be blocked/denied or cause termination per the documented behavior)
- Repeat in at least one non-production environment and one production-like environment.

## WHO
- **IAM/Identity Engineer**: implement IdP concurrency policy and identity-to-group/account-type mapping.
- **Application Security/Platform Engineer**: confirm the application/admin access paths use the IdP and are not bypassing enforcement.
- **SOC/SIEM Analyst**: validate log ingestion and alerting for blocked/denied concurrency attempts.
- **System Owner/IT Ops Lead**: approve runbook and exception workflow.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. For every **covered account** defined in AC-10_odp.01, the system enforces **max 2 concurrent sessions** per AC-10_odp.02.
2. A third concurrent session attempt for a covered account is **prevented** (blocked/denied) or results in **termination** according to the documented behavior.
3. Evidence exists and is auditable:
   - IdP concurrency policy configuration showing the limit is set to **2**
   - Logs demonstrating blocked/denied (or terminated) third-session attempts
4. Testing results are recorded with timestamps and identifiers showing the 2/3-session behavior.
5. Runbook and exception process are documented and approved by the system owner.


### ac-11 — Device Lock (Control)

#### Enforce 15-min inactivity device lock with re-authentication

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Days
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Configure managed endpoint devices (and any user-facing app runtimes) to automatically initiate a **device lock after 15 minutes of inactivity**, and ensure that while locked, **no further logical access is possible** until the user **re-authenticates using the organization’s normal identification/authentication method**.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-11 Device Lock** requires temporary logical access to be prevented when users stop work and move away, by initiating a device lock after a defined inactivity period (here: **15 minutes**) and requiring normal re-authentication to regain access. Guidance also clarifies that device locks are **not an acceptable substitute for logout** when logout is required.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Endpoint OS configuration (primary)
1. **Select the managed endpoint control plane** (e.g., Microsoft Intune, Jamf, Google Endpoint Management) and standardize a baseline for all user endpoints that access the application.
2. **Set inactivity lock timer to 15 minutes**:
   - Configure the OS policy equivalent to “require device lock after inactivity” to **15 minutes** (ac-11_odp.02).
   - Ensure the inactivity timer is based on standard user activity signals (keyboard/mouse/touch) as supported by the OS.
3. **Require sign-in to unlock**:
   - Configure the OS so that unlocking the locked screen requires the **same normal sign-in method** used for the organization (password/PIN, smart card, or SSO-backed sign-in).
   - Explicitly disable any options that allow unlocking without sign-in (e.g., “unlock without sign-in,” “resume without sign-in,” or weaker unlock modes) where those would bypass normal authentication.
4. **Prevent auto-unlock / retain lock**:
   - Ensure there is **no automatic unlock** after the inactivity lock triggers.
   - If proximity/trusted-device features exist, configure them so they do **not** weaken the requirement that unlock requires normal re-authentication.

### 2) Identity integration validation
5. Validate that the unlock prompt uses the **organization’s standard authentication flow** (e.g., OS sign-in integrated with the enterprise IdP such as Entra ID/Okta) rather than a local bypass.

### 3) Auditability and drift control
6. Enable endpoint compliance reporting so you can produce evidence that:
   - the inactivity lock timer is **15 minutes**, and
   - unlock requires sign-in.
7. Implement drift detection/alerting for endpoints that deviate from the baseline.

### 4) Ensure device lock is not treated as logout
8. Publish/confirm a separate end-of-workday **logout requirement** (policy/procedure) for systems where logout is mandated by organizational rules.
9. For the application, confirm that session persistence settings (e.g., “remember me,” long-lived tokens, or persistent app sessions) do not effectively negate the intent of AC-11 by allowing continued access after lock.

## WHO
- **Endpoint Security Engineer / IT Admin**: implements and maintains the managed endpoint configuration baseline.
- **IAM Engineer**: validates unlock authentication integrates with the normal enterprise sign-in method.
- **Application Owner / Security Engineer**: verifies application session behavior does not undermine lock intent.
- **IT Compliance / GRC**: ensures evidence collection and that logout requirements are documented and enforced where required.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. On managed endpoints, after **15 minutes of inactivity**, the device enters a locked state.
2. While locked, a user cannot access the application/session content without completing the **standard enterprise sign-in**.
3. There is **no automatic unlock** after the lock event.
4. Endpoints report compliance showing the inactivity lock timer is set to **15 minutes** and unlock requires sign-in.
5. Application behavior does not allow continued access to sensitive content after the endpoint is locked (e.g., no “resume” that bypasses re-authentication).
6. Organizational procedures still require **logout** when policy mandates it; device lock is not used as the sole compensating control for end-of-day logout.


### ac-11.1 — Pattern-hiding Displays (Enhancement)

#### Enforce lock-screen pattern-hiding image on managed endpoints

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Days
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Configure managed endpoints so that when a user session is locked, any previously visible display content is immediately concealed and replaced with an approved publicly viewable lock image (pattern/screen-saver style image, photographic image, solid color, clock, battery indicator, or blank). Ensure no controlled unclassified information is displayed during the lock transition (no ghosting/bleed-through).

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 AC-11(1) (Enhancement) requires: “Conceal, via the device lock, information previously visible on the display with a publicly viewable image.” The guidance allows static/dynamic images (patterns, photos, solid colors, clock, battery indicator) or blank, with the caveat that controlled unclassified information is not displayed.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define the approved lock-screen set
- Create an allowlist of lock-screen display types permitted by AC-11(1): pattern/screen-saver style image, photographic image, solid color, clock, battery indicator, or blank.
- Explicitly prohibit any lock-screen settings that can reveal prior content (e.g., notification previews on lock, “quick glance”/widgets showing sensitive UI, or any custom overlay that may not fully clear the framebuffer).

### 2) Enforce via endpoint management (MDM/UEM) for all managed endpoints
- Apply a baseline configuration (golden image + MDM policy) to ensure:
  - Session lock is enabled and reliably triggered (manual lock and inactivity lock).
  - Lock screen uses only the approved image type(s).
  - Lock-screen notifications/preview content are disabled or restricted so they cannot show controlled unclassified information.
  - Any “show status/details on lock screen” features are disabled.
- Ensure the lock transition clears prior screen content (no ghosting). Prefer OS-native lock-screen mechanisms over custom apps/overlays.

### 3) Validate “no ghosting” with repeatable test evidence
- For representative endpoint builds (OS versions and hardware classes), run a repeatable test:
  1. Open a dense document/application view containing sample controlled unclassified text.
  2. Trigger lock (manual and inactivity).
  3. Verify immediately after lock and for a defined observation window (e.g., 10 seconds) that:
     - The prior content is not readable/recognizable.
     - No brief “ghosting,” partial reveal, or bleed-through occurs.
     - Only the approved lock image is visible.
- Record evidence: configuration export/screenshots and test results (timestamped) for audit.

### 4) Ongoing compliance and drift detection
- Use endpoint compliance reporting to detect drift (e.g., lock-screen image type changed, notification preview enabled, noncompliant lock-screen policy).
- Alert on noncompliance and remediate via automated policy re-application.

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Endpoint Security/IT Admin**: implement and maintain MDM/UEM policies and golden images.
- **Cybersecurity/Compliance Analyst**: define approved lock-screen allowlist and review evidence.
- **IT Operations/Helpdesk**: remediate noncompliant devices and coordinate rollout.

## Acceptance criteria
1. For each managed endpoint OS/hardware class, configuration is enforced such that lock-screen display type is restricted to the approved set.
2. On manual lock and inactivity lock, previously visible content is not readable/recognizable from normal viewing distance.
3. No ghosting/partial reveal/bleed-through is observed during the lock transition and for the observation window used in testing.
4. Lock-screen notifications/preview features that could expose controlled unclassified information are disabled or restricted per the allowlist.
5. Audit evidence exists: MDM/UEM policy exports (or equivalent configuration baselines) and documented test results showing the required behavior.

### ac-12 — Session Termination (Control)

#### Enforce AC-12 session termination via IdP policies and app logout

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement automatic termination of user logical sessions for the application based on the organization-defined triggers in **AC-12 (ac-12_odp)**:
- **After 30 minutes of user inactivity**
- **Immediately upon account lockout**
- **Immediately upon suspected compromise**
- **At system-defined off-hours for non-privileged users**

When a session is terminated, ensure the user’s associated application session is ended and no authenticated application activity continues under that session.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-12 requires: “**Automatically terminate a user session after {{ insert: param, ac-12_odp }}**.” Guidance clarifies this is about **logical sessions** (not network disconnect) and that termination should end **all processes associated with the user’s logical session** except those specifically created by the user to continue after termination.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Centralize session termination at the enterprise IdP (SSO)
1. Ensure the application uses the enterprise identity provider (IdP) for user authentication (OIDC/SAML).
2. Configure IdP session policies to enforce the AC-12 triggers:
   - **Idle timeout = 30 minutes** (define “inactivity” as no authenticated user actions/events for the session; align with IdP/app session idle settings).
   - **Account lockout/disable revocation = immediate**: when the account is locked/disabled (or equivalent state change), configure the IdP to invalidate active sessions/tokens.
   - **Suspected compromise = immediate**: integrate the IdP with the organization’s incident/risk workflow so that when an account is marked “suspected compromise” (e.g., via SOAR automation or risk-based action), the IdP revokes/invalidate sessions.
   - **Off-hours termination for non-privileged users**: implement time-based conditional access/session policy that applies to **non-privileged** groups/roles only (exclude privileged roles/groups).
3. Implement these settings as **policy-as-code** (e.g., IdP configuration managed via IaC) with change control.

### 2) Ensure the application honors IdP session invalidation
1. For OIDC: ensure the application relies on IdP-issued tokens/sessions and does not maintain long-lived application sessions that outlive IdP session invalidation.
2. Implement logout/session-end handling:
   - On IdP session invalidation events (or on token invalidation), force the application to clear local session state and require re-authentication.
   - Ensure API endpoints reject requests when the user’s IdP session is no longer valid (e.g., token validation fails).
3. If the application supports background jobs tied to a user session, ensure they are only allowed to continue if they are explicitly created as **session-owner continuation processes**; otherwise, terminate/cancel them when the session ends.

### 3) Audit evidence and trigger cause logging
1. Configure the application and IdP to log session termination events including:
   - termination timestamp
   - trigger cause (idle timeout vs lockout vs suspected compromise vs off-hours)
   - user identifier and session identifier
2. Forward logs to SIEM and ensure correlation fields exist (user/session IDs) for auditability.

## WHO
- **Identity/Access Engineer**: IdP session policy configuration (idle timeout, off-hours conditional access, session revocation on lockout/disable, compromise integration).
- **Application Security Engineer**: application session handling, token/session validation behavior, background job/session-owner continuation rules.
- **SOC/IR Automation Owner**: integration that marks “suspected compromise” and triggers immediate session revocation.
- **Compliance/Assurance**: verifies audit evidence and that triggers match **ac-12_odp**.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. **Idle timeout**: After **30 minutes of user inactivity**, the user is automatically forced to re-authenticate; no authenticated application actions succeed under the terminated session.
2. **Account lockout**: When an account is locked/disabled, any active application sessions are terminated **immediately** (within the system’s defined revocation propagation window) and requests fail until re-authentication.
3. **Suspected compromise**: When an account is flagged as suspected compromise per the organization workflow, active sessions are terminated **immediately** and audit logs show the trigger cause as “suspected compromise.”
4. **Off-hours**: For **non-privileged** users, sessions are terminated during configured off-hours; privileged users are not terminated by this off-hours rule.
5. **Process termination behavior**: Any application background activity tied to the user session is stopped by default; only explicitly session-owner continuation processes persist after termination.
6. **Auditability**: For each termination event, logs in IdP/app/SIEM include termination time, user/session identifiers, and the correct trigger cause.


### ac-12.1 — User-initiated Logouts (Enhancement)

#### Implement user-initiated logout that terminates authenticated sessions

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Add a user-facing **Logout/Sign out** capability that users can trigger on demand for all **authenticated user-initiated communications sessions** for the organization’s defined **information resources** (ac-12.01_odp: *local workstations, password-protected web services, and authenticated database sessions*). When invoked, the system must **end the authenticated session(s)** so the user cannot continue using the session without authenticating again.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-12(1)** requires: “Provide a logout capability for user-initiated communications sessions whenever authentication is used to gain access to {{ insert: param, ac-12.01_odp }}.” This applies to information resources where users gain access via authentication (guidance explicitly includes local workstations, databases, and password-protected websites/web-based services). The control enhancement implies logout must actually terminate the authenticated session, not merely redirect the user.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
Implement logout consistently across the application’s session types:

### 1) Web services (password-protected web application/API UI)
- **UI/endpoint:** Provide a prominent **Logout** action (e.g., `/logout` or `/session/logout`) accessible to authenticated users.
- **Server-side session termination:** On logout request:
  - Invalidate the application session (e.g., delete/mark session record invalid in server session store).
  - Clear authentication cookies (set `Set-Cookie` with empty value and `Max-Age=0` / `Expires` in the past).
  - If using refresh tokens, **revoke/blacklist refresh tokens** and/or rotate tokens so they cannot be used after logout.
- **Token/session enforcement:** Ensure subsequent requests using the old session cookie or tokens are rejected (HTTP 401/403) and do not re-establish access.
- **User confirmation:** After logout, show a confirmation page/message (e.g., “You have been signed out”) and redirect to a public landing page.

### 2) Local workstations (user-initiated access to the application from managed endpoints)
- **OS/browser sign-out integration:** Ensure that when a user selects Logout in the web UI (or the workstation sign-in flow), the workstation’s authenticated browser session is cleared (cookies cleared) and any SSO session used by the application is ended where supported by the enterprise IdP.
- **No silent re-auth:** Prevent automatic re-authentication that would immediately restore access after logout (e.g., avoid relying solely on persistent SSO cookies without clearing them when the user logs out).

### 3) Authenticated database sessions
- **Define logout semantics for DB access:** For database sessions that are established via the application (or via an application-mediated connection), implement a user-initiated **disconnect/terminate** action that:
  - Closes the application’s DB connection(s) for that user.
  - If the system uses short-lived DB credentials/tokens, ensure they are not renewed after logout.
  - If the system uses long-lived DB sessions, implement a controlled termination mechanism (e.g., application-side disconnect and, where feasible/approved, DB-side session termination for the user’s session identifiers).
- **Enforcement:** After user logout, any attempt to use the previously established DB session through the application path must fail (re-auth required).

### 4) Audit logging for logout/session termination
- Emit an audit event for each logout/session termination including at minimum:
  - **User identifier** (who initiated logout)
  - **Timestamp**
  - **Resource/session identifiers** (web session ID and/or token/session ID; DB connection/session identifier if applicable)
  - **Source** (client IP / workstation identifier if available)
  - **Result** (success/failure)
- Ensure events are forwarded to the organization’s SIEM/logging platform and retained per enterprise logging requirements.

### 5) Documentation and configuration evidence
- Update system documentation/runbooks to describe:
  - Logout endpoints/flows for web services
  - How logout terminates sessions (cookie invalidation, token revocation, DB disconnect/termination)
  - Any IdP logout behavior assumptions
  - Troubleshooting steps when logout does not fully terminate access

## WHO
- **Application Security Engineer / Backend Engineer:** Implement logout endpoints, session invalidation, token revocation, and DB disconnect/termination logic.
- **Identity & Access Management (IAM) Engineer:** Configure IdP logout semantics (where applicable) and ensure SSO session termination aligns with the application logout.
- **Security Operations / SIEM Engineer:** Validate audit event schema, routing, and retention.
- **System Owner:** Approve documented procedures and verify operational readiness.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. **Web logout works:** When an authenticated user clicks Logout, the user is shown a confirmation and redirected to a public page.
2. **Session termination is enforced:** After logout, reusing the prior session cookie and/or tokens results in **401/403** and access is not restored without re-authentication.
3. **Token revocation/rotation:** If refresh tokens are used, they are revoked/invalidated such that they cannot be used after logout.
4. **DB session termination/disconnect:** After logout, any application-mediated DB access using the prior authenticated DB session fails and requires re-authentication (or the DB session is terminated/disconnected per the implemented mechanism).
5. **Audit evidence:** Logout/session termination events are recorded with user ID, timestamp, session/resource identifiers, and outcome, and are visible in the SIEM/log store.
6. **No immediate re-login:** Logout does not immediately restore access due to persistent SSO/session artifacts (within the supported IdP/endpoint capabilities).

### ac-12.2 — Termination Message (Enhancement)

#### Implement explicit logout termination message for web and FTP

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** High
- **Effort:** Days
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement an explicit, termination-confirming logout message that is presented to users when authenticated communications sessions end. Ensure correct timing semantics by session type:
- **Web/HTTP(S) sessions:** show the logout/termination message **after** the authenticated session has been terminated.
- **FTP sessions:** send/show the logout/termination message as the **final message prior to terminating** the session.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST **AC-12(2)** requires: “Display an explicit logout message to users indicating the termination of authenticated communications sessions.” Guidance clarifies that web access can display the message after termination, while **FTP typically sends logout messages as final messages prior to terminating sessions**.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define session-type behavior and message content
- Create a small, testable specification in the application code/config describing:
  - **Web:** message text (e.g., “Your authenticated session has been terminated.”), and that it is rendered only after server-side session invalidation.
  - **FTP:** message text and that it is emitted as the final server response before closing the control connection.

### 2) Web/HTTP(S) implementation (post-termination rendering)
- Implement logout endpoint(s) that perform, in order:
  1. **Terminate authenticated session server-side** (e.g., invalidate server session, revoke refresh tokens if applicable, clear auth cookies server-side).
  2. **Render a post-logout page** (or return a response body) that includes the explicit termination message.
- Ensure the post-logout page is not reachable while the session is still valid:
  - Gate rendering on a server-side flag/event (e.g., a one-time logout event ID stored server-side, or a check that the session is invalid).
- For SPA flows:
  - Ensure the message is produced by the server after invalidation (not only client-side cookie clearing).

### 3) FTP implementation (final message semantics)
- If the application provides FTP service (or integrates with an FTP server component), configure/extend the FTP server to:
  - Send an explicit logout/termination message as the **final reply** in the FTP control channel.
  - Only close the connection **after** the message is queued/sent.
- Validate the exact FTP reply behavior for the relevant FTP commands used during logout/disconnect (e.g., QUIT) so the termination message is the last observable server response.

### 4) Logging and audit evidence
- Emit audit events for both session types:
  - **Web:** record logout request, server-side session invalidation completion, and that the termination message page/response was served (include correlation ID).
  - **FTP:** record disconnect/logout event and that the final termination message was sent (include session identifier and correlation ID).
- Ensure logs are forwarded to the enterprise SIEM for retention and review.

### 5) Testing and verification
- Add automated tests and runbook-based manual tests:
  - **Web:** verify that the message is displayed only after server-side invalidation (attempt to access protected resource after logout must fail).
  - **FTP:** verify that the termination message is the final message observed before the connection closes.

## WHO
- **Application Owner / Backend Engineer:** implement logout orchestration, server-side invalidation, and post-logout rendering.
- **Platform/Integration Engineer:** configure/extend FTP server behavior and ensure final-message semantics.
- **Security/Compliance Analyst:** review message content/timing spec and confirm audit evidence meets AC-12(2) expectations.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. **Web:** After a user logs out, the user receives an explicit message indicating authenticated communications termination, and protected endpoints reject access immediately after logout.
2. **Web timing:** The termination message is rendered only after server-side session invalidation is completed (confirmed via correlation IDs in logs).
3. **FTP:** On logout/disconnect, the server sends an explicit termination message as the final message prior to closing the FTP session (confirmed by packet capture or FTP client transcript).
4. **Audit evidence:** For both web and FTP, logs exist showing (a) session termination occurred and (b) the termination message was served/sent, with correlation IDs suitable for SIEM review.
5. **Regression safety:** No path allows the post-logout message to be shown while the authenticated session remains valid.

### ac-12.3 — Timeout Warning Message (Enhancement)

#### Show 5-minute session-end warning via IdP and audit evidence

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** High
- **Effort:** Days
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement an explicit, user-facing warning during an active authenticated session stating: **"Your session will end in {{ac-12.03_odp}}"** (organization parameter **time until end of session for display to users**; default **5 minutes**). The warning must appear **before** the session terminates and must be aligned to the same session timeout/termination timing.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-12(3)** requires: **"Display an explicit message to users indicating that the session will end in {{ insert: param, ac-12.03_odp }}."** The guidance further states this is to **increase usability** by notifying users of **pending session termination** and that the **pending termination time period is based on the parameters defined in AC-12**.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### Implementation approach (SSO-first, applies across apps)
1. **Centralize session timeout and warning at the enterprise IdP** (e.g., Microsoft Entra ID / Okta / Ping) so the warning is tied to the same session lifetime that drives logout.
2. Configure the IdP session policy so that a **warning/notification is triggered exactly {{ac-12.03_odp}} before termination** (default: **5 minutes**). Prefer configurations that support an explicit warning offset rather than approximating in each application.
3. Ensure the IdP warning is **user-facing and explicit** (plain language) and includes the configured time period, e.g., **"Your session will end in 5 minutes."**
4. Provide a **user prompt to continue** (e.g., "Stay signed in" / "Continue session") where supported. The “continue” action must result in a **fresh session extension or reauthentication** (not merely dismissing the message).
5. **Instrument audit evidence**:
   - Export IdP events to the SIEM/logging platform (e.g., sign-in/session start, warning/reauthentication events if available, and session termination/logout events).
   - Retain logs long enough to support audit verification of the warning-to-logout timing.
6. **Document configuration evidence**:
   - Record the configured session lifetime and the warning offset value corresponding to **ac-12.03_odp**.
   - Store exported policy/config artifacts (or screenshots plus exported policy where possible) in the compliance repository.
7. **Validate timing** in a controlled test:
   - For a test user, measure the time delta between when the warning is displayed and when the session terminates.
   - Confirm the delta is approximately **5 minutes** within an agreed tolerance (e.g., ±30 seconds) and document results.

### If some apps do not rely on IdP UI
- For any application that cannot use IdP-driven warning UI, implement a **server-side session timer** that triggers a warning response/page **{{ac-12.03_odp}} before server-side session invalidation**, and ensure the warning is consistent with the AC-12 session termination mechanism. (Still log warning display and session termination timestamps.)

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Identity/SSO Engineer**: configure IdP session policy and warning behavior.
- **Application Security Engineer**: ensure apps relying on IdP behave correctly; implement fallback warning where needed.
- **Security Operations / SIEM Engineer**: ensure audit log export, retention, and correlation rules.
- **Compliance/Assurance Lead**: verify evidence package and acceptance testing results.

## Acceptance criteria
1. When a user is authenticated, the application/IdP displays an explicit message stating the session will end in **{{ac-12.03_odp}}** (default **5 minutes**).
2. The warning appears **before** the session terminates (no after-the-fact messaging).
3. The warning time period matches the configured **ac-12.03_odp** value used for session termination.
4. A “continue”/“stay signed in” action (if provided) results in a **real session extension or reauthentication**.
5. Audit evidence exists showing (a) warning display time (or equivalent IdP event) and (b) session termination time, enabling verification of the ~5-minute offset.
6. Configuration documentation/evidence is stored and traceable to the **ac-12.03_odp** setting.

### ac-14 — Permitted Actions Without Identification or Authentication (Control)

#### Define and document permitted unauthenticated actions per system

- **Category:** Manual
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Days
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
For the specific application/system, explicitly define the **exact user actions** that are permitted **before identification and authentication** and document them (with rationale) in the system’s security plan.

Using the organization-defined scope, the permitted unauthenticated actions must be limited to:
1) Access to **public information pages**
2) **Receiving inbound calls** via mobile phone
3) **Receiving facsimiles**
4) **Emergency maintenance** via a **protected physical bypass switch**

If the organization determines there are no such actions for the system, the assignment operation value can be **"none"**.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-14 requires that organizations **identify and document** the specific user actions that can occur **without identification or authentication**, including that such actions are limited to what the organization determines is appropriate. The guidance also emphasizes that bypasses apply only to situations **where identification/authentication have not yet occurred**, and that bypass mechanisms (e.g., physical switch) must be **protected from accidental or unmonitored use**.

## HOW (specific steps)
1) **Create an “Unauthenticated Actions” record for the system** (one per application/service boundary).
   - For each permitted action, capture:
     - **Action name** (e.g., “View public information pages”)
     - **Trigger/entry point** (e.g., public web routes, inbound call handling workflow, fax ingestion endpoint, emergency bypass mechanism)
     - **What is allowed** (precise outcome)
     - **What is explicitly excluded** (any action that would normally require authentication)
     - **Rationale** (plain-language justification for why identification/authentication are not required for that action)
     - **Evidence hooks** (what logs/tests/config will demonstrate enforcement, e.g., unauthenticated access tests; audit event for bypass use)
2) **Validate mission/business alignment** for each unauthenticated action.
   - Confirm each action supports the organization’s mission/day-to-day needs and is not a pathway to restricted functions.
3) **Update the system security plan** to include:
   - The **explicit list** of permitted unauthenticated actions for this system
   - The **rationale for each action** (why identification/authentication are not required)
   - A statement that these permissions apply only **before** identification/authentication occurs (i.e., not as a substitute for already-authenticated sessions)
4) **Emergency bypass documentation** (for the physical bypass switch case):
   - Document the emergency maintenance procedure at a level sufficient for audit (who can use it, how it is protected, and what it changes/does not change).
   - Ensure the bypass mechanism is described as **protected from accidental or unmonitored use** (e.g., physical controls, access restrictions, and auditability expectations).
5) **Governance review and approval**:
   - Route the unauthenticated actions record and security-plan updates through the system owner and security/compliance approver.
6) **Create an audit-ready evidence checklist** (even if technical enforcement is handled elsewhere):
   - For public pages: unauthenticated access test evidence
   - For inbound calls: evidence that call handling does not expose authenticated-only functions
   - For faxes: evidence that unauthenticated retrieval is not possible and ingestion is restricted
   - For emergency bypass: evidence that bypass use is traceable (audit event/log) and that bypass is protected

## WHO
- **System Owner / Application Owner**: owns the unauthenticated actions record and security-plan content
- **Information Security Officer / Compliance**: reviews that the list is complete, limited, and rationalized per AC-14 guidance
- **Operations/OT/Facilities (as applicable)**: provides details for the emergency physical bypass procedure and protections

## Acceptance criteria
- The system security plan contains an **explicit, per-system list** of permitted unauthenticated actions limited to the organization-defined scope (or explicitly states **"none"**).
- Each permitted unauthenticated action has a **documented rationale** explaining why identification/authentication are not required.
- The documentation clearly indicates these permissions apply only **before** identification/authentication occurs.
- Emergency maintenance via the physical bypass switch is documented with evidence expectations that it is **protected from accidental or unmonitored use**.
- The unauthenticated actions record is approved by the system owner and security/compliance reviewer and is audit-ready.

### ac-16 — Security and Privacy Attributes (Control)

#### Implement auditable security/privacy label binding with allow-lists

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 72%

## WHAT
Implement mechanisms in the covered systems (enterprise email, document repositories, OT historian, OT control workstations, API gateways) to:
1) **Bind** permitted **security attributes** and **privacy attributes** to information in **storage**, **processing**, and **transmission**.
2) **Persist** those bindings so they are not lost/blanked/overwritten across the information lifecycle.
3) **Restrict** attribute values to **organization-permitted types and values/ranges**.
4) **Audit** label binding and label changes with enough detail to identify **what changed, when, and by whom**.
5) Support **90-day applicability reviews** for both security and privacy attributes.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-16 requires that security and privacy attributes (metadata) are **associated (bound)** to active/passive entities and that this binding supports enforcement of security/privacy policies across **storage, processing, and transmission**. The guidance also emphasizes that attribute values and binding techniques affect enforcement trust, and that organizations should define **types**, **values/ranges**, and **review frequency** (here: every 90 days).

## HOW (specific approach)
### 1) Define the authoritative attribute schema and allow-lists (per system)
- Create an authoritative schema for **security attributes** and **privacy attributes** using the organization-defined parameters:
  - Security attribute types: **Information classification; export control (ITAR/EAR); retention category; encryption requirement; access domain** (ac-16_odp.01/07/04).
  - Privacy attribute types: **PII indicator; HIPAA identifier flag; consent/authorisation; data subject role** (ac-16_odp.02/08).
  - Permitted security attribute values/ranges: **Confidential/Secret; ITAR/EAR Yes/No; Retention 30–365 days; Encryption Required/Optional** (ac-16_odp.03/09).
  - Permitted privacy attribute values: **PII Yes/No; HIPAA identifier Yes/No; consent Granted/Not granted; data subject role Employee/Contractor/Customer** (ac-16_odp.04/09).
- For each covered system, document where labels live (e.g., message headers, document properties/IRM envelope, database record metadata, API gateway metadata) and which workflows can set/change them.

### 2) Implement label binding at creation/upload and propagate through workflows
- **Enterprise email**: enforce binding at message creation/send and on inbound ingestion (e.g., via mail gateway or IRM integration). Store labels as message metadata (e.g., headers/properties) and ensure downstream systems preserve them.
- **Document repositories**: enforce binding at upload/import and at document transformation workflows (conversion, sharing, export). Persist labels as repository metadata and ensure exports carry the same labels.
- **API gateways**: require clients to provide label metadata (or derive it via classification/PII detection) and bind it to the request context; propagate to downstream services via signed metadata/context headers.
- **OT historian / OT control workstations**: if native support exists, bind labels to historian records and workstation data objects; if not, implement a compensating binding mechanism (e.g., sidecar metadata store keyed by record/object IDs) and ensure it is used by enforcement points available in the OT environment.

### 3) Enforce permitted values (reject/normalize)
- Add backend validation so only permitted values/ranges are persisted:
  - Reject requests/uploads that attempt to set attributes outside the allow-lists.
  - If the system supports it, normalize equivalent inputs to the canonical permitted values.
- Ensure both UI/workflow validation and API/service-side validation are implemented (defense-in-depth).

### 4) Make label changes auditable and controlled
- For every label binding and label change, write an audit event containing at minimum:
  - object/message/record identifier
  - attribute type(s) changed
  - before/after values
  - timestamp
  - actor (user/service account)
  - reason/code (if available from workflow)
