4. SIEM receives gateway and network enforcement logs sufficient to demonstrate routing (session start/identity/source/target/port) and alerts exist for non-gateway attempts.
5. System design documentation and the **system security plan** explicitly describe the managed entry-point routing and the log sources used as evidence.


### ac-17.4 — Privileged Commands and Access (Enhancement)

#### Purpose-bound PAM for remote privileged commands and security info

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Months
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement a Privileged Access Management (PAM) broker for all **remote** privileged command execution and for **remote** access to **security-relevant information**, enforcing:
- **Explicit authorization** tied to allowed purposes.
- **Purpose-limited entitlements** (what can be executed/viewed).
- **Time-bounded access**.
- **Evidence-rich auditing** that records who did what, when, and why (linked to the approval).
- **OT/IT compatibility** (including vendor/legacy remote admin paths).

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-17(4) enhancement guidance requires restricting remote execution of privileged commands and remote access to security-relevant information to **clearly defined, approved needs**, and producing **assessable evidence** for those remote actions. The organization-defined purposes include:
- Privileged commands via remote access for **emergency privileged maintenance, patching, configuration changes, and access to security tooling for audit evidence**.
- Security-relevant information via remote access for **incident response support, vulnerability remediation, and security monitoring for Confidential and Secret data**.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Identify and inventory remote privileged paths (IT + OT)
- Enumerate all remote admin mechanisms that can run privileged commands or expose security-relevant information (e.g., SSH/RDP to admin accounts, jump hosts, vendor remote consoles, remote management agents, privileged web consoles, OT engineering workstations).
- For each path, define the **target systems** and **privileged command surfaces** (shells, CLIs, scripts, admin consoles) and **security-relevant information surfaces** (security dashboards, SIEM consoles, security tooling outputs, audit evidence repositories).

### 2) Configure PAM to enforce purpose-bound authorization
- Integrate PAM with the enterprise IdP (for strong authentication and identity context).
- Create **two purpose-to-entitlement policy sets** aligned to the organization-defined needs:
  - **Privileged command purposes**: emergency privileged maintenance, patching, configuration changes, security tooling for audit evidence.
  - **Security-relevant information purposes**: incident response support, vulnerability remediation, security monitoring for Confidential and Secret data.
- Require a **pre-approval** (ticket/approval record) before access is granted.
- Enforce **time-bound sessions** (JIT) and require the operator to select/attach the approved purpose.
- Embed the **approval/ticket ID** into the PAM session metadata so it can be correlated to audit evidence.

### 3) Restrict what can be executed/viewed during the session
- For privileged command sessions, restrict execution to approved command sets/roles (e.g., allow only specific admin commands or approved scripts for patching/configuration; block high-risk commands outside the approved purpose).
- For security-relevant information sessions, restrict viewing to approved security tooling and data classifications (Confidential/Secret) and block access to broader datasets not required for the approved purpose.

### 4) Enable evidence-rich auditing and central logging
- Enable **session recording** and **command-level audit** for privileged command sessions.
- Ensure logs include at minimum: user identity, approval/ticket ID, purpose, target system, session start/end time, commands executed (or actions performed), and source IP.
- Forward logs to the enterprise SIEM/log platform with integrity protections and retention per organizational policy.

### 5) Add enforcement for disallowed attempts
- Configure PAM to deny or step-up (require re-approval) when:
  - The purpose is missing/invalid.
  - The operator attempts privileged commands outside the allowed purpose.
  - The operator attempts to access security-relevant information outside the allowed purpose or data classification.
- Generate alerts for denied attempts and for repeated policy violations.

### 6) OT-specific adaptation
- For OT/legacy systems where direct PAM integration is constrained:
  - Use a **jump host / broker** pattern that terminates remote sessions and enforces purpose-bound policies.
  - Where vendor tools require special access, create vendor-specific entitlements mapped to the allowed purposes and ensure session recording/command logging still captures operator actions.
  - Ensure safety/uptime constraints are respected by limiting actions to approved maintenance windows and approved command sets.

## WHO
- **PAM/Identity Engineer**: implement PAM integration, policy mappings, session recording, and logging.
- **System Owners (IT/OT)**: approve the purpose-to-entitlement mappings for their systems and define allowed command/tooling surfaces.
- **Security Operations / SOC**: validate alerting, log forwarding, and evidence review workflows.
- **ITSM/Workflow Owner**: ensure approvals/tickets capture purpose and are available to PAM for correlation.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. For remote privileged command execution, access is granted only when an operator provides an **approved purpose** matching one of:
   - emergency privileged maintenance, patching, configuration changes, or security tooling for audit evidence.
2. For remote access to security-relevant information, access is granted only when an operator provides an **approved purpose** matching one of:
   - incident response support, vulnerability remediation, or security monitoring for Confidential/Secret data.
3. Every granted remote privileged session produces assessable evidence containing:
   - operator identity, target system, timestamps, executed commands/actions, and the **approval/ticket ID**.
4. Every granted remote security-relevant information session produces assessable evidence containing:
   - operator identity, target system/tool, timestamps, and the **approval/ticket ID**.
5. Attempts to execute privileged commands or access security-relevant information without a valid approved purpose are denied (or require re-approval) and generate an auditable event.
6. Evidence is centralized in the enterprise logging/SIEM platform and is reviewable by Security Operations during audits/incident investigations.

### ac-17.6 — Protection of Mechanism Information (Enhancement)

#### Vault and role-based access for remote access mechanism info

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement a controlled repository (“Remote Access Mechanism Information Vault”) for all information that describes how remote access mechanisms work (e.g., VPN/RDP/jump-host connection instructions, configuration details, diagrams, troubleshooting steps). Restrict access to this information to authorized, need-to-know roles and deliver it only through approved workflows.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-17(6) (Enhancement) requires: **“Protect information about remote access mechanisms from unauthorized use and disclosure.”** The guidance notes remote access requirements may be included in **information exchange agreements**, **rules of behavior (PL-4)**, and **access agreements (PS-6)**. This solution directly addresses the core requirement by controlling where the mechanism information is stored, who can access it, and how it is delivered.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Create an inventory of protected remote-access mechanism information**
   - Identify document types and artifacts that contain “how-to” or connection/configuration details (e.g., VPN client setup guides, jump host access steps, RDP broker instructions, firewall/NAT change notes, troubleshooting playbooks, network path diagrams).
   - Tag each item with a classification label (e.g., “Remote Access Mechanism Confidential”).

2. **Centralize storage in a dedicated, access-controlled repository**
   - Use an enterprise-approved document management system or secrets/document vault pattern.
   - Enforce confidentiality controls at the repository level (no public links; restrict sharing; disable anonymous access).

3. **Enforce role-based access using enterprise identity**
   - Define role groups aligned to need-to-know:
     - Remote access implementers/monitors (IT/network/security ops)
     - Authorized system users who require troubleshooting/operations access
     - Information security responsibilities (oversight)
   - Integrate with the enterprise IdP (e.g., Entra ID/Okta) and require strong authentication (at minimum MFA) for repository access.

4. **Implement “approved delivery” workflow for mechanism information**
   - Users request access via the ticketing/approval workflow.
   - Delivery must occur through the approved channel (e.g., time-bound vault link or controlled document access), not via email/chat or unrestricted file sharing.

5. **Add auditable controls and evidence generation**
   - Enable audit logging for: view/download events, permission changes, and access requests/approvals.
   - Periodically review entitlements for the repository role groups.

6. **Operationalize governance hooks (to support agreements/rules)**
   - Ensure the repository includes references to the organization’s remote access rules of behavior and access agreement templates (so that the protected mechanism information is consistently governed).
   - For partner/contractor scenarios, ensure the workflow supports linking to the applicable access agreement record (without embedding partner-specific connection details in uncontrolled locations).

## WHO
- **System Owner / IT Operations Lead**: owns the inventory and repository content lifecycle.
- **Information Security (or Compliance) Lead**: defines classification/handling rules and approves role model.
- **IAM/Platform Engineer**: implements IdP integration, role mappings, and authentication requirements.
- **Service Desk / Ticketing Admin**: runs the approved request/delivery workflow.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
- A documented inventory exists of all remote access mechanism information stored in the vault.
- All identified artifacts are stored only in the controlled repository (or are linked to it) and are not distributed via uncontrolled channels.
- Only the defined authorized role groups can view/download the mechanism information; unauthorized users cannot access the repository content.
- Access to the repository is protected by enterprise identity controls (including MFA).
- Mechanism information is delivered only via the approved workflow (e.g., time-bound vault access), with no email/chat distribution of connection/configuration instructions.
- Audit logs capture access and administrative actions, and periodic access reviews are performed with evidence retained.


### ac-17.9 — Disconnect or Disable Access (Enhancement)

#### Emergency remote-access kill switch within 15 minutes

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement a capability to **disconnect or disable remote access** to the system(s) by activating an **emergency “deny” kill switch** at the earliest enforcement points (e.g., VPN/remote access gateway, firewall policy, ZTNA application policy, bastion/SSH gateway), such that remote access is disabled **within the organization-defined time period**.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-17(9) Enhancement** requires: “Provide the capability to **disconnect or disable remote access** to the system within **{{ insert: param, ac-17.09_odp }}**.” The guidance notes the speed varies by mission/business criticality and must eliminate immediate/future remote access.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define remote access methods and enforcement points (design/config)
- Create/maintain an inventory mapping each remote access method to the **specific enforcement control** that can block it (e.g., VPN gateway policy, firewall rule set, ZTNA app access policy, bastion allowlist).
- For each method, define the exact policy/rule set that will be flipped to “deny” (include stable identifiers for audit correlation).

### 2) Build an emergency kill-switch policy per remote access method
- Implement a pre-defined **emergency deny** configuration for each remote access method:
  - VPN/remote access gateway: deny all remote access sessions to the target system(s) (or to the relevant destination networks/ports).
  - Firewall: disable/override inbound remote-access allow rules for the target system(s).
  - ZTNA: revoke/deny application access policies for the target system(s).
  - Bastion/SSH/RDP gateway: remove/deny access paths (e.g., disable target host/service entries in the gateway policy).
- Ensure the kill-switch is implemented as a **single operator action** (e.g., “activate kill switch” triggers a policy-as-code/runbook execution), not ad-hoc manual editing.

### 3) Enforce authorized activation with strong authentication
- Restrict kill-switch activation to **privileged operators** via RBAC on the orchestration platform and/or the enforcement platform.
- Require step-up authentication for activation (e.g., phishing-resistant MFA) and record operator identity.

### 4) Operationalize the 15-minute requirement with automation and verification
- Create an emergency runbook/playbook that:
  - Starts a timer when activation begins.
  - Executes the kill-switch activation for the relevant system(s).
  - Performs an immediate verification step (e.g., attempt connection from a controlled test account or validate policy state) and records the result.
- Set the target time to meet **ac-17.09_odp = “within 15 minutes”**.

### 5) Produce auditable evidence
- Centralize and retain evidence for each activation:
  - Operator identity, timestamp (start/end), and the runbook/playbook execution ID.
  - The specific policy/rule identifiers changed/activated.
  - Verification outcome showing remote access is blocked.
- Ensure logs from the enforcement points (VPN/firewall/ZTNA/gateway) and orchestration system are forwarded to the SIEM/log platform.

### 6) Test and continuously validate
- Perform periodic tabletop + technical tests to confirm the kill-switch can be activated and verified within 15 minutes.
- Update the kill-switch mappings when remote access methods change (new vendor tool, new gateway, new ports, new ZTNA app).

## WHO
- **Security Engineering / Network Security Engineer**: implement kill-switch policies at enforcement points and integrate with orchestration.
- **IAM/Privileged Access Administrator**: configure RBAC and step-up authentication for activation.
- **SOC / Incident Response Lead**: maintain runbooks, verification steps, and evidence requirements.
- **System Owner / Application Owner**: approve the scope (which systems/methods are covered) and ensure coverage for critical business functions.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. For each identified remote access method to the target system(s), there exists a pre-defined emergency deny configuration at the correct enforcement point.
2. A single authorized operator action (runbook/playbook trigger) activates the deny state.
3. In a controlled test, remote access attempts fail and the measured time from activation start to confirmed disablement is **≤ 15 minutes** (ac-17.09_odp).
4. Each activation produces audit evidence including: operator identity, start/end timestamps, runbook/playbook execution ID, and policy/rule identifiers.
5. Only authorized roles can activate the kill switch, and activation requires step-up authentication.
6. Evidence is retained and searchable in the centralized logging/SIEM system for audit review.

### ac-17.10 — Authenticate Remote Commands (Enhancement)

#### Authenticate remote admin commands with mTLS, HMAC, and replay protection

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement cryptographic authentication for **remote administrative commands** sent to **on-prem servers and OT gateways** over **approved management channels**, using:
- **Mutual TLS (mTLS)** for the channel
- **Per-command HMAC** to detect tampering
- **Monotonically increasing sequence numbers** (and/or equivalent replay protection) to prevent replay/out-of-order execution

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST AC-17(10) enhancement requires: **“Implement mechanisms to authenticate remote commands.”** The guidance states this protects against **unauthorized commands** and **replay of authorized commands**, ensuring systems **accept and execute commands in the order intended** and **reject unauthorized commands**.

This solution directly implements the organization-defined parameters:
- **ac-17.10_odp.01 (mechanisms)**: “mutual TLS with per-command HMAC and monotonically increasing sequence numbers”
- **ac-17.10_odp.02 (remote commands)**: “remote administrative commands issued to on-prem servers and OT gateways over the approved management channels”

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define the approved management channel endpoints and enforce mTLS
- Identify the exact management paths used for remote admin commands (e.g., ZTNA app connectors, management jump hosts, dedicated admin subnets, or a remote command gateway).
- Configure the command ingress service (or the remote management interface itself) to require **mTLS**:
  - Server presents a certificate trusted by clients.
  - Client presents a certificate trusted by the server.
  - Enforce certificate validation (chain, expiry, revocation/OCSP/CRL or equivalent, and hostname/SAN checks).
- Ensure only approved clients/identities can connect (certificate-to-identity mapping).

### 2) Implement a command envelope with per-command HMAC
- Define a canonical command envelope that includes at minimum:
  - `command_id` (or unique identifier)
  - `target` (on-prem server / OT gateway identifier)
  - `payload` (the command to execute)
  - `sequence_no`
  - `session_id` (or equivalent binding context)
  - `timestamp` (optional but recommended for diagnostics)
  - `hmac` (computed over canonicalized fields)
- Use a cryptographic MAC (e.g., HMAC-SHA-256) with a key established for the command channel (e.g., derived from the mTLS session via a key-derivation step, or provisioned per client/target pair).
- On the receiver (target agent/service or gateway adapter):
  - Verify the HMAC **before** any command parsing/execution.
  - Reject commands with missing/invalid HMAC.

### 3) Enforce monotonically increasing sequence numbers (replay/out-of-order rejection)
- Maintain per-authenticated command stream state on the receiver:
  - Track `last_accepted_sequence_no` per `(authenticated client identity, target)`.
- On each received command:
  - If `sequence_no <= last_accepted_sequence_no`, reject as **replay/out-of-order**.
  - If valid, update `last_accepted_sequence_no` and proceed to execution.
- Persist the sequence state as required for your operational model (e.g., durable storage or recovery-safe mechanism) so reboots do not allow replay.

### 4) Apply the same authentication checks to both on-prem servers and OT gateways
- For on-prem servers: implement/enable the receiver-side verification in the server-side management agent/service.
- For OT gateways: implement/enable the receiver-side verification in the gateway management adapter/service that receives remote admin commands.
- Ensure both paths use the same envelope validation logic (or a shared library/service) so the authentication behavior is consistent.

### 5) Audit and rejection reason logging (audit-ready evidence)
- Log at minimum, for each remote command attempt (success and failure):
  - authenticated identity (from mTLS)
  - target identifier
  - command_id
  - HMAC verification result (pass/fail)
  - sequence check result (accepted/rejected; reason: out-of-order/replay)
  - execution outcome (executed/rejected)
- Ensure logs are forwarded to SIEM and retained per enterprise logging/retention policy.

### 6) Operationalization
- Add configuration management so the mTLS trust anchors, HMAC keying/derivation parameters, and sequence enforcement settings are versioned and change-controlled.
- Provide a test harness that can generate valid/invalid commands to verify enforcement.

## WHO
- **Solution/Platform Engineer (primary):** implement receiver-side command envelope validation (mTLS requirement, HMAC verification, sequence enforcement) and the command ingress/adapter.
- **Security Architect (review/approval):** validate cryptographic choices, key management approach, and audit logging requirements.
- **OT/Infrastructure Engineer (OT gateways):** integrate the receiver-side enforcement into OT gateway command handling.
- **SOC/SIEM Engineer (support):** ensure logs and alerting are wired for HMAC/sequence rejection patterns.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. **mTLS enforced:** Remote admin command connections to the command ingress/receiver are rejected unless mutual TLS succeeds.
2. **HMAC enforced:** Any command with an invalid/missing HMAC is rejected and never executed.
3. **Replay/out-of-order rejected:** Commands with `sequence_no` less than or equal to the last accepted value for that authenticated stream are rejected with a logged reason.
4. **Order preserved:** For a valid increasing sequence, commands are accepted and executed in the intended order.
5. **Coverage:** The same authentication enforcement is implemented for both **on-prem servers** and **OT gateways**.
6. **Audit evidence:** For each rejected command, logs include authenticated identity, target, command_id, and specific rejection reason (HMAC failure vs sequence replay/out-of-order).


### ac-18 — Wireless Access (Control)

#### Authorise-then-enable wireless access with mutual authentication

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 72%

## WHAT
Implement an “authorise-then-enable” wireless access control that (1) enumerates every wireless technology used (e.g., 802.11x/Wi‑Fi, Bluetooth, and any microwave/packet radio links used for connectivity), (2) defines per-technology configuration/connection requirements including **authenticator protection and mutual authentication**, (3) prevents wireless profiles from being enabled unless there is a **formal authorisation record** for the specific purpose, and (4) produces **auditable evidence** linking wireless connection activity to the relevant authorisation.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-18 Wireless Access requires that wireless technologies used are configured/connected according to documented requirements and that wireless networks use authentication protocols providing **authenticator protection and mutual authentication**. It also requires that wireless access is only allowed when it has been formally approved, with auditable records of wireless access activity.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Create a Wireless Access Catalogue (per wireless technology)
- Maintain an inventory of all wireless access types used for connectivity to the system/environment:
  - 802.11 (Wi‑Fi) SSIDs / WLANs
  - Bluetooth (e.g., device pairing use cases)
  - Any microwave/packet radio links used for connectivity
- For each entry, record:
  - Purpose (e.g., staff access, contractor access, maintenance)
  - Allowed user/device/service identities (e.g., via identity provider groups, device certificates, MAC allowlists only if unavoidable)
  - Required authentication protocol characteristics: **authenticator protection** and **mutual authentication** (e.g., enterprise Wi‑Fi using EAP-TLS or equivalent mutual-auth mechanism; Bluetooth using authenticated pairing with identity binding)
  - Where enforcement occurs (WLC/controller, NAC/802.1X, Bluetooth management platform, radio gateway)

### 2) Define per-technology “Connection Requirements” and configuration baselines
- For each wireless access type in the catalogue, create a controlled configuration template that includes:
  - Security mode requirements (disable legacy/weak modes; require enterprise authentication where applicable)
  - Mutual authentication requirement (explicitly documented and mapped to the chosen protocol)
  - Certificate/credential validation expectations (e.g., trust anchors, server validation, client certificate requirements for mutual auth)
  - Any usage restrictions (e.g., which environments/sites can use the SSID/profile; allowed time windows; segmentation requirements)
- Store these templates in the organisation’s configuration management system so they are versioned and change-controlled.

### 3) Implement “authorise-then-enable” enforcement at the wireless enforcement point
- Integrate the wireless enforcement point(s) with the authorisation workflow:
  - Wireless LAN controllers / access points (for 802.11)
  - NAC/802.1X policy engine (if used)
  - Bluetooth management/policy system (if used)
  - Radio gateway configuration management (if applicable)
- Operational rule: **no SSID/profile/pairing policy/radio connectivity rule is enabled** unless an authorisation record exists for that exact purpose.
- Implementation pattern:
  - Authorisation record (in ticketing/GRC) includes: wireless access type ID, purpose, scope (site/environment), validity period, approver(s), and required authentication settings.
  - An automation step (script/API integration) generates/updates the enforcement configuration only after approval.
  - If authorisation is revoked/expired, the automation disables the corresponding wireless profile/policy.

### 4) Ensure mutual authentication and authenticator protection via configuration
- Configure wireless authentication to use protocols that provide:
  - **Authenticator protection** (preventing credential interception/impersonation)
  - **Mutual authentication** (both client and authenticator authenticate)
- Validate by configuration checks (e.g., confirm EAP method selection and certificate validation settings for Wi‑Fi; confirm authenticated pairing and identity binding for Bluetooth; confirm gateway-side authentication for radio links).

### 5) Log wireless connection activity and link it to authorisation
- Ensure the enforcement points and/or NAC produce logs for:
  - Association/authentication attempts and outcomes
  - Identity used (user/device identity where available)
  - SSID/WLAN/profile/policy identifier
  - Timestamp, site/environment scope
- Ensure logs are forwarded to SIEM/log platform.
- Add a correlation key so that each enabled wireless profile/policy has an identifier that matches the authorisation record (e.g., profile ID includes the authorisation ticket ID or a generated policy ID stored in both the enforcement config and the authorisation record).

### 6) Assign named responsibilities and operational ownership
- Define named roles for:
  - Maintaining the Wireless Access Catalogue
  - Approving authorisations for each wireless access type/purpose
  - Managing wireless enforcement configuration and ensuring changes follow configuration management
  - Reviewing wireless access logs for anomalies and compliance

## WHO
- **Wireless/Network Security Engineer**: implements enforcement integration, mutual-auth configuration, and logging/correlation.
- **GRC/Compliance Owner (or Security Governance Lead)**: owns the authorisation workflow and approval policy.
- **Network Operations / OT/Radio Engineering (as applicable)**: maintains enforcement points and validates configuration baselines.
- **SIEM/Monitoring Engineer**: ensures logs are collected, normalized, and retained.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. **Catalogue completeness**: A current inventory exists listing every wireless access type used (802.11, Bluetooth, and any microwave/packet radio connectivity used).
2. **Documented requirements**: For each wireless access type, there is a controlled, versioned configuration/connection requirement document specifying mutual authentication and authenticator protection expectations.
3. **Enforcement**: In the enforcement points, no wireless profile/policy is enabled without a corresponding approved authorisation record (tested by attempting to enable an unauthorised profile and verifying it is blocked/disabled).
4. **Mutual authentication**: For each enabled 802.11/Bluetooth wireless access type, configuration is set to a mutual-auth capable protocol and certificate/credential validation is enabled as specified in the requirement template (validated via configuration audit).
5. **Auditability**: Wireless connection/authentication logs in SIEM include the wireless profile/policy identifier that maps to the authorisation record; sample queries can show “connections for authorisation X” and “connections for revoked/expired authorisation Y = none (or only failures)”.
6. **Change control**: Wireless configuration changes are performed through the authorise-then-enable workflow and are traceable to the authorisation record and change ticket.


### ac-18.1 — Authentication and Encryption (Enhancement)

#### WPA3-Enterprise (802.1X) with user+device auth and strong encryption

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Configure all system wireless access to require **strong authentication for users and devices** and to use **strong encryption** for all wireless traffic.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-18(1)** enhancement requires: “Protect wireless access to the system using authentication of {{ insert: param, ac-18.01_odp }} and encryption.” The organization-defined parameter indicates authentication must cover **users, devices**.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
Implement wireless access enforcement at the Wi-Fi layer using **WPA3-Enterprise** with **802.1X (EAP-TLS or equivalent strong EAP)**, backed by centralized AAA/RADIUS and an enterprise identity provider.

1. **Eliminate weak/unauthenticated wireless modes**
   - Disable any open SSIDs and any “personal”/PSK modes.
   - Disable legacy/weak cipher suites and legacy authentication methods on Wi-Fi controllers/APs.

2. **Require strong user authentication**
   - Enable **WPA3-Enterprise** and configure **802.1X** for user authentication.
   - Integrate 802.1X with **RADIUS/AAA** pointing to the enterprise IdP.
   - Use phishing-resistant MFA for interactive user authentication where applicable (IdP-side).

3. **Require strong device authentication (approved devices)**
   - Use **certificate-based device authentication** (e.g., EAP-TLS) or an equivalent strong device credential mechanism supported by your Wi-Fi/AAA stack.
   - Establish a device certificate issuance/approval workflow that maps device identities to an “approved device” inventory.
   - Configure AAA authorization rules to allow association only when the presented device identity is recognized/approved.

4. **Enforce encryption for all wireless traffic**
   - Ensure the Wi-Fi configuration uses WPA3-Enterprise with strong cipher suites (no fallback to weaker modes).
   - Validate that all SSIDs intended for system access are configured to require 802.1X and negotiated encryption under WPA3-Enterprise.

5. **Bind wireless access to network segmentation (authorization outcome)**
   - Map authenticated sessions to the correct VLAN/VRF/zone based on AAA attributes (e.g., user role and/or device type/compliance).
   - Ensure only the required network paths are reachable from the wireless SSID(s) used to access the application.

6. **Produce audit-ready evidence**
   - Export and retain Wi-Fi controller/AP configuration baselines showing WPA3-Enterprise + 802.1X settings.
   - Retain AAA/RADIUS policy configuration and logs demonstrating authentication attempts and outcomes.
   - Retain documentation describing: (a) how **users** authenticate, (b) how **devices** authenticate, and (c) which encryption/authentication modes are enforced.

7. **Operational validation (testable checks)**
   - Verify that a client without valid user credentials cannot associate.
   - Verify that an unapproved device certificate/identity cannot associate.
   - Verify that wireless traffic is protected using the configured WPA3-Enterprise/802.1X encryption (no open/PSK SSIDs for system access).

## WHO
- **Network Security Engineer / Wi-Fi Administrator**: implement AP/controller configuration, SSID policies, and 802.1X settings.
- **IAM Engineer**: configure IdP integration for user authentication and MFA.
- **PKI/Device Management Owner**: manage device certificate issuance/approval and device identity lifecycle.
- **Security/Compliance Analyst**: verify evidence completeness and maintain audit artifacts.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. All SSIDs that provide access to the system/application require **802.1X** and **WPA3-Enterprise** (no open or PSK-based access for system access).
2. **User authentication** is enforced via centralized **RADIUS/AAA** integrated with the enterprise IdP.
3. **Device authentication** is enforced via strong device credentials (certificate-based or equivalent) and AAA authorization denies non-approved devices.
4. Wireless sessions are mapped to the correct network segmentation (VLAN/VRF/zone) based on AAA attributes.
5. Evidence exists and is retained: exported Wi-Fi/AP configuration, AAA/RADIUS policy configuration, and authentication logs showing successful/failed authentication events.


### ac-18.3 — Disable Wireless Networking (Enhancement)

#### Disable embedded Wi‑Fi/Bluetooth before device deployment (default-off)

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Days
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
For every system/component that contains embedded wireless networking capabilities (e.g., Wi‑Fi and/or Bluetooth), ensure the wireless radios are **disabled by default prior to issuance and deployment**, except where an **approved essential mission/function** exception explicitly permits wireless.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-18(3)** (enhancement) requires: “Disable, when not intended for use, wireless networking capabilities embedded within system components prior to issuance and deployment.” The guidance emphasizes that embedded wireless represents a significant vulnerability and disabling it when not needed reduces susceptibility to wireless-based adversary techniques.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Establish an authoritative wireless capability inventory
- Create/maintain an inventory of device classes and models used for the application environment (e.g., admin workstations, support laptops, field devices, embedded PCs).
- For each model, record which embedded radios exist (Wi‑Fi, Bluetooth) and the supported disablement mechanisms (e.g., BIOS/UEFI radio disable, OS/device management policy, hardware switch).

### 2) Define and document exception criteria (essential mission/function)
- Create an exception policy that states what qualifies as “intended for essential organizational missions or functions.”
- Require documented approval (system owner + security/infosec) and include: scope (which device models/asset tags), allowed radios (Wi‑Fi and/or Bluetooth), start/end dates (expiry), and compensating controls if wireless is enabled.

### 3) Implement “default-off” disablement in the build/provisioning process
- Update the standard build/golden image/provisioning workflow so wireless is disabled at the earliest feasible stage:
  - Prefer **firmware/UEFI** radio disable during provisioning when supported.
  - Otherwise, enforce **OS/device management** configuration that disables Wi‑Fi/Bluetooth radios (not just user-level toggles).
- Ensure the provisioning workflow applies the disablement consistently across **production, test, and DR** environments where devices are issued.

### 4) Enforce and prevent drift after deployment
- Configure centralized endpoint/device management to continuously enforce the “wireless disabled” baseline for non-exception devices.
- Implement an exception override mechanism that is the only supported path to enable wireless, tied to the approved exception record.

### 5) Produce audit-ready evidence that disablement occurred before issuance/deployment
- For each issued device, retain evidence showing:
  - the applied configuration baseline/build version,
  - the wireless disablement state (from device management compliance reports and/or firmware/OS configuration outputs),
  - the deployment/issuance record linking the device asset tag/serial to the baseline.
- Maintain change/deployment logs referencing the baseline and exception approvals.

### 6) Assign named responsibility and operationalize verification
- Assign named roles (e.g., Endpoint Engineering/IT Ops for build enforcement; Security/Compliance for exception approval).
- Perform periodic compliance checks (sampling is acceptable if documented) to confirm wireless remains disabled on non-exception devices.

## WHO
- **Endpoint Engineering / IT Ops:** implement golden build/provisioning changes and device management enforcement.
- **System Owner / Application Owner:** request and justify exceptions for essential mission/function use.
- **Information Security / Compliance:** approve exceptions and review evidence.
- **Asset/Configuration Management:** maintain inventory and baseline mapping.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. For each in-scope device model, there is a documented baseline showing how embedded Wi‑Fi/Bluetooth is disabled (firmware and/or OS/device management).
2. For non-exception devices, device management compliance reports show Wi‑Fi and/or Bluetooth are **disabled** (as applicable) after provisioning and at periodic intervals.
3. For every issued device, there is an auditable linkage between asset tag/serial, the applied build/baseline version, and the deployment/issuance record.
4. Any device with wireless enabled has an active, approved exception record specifying scope, allowed radios, and expiry.
5. Evidence is retained demonstrating disablement was applied **prior to issuance/deployment** (not only after deployment).

### ac-18.4 — Restrict Configurations by Users (Enhancement)

#### RBAC-gated Wi‑Fi independent configurators with audit reconciliation

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement an explicit, named-user authorization mechanism for “independently configuring wireless networking capabilities” (e.g., Wi‑Fi controller/admin configuration actions), and enforce it technically so only those users can perform independent wireless configuration changes. Ensure audit logs identify the user who made each wireless configuration change and can be reconciled to the authorization list.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 AC-18(4) enhancement requires that the organization **identify and explicitly authorize users allowed to independently configure wireless networking capabilities**. Guidance notes that enforcement is achieved, in part, by the **access enforcement mechanisms employed within organizational systems**. This solution makes the authorization list enforceable and auditable.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define the in-scope wireless configuration actions
- Create a short, controlled scope definition for what counts as “independently configure wireless networking capabilities” for your environment (e.g., SSID/VLAN/radio profile changes, WPA2/3 settings, captive portal templates, RADIUS/WPA enterprise parameter changes, controller policy changes).
- Identify any actions that are excluded or require additional approvals (e.g., emergency/break-glass procedures).

### 2) Create and maintain an authoritative authorization register
- Maintain a controlled register (GRC system, IAM admin app, or other controlled repository) containing **named users** permitted to independently configure wireless.
- For each user, store at minimum: full name, unique identity (username/IdP subject), authorization start date, review/expiration date, approver, and the scope tag(s) they are authorized for.
- Establish a periodic review cadence (e.g., aligned to privileged access reviews) and record evidence of review.

### 3) Enforce authorization in the wireless management plane using identity-based RBAC
- Integrate Wi‑Fi controller/admin portals with the enterprise IdP (SSO) already used by the organization.
- Configure RBAC so that only the IdP groups/roles corresponding to the authorization register can access **wireless configuration functions**.
- Ensure non-authorized users cannot access configuration endpoints (deny by default) and are limited to read-only or no access as appropriate.
- Require strong authentication for any admin role (e.g., phishing-resistant MFA where supported by the wireless platform/IdP).

### 4) Ensure auditability of wireless configuration changes
- Configure the wireless management plane to log configuration changes with the **exact identity** used for access (IdP username/subject) and include enough detail to identify what changed.
- Forward these logs to the organization’s centralized logging/SIEM as per existing logging standards.

### 5) Implement periodic reconciliation (authorization list ↔ audit logs)
- Run a scheduled reconciliation report:
  - Extract “changed-by” identities from wireless configuration audit logs over the review period.
  - Compare each identity to the authorization register.
  - Flag any “changed-by” identities not present in the register for investigation and corrective action.
- Retain reconciliation results as audit evidence.

### 6) Update governance artifacts to reference the enforced restriction
- Update the system security plan and wireless configuration/procedure documentation to reference:
  - the authorization register as the source of truth,
  - the defined scope of “independent configuration,” and
  - the enforcement mechanism (IdP-integrated RBAC) and audit reconciliation process.

## WHO
- **Wireless/Network Security Engineer (lead):** defines scope, configures RBAC and logging on Wi‑Fi controllers.
- **IAM Engineer (support):** implements IdP integration, group/role mappings, and MFA requirements.
- **GRC/Compliance or Security Governance (owner of register):** maintains the authorization register, review cadence, and evidence.
- **SOC/Monitoring Analyst (support):** validates log forwarding and assists with reconciliation investigations.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. A controlled authorization register exists listing **named users** authorized for wireless independent configuration, including scope tags and review evidence.
2. The Wi‑Fi management plane enforces RBAC such that only IdP identities mapped to authorized roles can access wireless configuration functions; unauthorized users cannot perform independent configuration changes.
3. Wireless configuration audit logs record the identity (IdP subject/username) that performed each configuration change.
4. A reconciliation report can be produced showing that all identities appearing as “changed-by” in the audit logs during the review period are present in the authorization register (or exceptions are investigated and documented).
5. The system security plan and wireless configuration procedures reference the register, scope definition, enforcement mechanism, and reconciliation process.

### ac-18.5 — Antennas and Transmission Power Levels (Enhancement)

#### RF survey-informed antenna selection and calibrated Wi‑Fi power baselines

- **Category:** Manual
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Days
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
For each enterprise wireless access point (WAP), select antennas (directional/sector/beamforming where applicable) and calibrate transmission power levels so that Wi‑Fi signals are unlikely to be received outside organization-controlled boundaries. Maintain auditable evidence of the antenna/power decisions and the measured basis for them.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 AC-18(5) (Enhancement) requires: **“Select radio antennas and calibrate transmission power levels to reduce the probability that signals from wireless access points can be received outside of organization-controlled boundaries.”** The guidance further supports using **periodic wireless surveys** to understand the RF profile and nearby systems before making mitigating changes.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Establish an authoritative WAP inventory and boundary risk tags
- Create/maintain a WAP inventory including: WAP ID, location (building/floor/coordinates), mounting details, antenna model (current), controller model, radio bands (2.4/5/6 GHz), and “boundary adjacency” classification (e.g., perimeter wall, exterior door, loading bay, parking lot side).
- Tag WAPs that have line-of-sight or proximity to areas outside organization-controlled boundaries as **boundary-adjacent**.

### 2) Perform an initial wireless survey that includes nearby emitters
- Conduct a site RF survey for boundary-adjacent coverage areas.
- Include measurements that capture the local RF environment and other systems operating in the area (e.g., neighboring Wi‑Fi SSIDs, other unlicensed emitters, microwave links if present).
- Record survey outputs (heatmaps/RSSI maps, channel utilization, noise floor, and any observed external detectability indicators).

### 3) Select antennas to reduce out-of-boundary reception
- For each boundary-adjacent WAP, choose antenna type/model and mounting orientation intended to reduce outward lobes toward uncontrolled areas.
- Prefer directional/sector antennas and, where supported by the WAP/controller hardware, beamforming-capable antennas.
- Document the rationale linking the antenna choice to the survey findings and boundary directionality.

### 4) Calibrate transmit power to the minimum viable level
- For each WAP radio, set transmit power to the **minimum level that still meets in-boundary coverage requirements** (coverage targets defined by the organization’s WLAN design standards).
- Use measured results to validate that the signal is unlikely to be received outside controlled boundaries (e.g., external-side measurement points, perimeter test locations, or “beyond boundary” reference points).
- Record the final calibrated power settings (e.g., dBm or controller-specific power levels) per WAP and per band.

### 5) Implement and lock configuration baselines
- Apply the approved antenna and power settings consistently via the WLAN controller configuration (or WAP configuration mechanism).
- Establish a configuration baseline and enforce it through change management so that drift is detectable and changes are traceable.

### 6) Schedule periodic surveys and re-calibration triggers
- Define survey periodicity (e.g., annually or after major environmental changes) and triggers (e.g., construction, new nearby tenants, new microwave links, antenna replacement, controller firmware changes).
- When triggers occur, repeat steps 2–4 and update baselines with approval.

### 7) Produce audit-ready evidence package
Maintain a single evidence set per WAP (or per boundary-adjacent zone) containing:
- Survey report(s) and measurement methodology (including nearby emitters).
- Antenna selection rationale and mapping to WAP location/orientation.
- Calibrated transmit power settings per band/radio.
- Validation results demonstrating reduced likelihood of out-of-boundary reception.
- Change approvals and configuration baseline identifiers.

## WHO
- **Wireless Network Engineer / WLAN Administrator:** performs surveys coordination, antenna/power design, and controller configuration.
- **Information Security / Compliance Lead:** reviews evidence package for AC-18(5) compliance and approves risk acceptance if any boundary leakage remains.
- **Change Management Owner:** ensures approvals, traceability, and controlled deployment.
- **Facilities/OT/Plant Engineering (if applicable):** supports physical access and ensures survey timing aligns with operational constraints.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. For each boundary-adjacent WAP, there is documented evidence of **antenna selection** (model/type and orientation) tied to survey findings.
2. For each boundary-adjacent WAP, there is documented evidence of **calibrated transmit power settings** (per band/radio) with measured basis.
3. Survey evidence includes the **RF profile** and **nearby radio systems** operating in the area.
4. WLAN controller/WAP configurations match the approved baseline (no unapproved deviations) and changes are traceable via change records.
5. Periodic survey schedule and re-calibration triggers are defined and implemented (at least initial survey completed; periodicity documented).


### ac-19 — Access Control for Mobile Devices (Control)

#### MDM-enforced mobile device authorization and compliance access gating

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement organization-controlled mobile device access control using (1) an MDM-managed device enrollment/configuration baseline and (2) identity-provider “conditional access” so that only explicitly authorized, enrolled, and compliant mobile devices can connect to organizational systems (especially when outside controlled areas).

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-19 requires protection and control of mobile devices through behavior/policy-based restrictions and explicit authorization to connect, including enforceable rules for mobile device use outside controlled areas. The guidance further calls for configuration management, device identification/authentication, mandatory protective software, malicious code scanning, security update/patch scanning, (where applicable) primary OS integrity checks, and disabling unnecessary hardware.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Define and publish mobile device access rules (off-site vs controlled areas)
- Create an AC-19 mobile device standard that states required user behaviors when outside controlled areas (e.g., screen lock, encryption, no unmanaged apps, and immediate reporting for loss/theft).
- Define which mobile devices are “organization-controlled” (e.g., company-issued only, or BYOD with explicit opt-in) and the required enrollment/authentication method.

**Deliverable:** AC-19 Mobile Device Standard + user quick-start guide.

### 2) Establish an explicit device authorization list via MDM enrollment
- Configure MDM to require enrollment before any access is allowed.
- Maintain an authoritative inventory of enrolled devices (device ID/serial/UDID/IMEI as available) mapped to user identity.
- Only devices that are successfully enrolled and remain in a managed state are considered “authorized to connect.”

**Deliverable:** MDM device inventory export/reporting for audit.

### 3) Enforce a hardened configuration baseline (configuration management)
Create MDM configuration profiles that enforce, at minimum:
- Strong device lock (passcode/biometrics policy) and automatic lock.
- Full-disk encryption (or equivalent) enabled.
- Disable or restrict unnecessary hardware/features (as supported by platform) such as Bluetooth/Wi‑Fi Direct where not required.
- Restrict installation of non-approved apps and require managed app/container for access to organizational data.

### 4) Require mandatory protective software and scanning
- Deploy the organization’s mobile security agent (or MDM-enforced security app) and require it to be present and enabled.
- Enable malware detection/scanning capabilities where supported by the platform/agent.
- Configure MDM/agent to block access when the protective software is missing, disabled, or out of date.

### 5) Enforce security update/patch posture
- Configure MDM to check OS version and security patch level.
- Enforce minimum security version thresholds and/or require the device to be within an acceptable patch window.
- Require the mobile security agent to be updated.

### 6) Implement device integrity checks where supported
- Use MDM/agent attestation signals (e.g., jailbreak/root detection, secure boot status, OS integrity posture) where available.
- If full OS integrity checks are not supported on a platform, document the limitation and rely on the available attestation signals.

### 7) Gate access using identity-provider conditional access
- Integrate MDM compliance signals (enrolled/compliant status, protective agent enabled, patch level, integrity posture) into the identity provider.
- Configure conditional access policies so that mobile access to organizational apps/resources is allowed only when:
  - The device is enrolled in MDM,
  - The device is marked compliant,
  - Required protective software is present/enabled,
  - Patch/security version requirements are met,
  - (Where supported) integrity/attestation checks pass.
- Apply stricter conditions for “outside controlled areas” (e.g., require VPN and/or stronger authentication, and deny access if compliance signals fail).

### 8) Logging and audit evidence
- Ensure the identity provider logs conditional access outcomes (success/failure) including device compliance state.
- Ensure MDM logs enrollment/compliance changes and security agent status.
- Retain logs per enterprise retention requirements and ensure they can be correlated by user and device.

## WHO
- **Security Architect / IAM Engineer:** conditional access policy design and integration.
- **MDM Administrator:** MDM enrollment, configuration baselines, compliance rules, and reporting.
- **Cybersecurity (Mobile Security):** protective agent deployment, scanning configuration, and integrity/attestation validation.
- **App Owner / Platform Owner:** ensure mobile app access uses managed app/container and approved authentication flows.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. **Authorization enforcement:** A non-enrolled/unmanaged mobile device cannot access the organization’s mobile-enabled apps/resources (conditional access denies).
2. **Compliance gating:** Devices that fail required checks (protective agent missing/disabled, out-of-date patch/security version, or failed integrity/attestation where supported) are denied access.
3. **Configuration baseline:** MDM reports show enforced settings (encryption enabled, screen lock policy, unnecessary hardware restrictions where supported, and app installation restrictions).
4. **Off-site behavior:** When outside controlled areas, access requires the defined stricter conditions (e.g., VPN/stronger auth) and is denied if compliance signals fail.
5. **Audit evidence:** For at least one test user/device, you can produce audit evidence showing (a) device enrollment/inventory record, (b) compliance evaluation, and (c) conditional access success/failure logs.
6. **Operational monitoring:** Alerts/notifications exist for devices that become non-compliant (e.g., protective agent disabled or patch level falls below threshold).

### ac-19.4 — Restrictions for Classified Information (Enhancement)

#### MDM-enforced mobile restrictions for classified facilities

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 72%

## WHAT
Implement an MDM/UEM-enforced control set for **unclassified mobile devices permitted in classified facilities** that:
1) allows only **Authorising Official-approved** devices to be used in those facilities,
2) prevents any connection from those permitted devices to **classified systems**,
3) requires **Authorising Official approval** for any connection from those devices to **unclassified systems**,
4) disables/blocks **wireless/modem capabilities** (e.g., Wi‑Fi, Bluetooth, cellular/mobile data, hotspot/tethering) on those permitted devices while in classified facilities,
5) ensures the SOC/ISSO can produce **audit evidence** to support random inspections and incident response.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-19(4) (Enhancement) requires restrictions for classified information, specifically including: (per organization-defined parameters) defining **security officials** responsible for review/inspection of unclassified mobile devices and stored information, defining **security policies** restricting connection of classified mobile devices to classified systems, and enforcing restrictions that prevent unauthorized exposure of classified information through mobile devices.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Establish the device allowlist and facility gating (Authorising Official permission gate)
- In the MDM/UEM, create an **enrollment/assignment policy** that only allows devices whose identity matches the **Authorising Official-approved list** (e.g., device serial/IMEI and/or device certificate/managed identity).
- Integrate facility entry workflow (badge/QR check-in) with MDM status:
  - If device is not in the allowlist or not currently compliant, place the device in **quarantine** (no access to facility Wi‑Fi/portals; optionally block access to any managed apps).

**Implementation detail:** Use MDM conditional access / compliance states to drive network/app access decisions.

### 2) Enforce “no unclassified-to-classified connectivity”
- Configure MDM to block routing/VPN profiles that could reach classified networks:
  - Deny any VPN/Private Access profiles that target classified IP ranges/FQDNs.
  - Block managed DNS overrides that resolve classified domains.
  - Enforce firewall-like restrictions where supported (per-app VPN only to approved unclassified destinations).
- Add a hard deny in the network access layer (e.g., ZTNA/SASE policy) for sessions originating from these devices to **classified segments**.

### 3) Require Authorising Official approval for unclassified destinations
- Implement **destination allowlisting** at the app/VPN layer:
  - Only permit managed apps to connect to explicitly approved **unclassified** endpoints (FQDN/IP) that are tagged with an Authorising Official approval reference.
  - For any new destination, require an approval workflow that updates the allowlist.

### 4) Disable wireless/modem capabilities on permitted devices in classified facilities
- Using MDM configuration profiles, disable or restrict:
  - Wi‑Fi (disable Wi‑Fi radios or restrict to approved SSIDs only; prefer disabling Wi‑Fi entirely in classified mode if feasible)
  - Bluetooth
  - Cellular/mobile data
  - Hotspot/tethering
  - Any modem/ethernet-over-cell profiles
- Create a **“Classified Facility Mode”** profile that is activated only when the device is detected to be in the facility (e.g., via geofence, facility check-in, or managed location signal).

### 5) Enable SOC/ISSO inspection readiness and incident evidence
- Ensure MDM collects and retains evidence needed for random inspections:
  - Device compliance state history (classified facility mode enabled/disabled)
  - Installed managed apps list
  - Configuration profile status
  - Network/VPN profile usage logs (where available)
  - Storage/backup indicators relevant to stored information review (as supported by platform)
- Stream relevant events to SIEM/SOC tooling for correlation and rapid incident triage.

### 6) Incident trigger integration
- Configure MDM/SIEM alerting for policy violations (e.g., Wi‑Fi enabled, tethering enabled, unauthorized VPN profile, attempts to reach blocked destinations).
- When alerts indicate potential classified data exposure, route to the **SOC incident response team** and follow the organization’s incident response plan.

## WHO
- **Information System Security Officer (ISSO):** Owns the inspection/inspection-evidence requirements and validates that MDM compliance evidence supports random checks.
- **SOC incident response team:** Monitors SIEM alerts, performs triage, and initiates incident response when violations or classified-data indicators occur.
- **MDM/UEM administrator (IT):** Implements and maintains device allowlists, compliance profiles, and destination allowlists.
- **Authorising Official:** Approves which devices and which unclassified destinations are permitted.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1) **Allowlist enforcement:** Only Authorising Official-approved devices can enter/operate in classified facilities (non-allowlisted devices are quarantined or denied access).
2) **Classified connectivity blocked:** From permitted devices, attempts to reach classified IP ranges/FQDNs are blocked (verified by test attempts and logged denials).
3) **Unclassified connectivity approval enforced:** Connections to unclassified endpoints succeed only when the destination is present in the Authorising Official-approved allowlist (verified by adding/removing a test endpoint).
4) **Wireless/modem restrictions enforced:** In “Classified Facility Mode,” Wi‑Fi/Bluetooth/cellular/hotspot are disabled or restricted per MDM configuration (verified by device checks and compliance logs).
5) **Audit evidence available:** MDM/SIEM provides retained logs showing compliance state, profile status, and relevant network/VPN denials sufficient to support random ISSO/SOC inspections.
6) **Incident alerting works:** Policy violations generate SOC alerts and are correlated in SIEM with device identity and timestamps.

## Notes on scope
This item focuses on the **unclassified mobile device restrictions** and the operational evidence needed for ISSO/SOC random inspections and incident handling. Classified mobile device connection restrictions are handled by the organization’s classified mobile device connection policy (AC-19(4) parameter ac-19.04_odp.02) and should be implemented separately where applicable.

### ac-19.5 — Full Device or Container-based Encryption (Enhancement)

#### MDM/MAM-enforced container encryption for DoD-managed mobile devices

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Days
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement **container-based encryption** for **DoD-managed mobile devices and endpoints** by using MDM/MAM capabilities to encrypt **selected data containers** (e.g., managed-app data, protected document stores, or specific file/record/field types) rather than relying only on full-device encryption.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-19(5)** enhancement requires: “Employ **{{ insert: param, ac-19.05_odp.01 }}** to protect the confidentiality and integrity of information on **{{ insert: param, ac-19.05_odp.02 }}**.”
- **ac-19.05_odp.01**: container-based encryption
- **ac-19.05_odp.02**: mobile devices (DoD-managed mobile devices and endpoints)

Container-based encryption must protect **confidentiality and integrity** of the **defined encrypted scope** (files/records/fields or equivalent containerized data structures).

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
1. **Define the encrypted scope (policy-level)**
   - In the MDM/MAM platform, create an encryption policy that explicitly states what is encrypted as “containers” (examples: managed app document storage, email attachments within the managed container, specific app data stores, or other supported protected data structures).
   - Document exclusions (what is *not* container-encrypted) to ensure the scope is unambiguous.

2. **Configure encryption to protect confidentiality and integrity**
   - Enable the platform’s container encryption/data protection feature for the selected managed apps/data containers.
   - Enforce required device/user prerequisites that support integrity/confidentiality of the container (as supported by the platform), such as:
     - device unlock requirements (passcode/biometric) for access to the container
     - prevention of plaintext backup/export where the platform supports it
     - integrity-preserving container access controls (e.g., managed app only, restricted copy/paste or sharing controls if supported)
   - Ensure the configuration is applied to the **DoD-managed** device groups/endpoints.

3. **Deploy via MDM/MAM policy baselines with change control**
   - Store the encryption policy configuration baseline (or exported policy definition) in a controlled repository.
   - Use the organization’s change management process to approve updates.
   - Map each baseline version to the policy name/version deployed to device groups.

4. **Enable and centralize audit evidence**
   - Turn on MDM/MAM audit logging for encryption-related events (e.g., policy assignment, encryption enabled/disabled, policy changes, compliance status changes).
   - Forward logs to the enterprise SIEM/log platform.
   - Retain logs per the organization’s logging retention requirements.

5. **Assign named operational responsibility**
   - Designate roles responsible for:
     - initial encryption policy configuration
     - monitoring device compliance/encryption status
     - approving and implementing encryption policy changes
   - Provide runbook steps for remediation (e.g., reapply policy, investigate non-compliant devices).

6. **Document in system security plan / design records**
   - Update the system security plan and/or security architecture documentation to include:
     - which mobile devices/endpoints are in scope (DoD-managed)
     - which data containers are encrypted
     - the MDM/MAM encryption mechanism and key operational assumptions (at a level consistent with vendor documentation)
     - how audit evidence is generated and reviewed

## WHO
- **MDM/MAM Administrator (or Mobile Security Engineer):** implement and maintain encryption policies and baselines.
- **Security Compliance/Assurance Lead:** ensure documentation and evidence requirements are met for AC-19(5).
- **SOC/SIEM Analyst (or Monitoring Lead):** validate log forwarding and monitor encryption/compliance events.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. For all **DoD-managed mobile devices/endpoints** in scope, the MDM/MAM reports **container encryption enabled** for the defined managed apps/data containers.
2. The encrypted scope is explicitly documented (what “containers” are encrypted) and matches the MDM/MAM policy configuration.
3. The MDM/MAM configuration baseline is stored under change control, with evidence of approved changes.
4. Audit logs exist in the SIEM for encryption-relevant events, including at minimum:
   - encryption policy assignment/enabled status
   - encryption policy changes
   - device compliance/encryption status changes
5. System security plan/design documentation references the container encryption mechanism and the evidence sources used for compliance.


### ac-20 — Use of External Systems (Control)

#### External system trust agreements with enforceable access limits

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement AC-20 requirements for any **external system** used to access or interact with organizational systems by:
1) establishing **written terms and conditions** (trust agreements) with external system owners/administrators, and
2) enforcing those terms through **technical controls** (identity, network/app allow-listing, encryption, MFA, audit logging, vulnerability management) and **explicit prohibitions** for disallowed external system types.

## WHY (control requirement)
AC-20 requires that for external systems, the organization has **terms and conditions** that at a minimum address:
- **specific types of applications** that can be accessed,
- the **highest security category of information** that can be processed/stored/transmitted on the external system (per your parameter set, up to **Secret max**),
- and that the organization ensures external-system usage is aligned to trust relationships.
If terms cannot be established, the organization must impose restrictions on personnel using those external systems.
Your organization-defined parameters further require defined **controls asserted** (e.g., encryption in transit/at rest, MFA, audit logging, vulnerability management, incident notification within 24 hours) and defined **prohibited types** (e.g., personally owned devices, unmanaged contractor-managed systems, public cloud without an approved authorization boundary).

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Create an “External System Trust Pack” (template + annex)
**Manual artifact** maintained by Security/Compliance and used for every external system relationship.
- Include a standard **trust agreement** section that specifies:
  - **Allowed applications** (application allow-list or named integration endpoints).
  - **Data classification ceiling**: explicitly state the maximum information category permitted on the external system as **Secret max** (per `ac-20_odp.02`).
  - **Access rules**: who may access (authorized individuals), authentication requirements, session constraints, and least-privilege expectations.
  - **Logging requirements**: what must be logged on the external system and retention expectations (per `ac-20_odp.03`).
  - **Incident/breach notification**: require notification to the organization **within 24 hours** (per `ac-20_odp.03`).
- Include a **controls asserted** annex requiring the external system owner to assert/attest implementation of:
  - encryption **in transit and at rest**,
  - **MFA** for access,
  - **audit logging**,
  - **vulnerability management**,
  - incident notification within 24 hours (per `ac-20_odp.03`).
- Include a **prohibited external system types** section and require the external system owner to confirm the arrangement does not fall into prohibited categories (per `ac-20_odp.04`).

### 2) Enforce the trust terms with technical controls (policy + configuration)
**Software/configuration** in the enterprise access path to ensure only approved external systems and applications can be used.
- Implement **application allow-list enforcement** at the access layer:
  - For web/API access: restrict to approved apps/endpoints only.
  - For remote access: restrict to approved gateways/jump hosts and approved protocols.
- Enforce **data classification ceiling** operationally:
  - Map each allowed application/integration to an approved data classification level (Secret max) and block/alert if users attempt to export/share data beyond the ceiling (e.g., DLP policy tied to the allowed app list).
- Enforce **identity and MFA** for external access:
  - Require MFA (prefer phishing-resistant where feasible) for all sessions originating from external systems.
- Enforce **encryption**:
  - Require TLS for all external-to-org traffic and ensure storage encryption is enabled where the external system stores org data.
- Enforce **audit logging**:
  - Ensure logs from the access layer and the external integration are forwarded to SIEM (or equivalent) and are tamper-evident.
- Enforce **vulnerability management evidence**:
  - Require vulnerability scan/patch cadence evidence as part of the trust pack acceptance and revalidation.

### 3) Prohibit disallowed external system types
**Software/configuration** plus governance.
- Create explicit deny rules in the access layer for:
  - personally owned devices,
  - unmanaged contractor-managed systems,
  - public cloud services **without** an approved authorization boundary (per `ac-20_odp.04`).
- Operationalize “approved authorization boundary” by requiring an internal approval record (e.g., a boundary ID) that is checked by the access policy.

### 4) If trust terms cannot be established, restrict personnel usage
**Manual + Software**.
- For any external system where written terms cannot be executed:
  - apply stricter access restrictions (e.g., deny access to Secret data; allow only non-sensitive functions; require additional approvals per access request).
  - record the exception decision and compensating restrictions.

### 5) Maintain and revalidate
- Establish a periodic review cadence for trust packs and access policy mappings (e.g., on partner change, system change, or at least annually).
- Require re-attestation of controls asserted and revalidation of prohibited-category status.

## WHO
- **Security/Compliance (Control Owner):** maintains the External System Trust Pack templates; approves trust agreements; manages exceptions when terms cannot be established.
- **IAM/Network Security Engineering:** implements and maintains allow-lists, MFA enforcement, encryption requirements, and deny rules for prohibited external system types.
- **Application Owner / Integration Owner:** maps allowed applications/endpoints to data classification ceilings and ensures integration logging/telemetry is available.
- **SIEM/Monitoring Team:** ensures audit logs are collected, normalized, retained, and monitored for external-access events.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1) For each external system type/relationship in use, there exists a **signed written trust agreement** (trust pack) that includes:
   - allowed applications,
   - **Secret max** data ceiling,
   - access rules,
   - logging requirements,
   - and incident/breach notification **within 24 hours**.
2) The enterprise access path enforces:
   - **application allow-listing** (only approved apps/endpoints reachable),
   - **MFA required** for external-origin sessions,
   - **encryption in transit** enforced (TLS-only),
   - **audit logging** forwarded to SIEM.
3) Access policy includes explicit **deny** controls for prohibited external system types (personally owned devices, unmanaged contractor-managed systems, and public cloud without approved authorization boundary).
4) For any external system lacking a signed trust agreement, documented compensating restrictions exist and are enforced (e.g., reduced data classification access and/or additional approval workflow).
5) Evidence exists that vulnerability management and revalidation are part of the trust pack acceptance (e.g., attestation and/or scan/patch cadence evidence).

### ac-20.1 — Limits on Authorized Use (Enhancement)

#### Assurance-gated external system access with audit-ready evidence

- **Category:** Software
- **Priority:** Critical
- **Effort:** Weeks
- **Confidence:** 78%

## WHAT
Implement an “external system assurance gate” so that authorized users can access or use external systems to access the organization’s system or to process, store, or transmit organization-controlled information **only after** the organization either:
1) **Verifies** the external system contains the required security/privacy controls (via independent assessment/attestation or other means), **or**
2) Maintains an **approved written agreement** covering the external system’s connection/processing.

## WHY (control requirement)
NIST SP 800-53 **AC-20(1) Limits on Authorized Use (Enhancement)** requires that external-system access/processing of organization-controlled information by authorized individuals is permitted **only after** verification of required controls or retention of an approved agreement. The enhancement guidance emphasizes that verification can be achieved by external independent assessments, attestations, or other means depending on required confidence.

## HOW (specific steps/approach)
### 1) Create/maintain an External System Register (ESR)
For each external system/vendor/host that may be used for access or for processing/storing/transmitting organization-controlled information, record:
- External system identifier (name, vendor/host entity)
- Purpose and business use case
- Information types/data classifications involved
- Connection method (user login, API, file transfer, integration, etc.)
- Environments impacted (prod/test/dev as applicable)
- Risk tier / confidence level required (risk-based)
- Chosen assurance path: **Verification** or **Approved Agreement**

### 2) Define the required control checklist per external system category
Translate your organization’s security/privacy policies/plans into a checklist that evidence must address (e.g., identity/authentication expectations, encryption, logging/auditability, data handling/retention, breach notification, subprocessors, privacy controls). This checklist is used to evaluate evidence coverage.

### 3) Implement an assurance workflow that blocks access until approved
Integrate the assurance gate into the access request/approval process:
- When a user requests access to an external system (or when an integration is proposed), the workflow must require an ESR entry and an **active, approved assurance record**.
- If no approved assurance record exists (or it is expired/out of scope), the request is denied until assurance is completed.

### 4) Verification path (evidence-first)
For the **Verification** assurance path, require evidence that explicitly supports the checklist items, such as:
- Independent assessment reports (e.g., SOC 2 / ISO-style reports) covering relevant periods
- Signed attestations from the provider
- Where needed for higher-risk data flows: penetration test summaries or other assurance artifacts

Perform evidence review by a defined reviewer (security/privacy owner) and record:
- Evidence type(s), coverage statement, report period
- Reviewer identity and review date
- Determination of whether required controls are met for the specific use case/data types
- Expiration/validity rules (e.g., based on report period)

### 5) Agreement path (approved agreement retention)
For the **Approved Agreement** assurance path:
- Require a contract/addendum or written agreement that is **approved** by the organization and is **in date**.
- Ensure the agreement scope matches the external system use case (connection/processing, data types, responsibilities, breach notification, subprocessors, etc.).
- Store the approved agreement and link it to the ESR entry and the access request.

### 6) Enforce access using technical controls tied to the assurance record
Use your enterprise identity/access management (e.g., conditional access / ZTNA / IdP policy) so that access is only granted when:
- The user is authorized (AC-20(1) still assumes “authorized individuals”), and
- The external system’s assurance record is marked **approved** and **current**.

### 7) Maintain audit-ready traceability
For each external system and each assurance decision, retain:
- ESR entry
- Assurance method used (verification vs agreement)
- Evidence or agreement artifact references
- Review/approval dates and reviewer
- Scope mapping to the specific data types and activities (access, process, store, transmit)

## WHO (role responsible)
- **Security/Privacy Governance Owner**: defines checklist, confidence/risk criteria, and evidence review standards.
- **External Risk/Third-Party Risk Manager**: maintains ESR and manages agreement approvals.
- **IAM/Platform Engineer**: implements enforcement in access request/IdP/conditional access.
- **System/Application Owner**: initiates access requests and ensures correct data classification/use-case mapping.
- **Evidence Reviewer (Security/Privacy)**: validates evidence coverage and records approval.

## Acceptance criteria (verifiable)
1. For every external system that is used to access the organization’s system or to process/store/transmit organization-controlled information, there exists an ESR entry with an assurance method selected.
2. No access request (user access or integration enablement) is approved unless an assurance record is present and marked **approved** and **current**.
3. For systems using the **Verification** path, retained evidence covers the defined checklist items and includes review date, reviewer, and evidence coverage statement.
